The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Parsing Conditional Syllogisms Under the Second Condition
Author enumerates eight then ten productive moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse a conditional syllogism expressed in variable notation and enumerate the eight (then ten) productive moods based on the second condition.

Core Idea
The second condition yields eight moods; combining with the first condition gives ten.

This passage dives into the mechanics of conditional syllogisms, where premises are universal conditionals. The author uses variable letters to illustrate how the middle term (the entailment) drives the inference. By carefully tracing the logical steps, you will see how eight productive moods arise from the second condition, and how they relate to the first condition to total ten. This parsing exercise will sharpen your ability to handle formal logical notation in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the variable letters
Locate the letters أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, و, ز in the passage. They represent terms or propositions. The minor premise introduces ج and د; the major involves the others. Note how they reappear in conclusions.
The specific assignment of letters can be arbitrary; focus on their roles.
2
Locate the minor and major premises
Find the two premises. For example, the author gives: 'كلما كان ج د فكل أ ب' (minor) and 'كلما كان كل ب هـ فـ و ز' (major). The minor has an antecedent (ج د) and consequent (كل أ ب); the major has a universal condition (كل ب هـ) and a consequent (فـ و ز).
The major is always universal under the second condition.
3
Determine the middle term (الأوسط)
The middle term is the logical entailment (ملازمة) linking the premise of the major to one extreme of the minor. In the example, it is the relation between 'كل أ ب' and 'كل ب هـ'. This relation allows composition.
The middle is not a term but a conditional relationship.
4
Compute the result of composition (نتيجة التأليف)
Combine the minor and major to get an intermediate conclusion. Example: from the minor and major, we derive 'كلما كان ج د فكل أ هـ'. This is called the result of composition.
This intermediate is itself a conditional.
5
Combine to get the final conclusion
The intermediate conclusion is then combined with the major's consequent or other premises to produce the final conclusion, e.g., 'كلما كان كل أ هـ فـ و ز'. The author shows how this continues through the third figure.
The final conclusion may be affirmative or negative, universal or particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه كلَّما كان كل «أب» فكل «ب هـ»؛ فقد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فكل «أهـ»، وكلَّما كان كل (أ ب) فكل ب (هـا) فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «و ز». وإنهما ينتجان من الثالث: أما الصغرى فلأنه حينئذ كلما كان كل «أب» وكل «أهـ» لما مر غير مرة وإنه مع الصغرى ينتج قد يكون إذا كان ج د فكل «أهـ». وأما الكبرى فلإنتاج قولنا كلَّما كان كل «أب» وكل «أه» مع ما ينتج مقدمها مع كبرى القياس وهو قولنا كلما كان كل أب فـ «و ز» تاليها من الثالث أيضا ينتج قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د فكل «أهـ» فقد لا يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «و ز» بالبرهان الذي مرَّ ؛ والأوسط هو الأوسط بعينه. ولو كان الأوسط ملازمة مقدم الكبرى لمقدم الصغرى فقد تم البرهان أيضًا. وبالجملة فالأوسط في هذه الضروب ملازمة مقدم الكلية لإحدى طرفي الأخرى. ثم الصغرى إذا كانت موجبة كلية والكبرى جزئية كما في قولنا كلما كان ج د فكل «أب»؛ وقد يكون إذا كان كل ب هـ فـ و ز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فكل «أه» فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «و ز». وإن كانت الكبرى سالبة جزئية ينتج قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د فكل «أهـ» فقد لا يكون إذا كان كل أهـ فـ و ز. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب من هذا الشكل وغيره. وأما المنتج بحسب الشرط الثاني فثمانية أضرب؛ إذ الكبرى كلية لا محالة فيحصل أربعة من الصغرى الموجبة؛ والمنتج مع نتيجة التأليف أو عكسها بكليته لمقدم الكبرى الكلية هو تالي الصغرى بعينه أو بكليته في هذه الضروب الأربعة. ونتائج هذه الضروب كلية لأن مقدم النتيجة مستلزم لتاليها لما أن مقدم مقدّمها الذي هو بعينه مقدم الصغرى مستلزم لتالي الصغرى استلزامًا كليا إن كانت الصغرى كلية، وجزئيا إن كانت جزئية لإلزامه للمجموع المركَّب من تالي الصغرى. ونتيجة التأليف المنتجين لمقدم الكبرى وهو مع الكبرى الموجبة للقياس ينتج موجبة من الأول، ومع مقدم النتيجة ينتج تالي النتيجة من الثالث. وكذلك مع الكبرى السالبة كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان جد فكل «أب»، وكلما كان بعض ب هـا فـ «و ز» ينتج كلما كان ج د فكل «أهـ» فقد يكون إذا كان كل أهـ فـ و ز. وإن كانت الكبرى سالبة كلية ينتج كلَّما كان «ج د» فكل «أه» فقد لا يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «و ز»، فظهر أن مقدم النتيجة في هذه الضروب موجب كلي وتاليها جزئي وإنه كالكبرى في الكيف وأربعة من الصغرى السالبة مع الكليتين سواء كان المنتج مع نتيجة التأليف تالي الصغرى لمقدم الكبرى أو مقدم الكبرى لتالي الصغرى على الوجه الذي ذكرناه. ونتائجها جزئية كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فبعض «أ» ليس «ب»، وكلَّما كان كل ب هـا فـ و ز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان قد لا يكون إذا كان (ج) د فليس بعض أهـ فكلما كان ليس بعض أهـ فـ «و ز». وكذلك إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة فإنه ينتج هذه النتيجة أيضًا. وإن كان المنتج من المتشاركين بتالي الصغرى كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان ج د فكل «أب»، وكلما كان بعض اب هـا فـ و ز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان قد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فكل «أب» فكلما كان كل «أهـ» فـ و ز. وكذلك إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة لكن يكون تالي النتيجة سالبا كليا ولما كانت الأربعة الأولى من هذه الضروب الثمانية هي بعينها أربعة من الضروب الستة المنتجة بحسب الشرط الأول صارت الضروب المنتجة باعتبار حال المقدمين من هذا القسم عشرة أضرب، ضربان بحسب الشرط الأول الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى مع الجزئيتين، وأربعة بحسب الشرط الثاني وهي الحاصلة من الصغرى السالبة مع الكليتين، وأربعة بحسب الشرط الأول والثاني وهي الحاصلة من الصغرى الموجبة مع الكليتين.
Because whenever every A is B, then every B is C; then it may be that if J is D, then every A is C, and whenever every A is B, then every B is C? then it may be that if every A is C, then W is Z. And they both produce from the third: as for the minor, because then whenever every A is B and every A is C, as mentioned multiple times, and it with the minor produces: it may be that if J is D, then every A is C. As for the major, it produces from our saying: whenever every A is B and every A is C, with what its premise produces with the major of the syllogism, namely our saying: whenever every A is B, then W is Z, its consequent from the third also produces: it may be that if J is D, then every A is C, and it may not be that if every A is C, then W is Z, by the proof that passed; and the middle is exactly the middle. And if the middle were the necessary connection (luzum) of the premise of the major to the premise of the minor, then the proof would also be complete. In summary, the middle in these moods is the necessary connection of the premise of the universal to one of the two extremes of the other. Then the minor if it is affirmative universal and the major is particular, as in our saying: whenever J is D, then every A is B; and it may be that if every B is C, then W is Z, produces: it may be that if J is D, then every A is C, and it may be that if every A is C, then W is Z. And if the major is negative particular, it produces: it may be that if J is D, then every A is C, and it may not be that if every A is C, then W is Z. And similarly for all moods of this figure and others. As for the productive according to the second condition, there are eight moods; since the major is necessarily universal, four are obtained from the affirmative minor; and the product with the result of composition or its converse entirely for the premise of the universal major is exactly the consequent of the minor or entirely in these four moods. The results of these moods are universal because the premise of the result entails its consequent, since the premise of its premise, which is exactly the premise of the minor, entails the consequent of the minor with universal entailment if the minor is universal, and with particular entailment if it is particular, because it forces the composite from the consequent of the minor. And the result of the composition of the two productive ones for the premise of the major, which with the affirmative major of the syllogism produces an affirmative from the first, and with the premise of the result produces the consequent of the result from the third. And similarly with the negative major, as in our saying: it may be that if J is D, then every A is B, and whenever some B is C? then W is Z, produces: whenever J is D, then every A is C, and it may be that if every A is C, then W is Z. And if the major is universal negative, it produces: whenever J is D, then every A is C, and it may not be that if every A is C, then W is Z. So it appears that the premise of the result in these moods is universal affirmative and its consequent is particular, and it is like the major in quality, and four from the negative minor with the two universals, whether the product with the result of composition is the consequent of the minor for the premise of the major or the premise of the major for the consequent of the minor in the manner we mentioned. And their results are particular, as in our saying: it may not be that if J is D, then some A is not B, and whenever every B is C? then W is Z, produces: it may be that if it may not be that if J is D, then not some A is C, then whenever not some A is C, then W is Z. And similarly if the major is negative, it also produces this result. And if the product is from the two shared by the consequent of the minor, as in our saying: it may not be that if J is D, then every A is B, and whenever some A is B? then W is Z, produces: it may be that if it may not be that if J is D, then every A is B, then whenever every A is C, then W is Z. And similarly if the major is negative, but the consequent of the result is universal negative. And since the first four of these eight moods are exactly four of the six moods productive according to the first condition, the productive moods considering the state of the premises from this section become ten moods: two according to the first condition: the affirmative universal minor with the major with the two particulars, and four according to the second condition, which are obtained from the negative minor with the two universals, and four according to the first and second conditions, which are obtained from the affirmative minor with the two universals.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition of the productive moods of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). It builds on earlier discussions about the first condition (al-shart al-awwal) and introduces the second condition (al-shart al-thani). The author uses variable letters (أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, و, ز) to represent terms and propositions. The passage explains how, from premises of specific forms, conclusions follow according to the interaction of the middle term (al-awsat) which here is the logical relation (luzum) between the antecedent and consequent. It first details how certain combinations produce results from the third figure (al-thalith), then systematically enumerates eight moods based on the second condition, and finally reconciles them with the first condition to yield ten total productive moods. The explanation proceeds by example: for instance, from 'whenever J is D, then every A is B' (minor) and 'whenever every B is C, then W is Z' (major), one can derive 'whenever J is D, then every A is C' and then possibly 'if every A is C, then W is Z'. The author also considers variations in quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the premises, showing how the entailment strength and type of conclusion change. The conclusion summarizes that the productive moods combine to ten: two from the first condition (affirmative universal minor with major and the two particulars), four from the second condition (negative minor with two universals), and four from both conditions (affirmative minor with two universals). This passage is dense and requires careful parsing of the variable notation to track the logical forms.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the minor premise 'If the Quran is created (J D), then every attribute (A) is temporal (B)' and the major premise 'Whenever every attribute is temporal (A B), then the Quran is changeable (W Z),' use the method from this lesson to derive the intermediate and final conclusions. State whether the mood is productive under the second condition.
?
From the passage, list the eight moods of the second condition and explain how they combine with the first condition to yield ten.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
The Ten Productive Moods in Conditional Syllogisms
Enumerates ten productive moods in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the ten productive moods in the third division of conditional syllogisms and explain how the two conditions generate them.

Core Idea
Ten moods, no more: the author's precise enumeration defines valid conditional reasoning.

This passage is the culmination of the discussion on conditional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī). The author, after setting up the framework, now counts exactly ten productive moods. This enumeration is crucial for any scholar who wishes to evaluate arguments that use 'if...then' structure, such as many proofs in kalām and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two conditions
The author distinguishes between two conditions (شرطان) that determine when a mood is productive. The first condition involves a particular premise, and the second involves universal premises.
2
Identify the role of the major premise
The major premise (الكبرى) can be affirmative or negative, and this affects the quality of the conclusion. The author gives examples with variables.
3
Count the moods from the first condition alone
From the first condition, only two moods are productive: when the minor is affirmative universal and the major is with the two particulars.
4
Count the moods from the second condition alone
From the second condition, four moods are derived from the negative minor with the two universal premises.
5
Count the moods from the combination of both conditions
Four more moods come from the affirmative minor with the two universal premises, totaling ten. The author states that any additional moods are invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكبرى الموجبة للقياس ينتج موجبة من الأول، ومع مقدم النتيجة ينتج تالي النتيجة من الثالث. وكذلك مع الكبرى السالبة كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان جد فكل «أب»، وكلما كان بعض ب هـا فـ «وز» ينتج كلما كان جد» فكل «أهـ فقد يكون إذا كان كل أهـ» فـ و ز » . وإن كانت الكبرى سالبة كلية ينتج كلَّما كان «ج د» فكل «أه» فقد لا يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «و ز»، فظهر أن مقدم النتيجة في هذه الضروب موجب كلي وتاليها جزئي وإنه كالكبرى في الكيف وأربعة من الصغرى السالبة مع الكليتين سواء كان المنتج مع نتيجة التأليف تالي الصغرى لمقدم الكبرى أو مقدم الكبرى لتالي الصغرى على الوجه الذي ذكرناه. ونتائجها جزئية كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فبعض «أ» ليس «ب»، وكلَّما كان كل ب هـا فـ او زه ينتج قد يكون إذا كان قد لا يكون إذا كان (ج) د فليس بعض أهـ) فكلما كان ليس بعض أهـه فـ «اوز». وكذلك إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة فإنه ينتج هذه النتيجة أيضًا. وإن كان المنتج من المتشاركين بتالي الصغرى كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان ج د فكل «أب»، وكلما كان بعض اب هـا فـ او ز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان قد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فكل «أب» فكلما كان كل «أهـ» فـ و ز ». وكذلك إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة لكن يكون تالي النتيجة سالباً كلياً ولما كانت الأربعة الأولى من هذه الضروب الثمانية هي بعينها أربعة من الضروب الستة المنتجة بحسب الشرط الأول صارت الضروب المنتجة باعتبار حال المقدمين من هذا القسم عشرة أضرب، ضربان بحسب الشرط الأول الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى مع الجزئيتين، وأربعة بحسب الشرط الثاني وهي الحاصلة من الصغرى السالبة مع الكليتين، وأربعة بحسب الشرط الأول والثاني وهي الحاصلة من الصغرى الموجبة مع الكليتين.
وأما الزيادة على هذه الضروب فساقطة لا اعتبار لها وإن كانت منتجة عند البعض. وعدد الضروب في كل واحد من هذه الضروب العشرة باعتبار حال المتشاركين من جملة ما لا يخفى من الطالب بعد الاطلاع على ما مر .
The major affirmative of the syllogism produces an affirmative from the first, and with the antecedent of the conclusion it produces the consequent of the conclusion from the third. And likewise with the negative major as in our saying: It may be that if Jad, then every Alif-Ba, and whenever some Ba-Ha, then Waw-Zay, it produces: whenever Jad, then every Alif-Ha; it may be that if every Alif-Ha, then Waw-Zay. And if the major is universal negative, it produces: whenever Jad, then every Alif-Ha; it may not be that if every Alif-Ha, then Waw-Zay. So it appears that the antecedent of the conclusion in these moods is universal affirmative and its consequent is particular, and it is like the major in quality, and four from the negative minor with the two universals, whether the product with the compositional conclusion is the consequent of the minor for the antecedent of the major or the antecedent of the major for the consequent of the minor in the manner we mentioned. And their conclusions are particular, as in our saying: It may not be that if Jad, then some Alif is not Ba, and whenever every Ba-Ha, then Alif-Waw-Zay, it produces: it may be that if it may not be that if (J) D, then not some Alif-Ha, then whenever not some Alif-Ha, then Alif-Waw-Zay. And likewise if the major is negative, it produces this conclusion also. And if the product is from the two sharers by the consequent of the minor, as in our saying: It may not be that if Jad, then every Alif-Ba, and whenever some Alif-Ba-Ha, then Alif-Waw-Zay, it produces: it may be that if it may not be that if Jad, then every Alif-Ba, then whenever every Alif-Ha, then Waw-Zay. And likewise if the major is negative, but the consequent of the conclusion is universal negative. And since the first four of these eight moods are exactly four of the six productive moods according to the first condition, the productive moods considering the state of the two premises from this section become ten moods: two according to the first condition: the universal affirmative minor with the major with the two particulars, and four according to the second condition: those derived from the negative minor with the two universals, and four according to the first and second conditions: those derived from the affirmative minor with the two universals.
As for the increase over these moods, it is invalid and not considered, even if it is productive according to some. And the number of moods in each of these ten moods considering the state of the two sharers is among what is not hidden from the student upon reviewing what has passed.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of the productive moods in the third division of conditional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī). The author first describes the basic patterns: when the major is affirmative, it produces an affirmative conclusion from the first figure; when combined with the antecedent of the conclusion, it produces the consequent from the third figure. Similarly for negative majors, with examples using variable letters (Jad, Alif, Ba, etc.). He explains that the antecedent of the conclusion is universal affirmative and the consequent is particular, and the major determines the quality. Then he counts four moods from the negative minor with universal premises, and four from the affirmative minor, totaling ten moods. Two additional moods come from the first condition alone. Finally, he states that any increase beyond these ten is invalid, even if some consider them productive. The number of moods in each of the ten depends on the situation of the two shared terms (al-mushtarikān). This passage is a technical specification of valid moods in conditional syllogisms, important for distinguishing valid from invalid argument forms.

الكبرى
الضرب

المتشاركين

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The third division of conditional syllogisms has exactly ten valid moods, derived from two conditions regarding the premises; any additional moods are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the following argument: 'If the food is pork, then it is haram. Whenever it is haram, it is impermissible to eat. Therefore, if it is pork, it is impermissible to eat.' Does this match one of the ten productive moods? Which condition(s) apply?
?
Task 2: Try to construct a conditional syllogism that is invalid according to the author's list, and explain why it fails.

Fourth Division of Incomplete Syllogism: Definition and Conditions
Defines the fourth division and states its two conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth division of the incomplete syllogism and state its two conditions.

Core Idea
The middle term connects the antecedent of the minor to the consequent of the major.

The fourth division is a specific type of incomplete syllogism where the middle term is shared between the antecedent of the minor premise and the consequent of the major premise. This lesson defines that structure and explains the two conditions that make it productive.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of the fourth division
Find the exact sentence that defines the fourth division. It begins with 'والقسم الرابع من هذا القسم: هو ما يكون الأوسط مشتركًا بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى'.
The definition specifies the location of the middle term.
2
Identify the two sharikat
Determine which two premises share the middle term. They are the minor and major premises, but specifically the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major.
This differs from other divisions where the middle term is shared between whole premises.
3
Extract the two conditions
The author states that there are two conditions. The first requires a productive composition with the major affirmative and at least one premise universal. The second involves producing the result of the composition or its converse with one premise.
Both conditions are necessary for the syllogism to be productive.
4
Understand the result of composition
Recognize that 'نتيجة التأليف' is an intermediate conclusion that becomes the antecedent of the final conclusion's antecedent and the consequent of its consequent.
This intermediate conclusion is used in the second condition.
5
Compare with previous divisions
Contrast this division with earlier ones: here the middle term is not shared between whole premises but between parts of them.
This helps avoid confusion with other divisions.

Source Text

وأما الزيادة على هذه الضروب فساقطة لا اعتبار لها وإن كانت منتجة عند البعض. وعدد الضروب في كل واحد من هذه الضروب العشرة باعتبار حال المتشاركين من جملة ما لا يخفى من الطالب بعد الاطلاع على ما مر. والقسم الرابع من هذا القسم [أي من القسم الثاني وهو غير التام]: هو ما يكون الأوسط مشتركًا بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى، والشرط فيه أيضًا على قسمين: أحدهما اشتمال المشاركين على تأليف منتج مع إيجاب الكبرى وكلية إحديهما. والثاني إنتاج نتيجة التأليف أو عكسها بكليته مع تالي الكبرى بعينه، أو بكليته لمقدم الصغرى الكلية أو إنتاج مقدم الصغرى الكلية مع نتيجة التأليف لتالي الكبرى السالبة؛ ونتيجة التأليف في هذا القسم مقدم لمقدم النتيجة وتالي تاليها والضروب المنتجة بحسب الشرط الأول ستة: الكبرى الموجبة الكلية مع الصغريات الأربع، والكبرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكليتين. وعدد الضروب المنتجة في كل شكل في كل ضرب من هذه الضروب الستة عدد الضروب المخصوصة بذلك الشكل؛ ونتائجها متصلات جزئية من الثالث والأوسط فيه ملازمة مقدم الكلية لأحد طرفي الأخرى كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د» وكلما كان «و ز» فكل «ب ه» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «ج د» فقد يكون إذا كان «و ز» فكل «أه» لأنه كلما كان كل «أب» فـ «و ز»؛ فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «أب» فـ «و ز» فقد يكون إذا كان «و ز» فكل «أه»، وإنهما ينتجان من الثالث؛ أما الصغرى فلأنه كلما كان كل «أب» فكل «أه»، وكلما كان كذلك فكلما كان كل «أب» فكل «أه»، وإنه مع الصغرى ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «ج د». وأما الكبرى فلأن قولنا كلما كان كل «أب» فكل «أه» ينتج مقدمها تاليها من الثالث. ويمكن أن يتبين بغير هذا الطريق أيضًا: وذلك يعرف بالتأمل فيما مر. وإن كانت الصغرى سالبة فإنه ينتج النتيجة المذكورة لكن يكون مقدمها سالبًا جزئيًا. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب في كل ضرب من الستة. وأما الضروب المنتجة بحسب الشرط الثاني فثمانية: أربعة من الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الأربع، وأربعة من الصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الأربع. فالتي تكون كبراها موجبة من هذه الثمانية بحسب أن يكون فيه الطرف المنتج من الكبرى وهو الثاني منها بعينه، أو بكليته مع نتيجة التأليف أو عكسها بكليته منتجًا لمقدم الصغرى؛ ونتائجها كلية سواء كانت الكبرى كلية، أو جزئية كما يقال على هيئة الشكل الثاني كلما كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان «و ز» فكل «هب» ينتج كلما كان ليس البتة إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «ج د» فقد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فكل «أه» لأنه يصدق حينئذ ليس البتة إذا كان كل «أه» فكل «أب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فكل «أب»؛ وإنه ينتج مع الصغرى قد يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «ج د»، وقد كان ليس البتة إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «ج د»، هذا خلف. وإذا صدقت هي صدق قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان كل «أه» فكل «هب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وذلك يفضي إلى الخلف أيضًا. وإن كانت الصغرى سالبة كلية والكبرى بحالها والقياس على هيئة الشكل الأول كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من «أب» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان «و ز» فلا شيء من «ب ه» ينتج كلما كان كل «أه» فـ «ج د» فقد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فكل «أه» لأنه حينئذ يصدق ليس البتة إذا كان كل «أه» فلا شيء من «أب» فيصدق إذن ليس البتة إذا كان كل «أه» فلا شيء من «ب ه» وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وذلك يفضي إلى الخلف.
As for the increase over these moods, it is invalid and of no account, even if it is productive according to some. The number of moods in each of these ten moods with respect to the state of the sharikat is among what is not hidden from the student after examining what has passed. The fourth division of this division [i.e., of the second division, which is incomplete] is that in which the middle term is shared between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major. The condition for it is also of two types: one of them is that the two sharikat contain a productive composition with the affirmation of the major and the universality of one of them. The second is the production of the result of the composition or its converse in its entirety with the consequent of the major itself, or in its entirety for the universal antecedent of the minor, or the production of the universal antecedent of the minor with the result of the composition for the negative consequent of the major. The result of the composition in this division is the antecedent of the antecedent of the conclusion and the consequent of its consequent. The productive moods according to the first condition are six: the universal affirmative major with the four minors, and the particular affirmative major with the two universal ones. The number of productive moods in each figure in each of these six moods is the number of moods specific to that figure. Their results are particular conditionals from the third [figure], and the middle term in it is a consequent of the universal of one of the two ends of the other, as in our saying: 'It may be that whenever every A is B then C is D, and whenever W is Z then every B is H' produces 'It may be that whenever every A is H then C is D, so it may be that whenever W is Z then every A is H' because whenever every A is B then W is Z; so it may be that whenever every A is H then C is D, and whenever every A is B then W is Z, so it may be that whenever W is Z then every A is H, and they both are produced from the third [figure]; as for the minor, because whenever every A is B then every A is H, and whenever it is so then whenever every A is B then every A is H, and it with the minor produces 'It may be that whenever every A is H then C is D'. As for the major, because our saying 'Whenever every A is B then every A is H' produces its antecedent from its consequent from the third [figure]. It can also be demonstrated by another way: that is known by reflection on what has passed. If the minor is negative, then it produces the mentioned result, but its antecedent is particular negative. And so on for the rest of the moods in each of the six moods. As for the productive moods according to the second condition, they are eight: four from the universal affirmative minor with the four [forms], and four from the universal negative minor with the four [forms]. Those whose major is affirmative among these eight are according to the productive term being from the major, which is the second of it itself, or in its entirety with the result of the composition or its converse in its entirety producing the antecedent of the minor. Their results are universal, whether the major is universal or particular, as is said in the form of the second figure: 'Whenever every A is B then C is D, and it may be that whenever W is Z then every H is B' produces 'Whenever it is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then C is D, then it may be that whenever C is D then every A is H' because then it is true that 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then every A is B', otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely our saying: 'It may be that whenever every A is H then every A is B'; and it produces with the minor 'It may be that whenever every A is H then C is D', and it was 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then C is D'; this is a contradiction. And if it is true, then our saying 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then every H is B' is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true; that also leads to a contradiction. If the minor is universal negative and the major is as it is, and the syllogism is in the form of the first figure, as in our saying: 'It is absolutely not the case that if no A is B then C is D, and it may be that whenever W is Z then no B is H' produces 'Whenever every A is H then C is D, so it may be that whenever C is D then every A is H' because then it is true that 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then no A is B', so then it is true that 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then no B is H', otherwise its contradictory would be true; that leads to a contradiction.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the fourth division of the incomplete syllogism in mixed conditional and categorical logic. The author begins by stating that any additional moods beyond the ten already discussed are invalid, even if some consider them productive—this is a firm rejection, establishing the sufficiency of the enumerated moods. He then defines the fourth division: it occurs when the middle term (al-awsat) is shared between the antecedent of the minor premise (muqaddam al-sughra) and the consequent of the major premise (tali al-kubra). The condition for this division is twofold. The first condition requires that the two sharikat (the premises that share the middle term) together form a productive composition, with the major being affirmative and at least one of the two premises universal. Under this condition, the productive moods are six: the major is universal affirmative with all four types of minor (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), and the major is particular affirmative with the two universal minors. The author notes that the number of moods in each figure corresponds to the known moods of that figure. The results are particular conditional propositions from the third figure, where the middle term is a consequent of the universal of one of the two ends of the other premise. He illustrates with a complex example using variables: 'It may be that whenever every A is B then C is D, and whenever W is Z then every B is H' produces several conclusions, demonstrating the third-figure derivation. He then addresses the case where the minor is negative, stating that the result is the same but its antecedent becomes particular negative. Next, he turns to the second condition, which yields eight productive moods: four from a universal affirmative minor and four from a universal negative minor. When the major is affirmative, the productive term from the major (the second term of the major) produces the antecedent of the minor, either directly or via the result of the composition or its converse. The results are universal. He provides examples using the second figure and the first figure, each time using a reduction to contradiction (khulf) to show the validity. In the first example, he shows that assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction. In the second, with a negative minor in the first figure, he again employs khulf. The passage is technical and assumes familiarity with the prior classification and figures of syllogisms. The key takeaway is the precise enumeration of moods under well-defined conditions.

Checkpoint
In the fourth division of the incomplete syllogism, where is the middle term shared?
A) Between the two antecedents
B) Between the two consequents
C) Between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major
D) Between the whole premises

Key Takeaway
The fourth division is defined by the middle term linking the antecedent of the minor to the consequent of the major.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an argument from a kalam text that mixes a conditional premise with a categorical premise. Identify the middle term and state which division of incomplete syllogism it belongs to.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Moods Under the First Condition of the Fourth Division
Enumerates the six moods under the first condition and illustrates with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six productive moods under the first condition and trace the derivation of a mixed conditional-categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Six moods arise when the major is affirmative and at least one premise is universal.

Under the first condition of the fourth division, the author lists six productive moods. These moods produce particular conditional conclusions. Using variables, he illustrates how the middle term relates the antecedent of one premise to the consequent of the other, deriving intermediate conclusions through the third figure.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the six moods
List the six combinations: universal affirmative major with each of the four minors (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), and particular affirmative major with the two universal minors.
The minor can be any of four types, but for the particular affirmative major, only universal minors are allowed.
2
Identify the figure
The author states that the results are particular conditionals from the third figure. Understand how the middle term connects in the third figure.
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises.
3
Analyze the example with variables
Trace the argument: 'قد يكون إذا كان كل أ ب فـ ج د، وكلما كان و ز فكل ب ه' produces 'قد يكون إذا كان كل أ ه فـ ج د' and other conclusions. Identify the intermediate steps.
The example uses the third figure to derive the conclusion.
4
Verify the derivation
Check how the intermediate conclusions are produced. The author shows that from 'كلما كان كل أ ب فـ ج د' and 'كلما كان و ز فكل ب ه' one gets 'كلما كان كل أ ب فـ و ز' and then the final conditional.
This involves a categorical syllogism within the conditional framework.
5
Note the negative minor case
If the minor is negative, the result is the same but the antecedent becomes particular negative.
This is an exception to the general form.

As for the increase over these moods, it is invalid and of no account, even if it is productive according to some. The number of moods in each of these ten moods with respect to the state of the sharikat is among what is not hidden from the student after examining what has passed. The fourth division of this division [i.e., of the second division, which is incomplete] is that in which the middle term is shared between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major. The condition for it is also of two types: one of them is that the two sharikat contain a productive composition with the affirmation of the major and the universality of one of them. The second is the production of the result of the composition or its converse in its entirety with the consequent of the major itself, or in its entirety for the universal antecedent of the minor, or the production of the universal antecedent of the minor with the result of the composition for the negative consequent of the major. The result of the composition in this division is the antecedent of the antecedent of the conclusion and the consequent of its consequent. The productive moods according to the first condition are six: the universal affirmative major with the four minors, and the particular affirmative major with the two universal ones. The number of productive moods in each figure in each of these six moods is the number of moods specific to that figure. Their results are particular conditionals from the third [figure], and the middle term in it is a consequent of the universal of one of the two ends of the other, as in our saying: 'It may be that whenever every A is B then C is D, and whenever W is Z then every B is H' produces 'It may be that whenever every A is H then C is D, so it may be that whenever W is Z then every A is H' because whenever every A is B then W is Z; so it may be that whenever every A is H then C is D, and whenever every A is B then W is Z, so it may be that whenever W is Z then every A is H, and they both are produced from the third [figure]; as for the minor, because whenever every A is B then every A is H, and whenever it is so then whenever every A is B then every A is H, and it with the minor produces 'It may be that whenever every A is H then C is D'. As for the major, because our saying 'Whenever every A is B then every A is H' produces its antecedent from its consequent from the third [figure]. It can also be demonstrated by another way: that is known by reflection on what has passed. If the minor is negative, then it produces the mentioned result, but its antecedent is particular negative. And so on for the rest of the moods in each of the six moods. As for the productive moods according to the second condition, they are eight: four from the universal affirmative minor with the four [forms], and four from the universal negative minor with the four [forms]. Those whose major is affirmative among these eight are according to the productive term being from the major, which is the second of it itself, or in its entirety with the result of the composition or its converse in its entirety producing the antecedent of the minor. Their results are universal, whether the major is universal or particular, as is said in the form of the second figure: 'Whenever every A is B then C is D, and it may be that whenever W is Z then every H is B' produces 'Whenever it is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then C is D, then it may be that whenever C is D then every A is H' because then it is true that 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then every A is B', otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely our saying: 'It may be that whenever every A is H then every A is B'; and it produces with the minor 'It may be that whenever every A is H then C is D', and it was 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then C is D'; this is a contradiction. And if it is true, then our saying 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then every H is B' is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true; that also leads to a contradiction. If the minor is universal negative and the major is as it is, and the syllogism is in the form of the first figure, as in our saying: 'It is absolutely not the case that if no A is B then C is D, and it may be that whenever W is Z then no B is H' produces 'Whenever every A is H then C is D, so it may be that whenever C is D then every A is H' because then it is true that 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then no A is B', so then it is true that 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then no B is H', otherwise its contradictory would be true; that leads to a contradiction.

When the major is affirmative, the productive term from the major (the second term of the major) produces the antecedent of the minor, either directly or via the result of the composition or its converse. The results are universal. He provides examples using the second figure and the first figure, each time using a reduction to contradiction (khulf) to show the validity. In the first example, he shows that assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction. In the second, with a negative minor in the first figure, he again employs khulf. The passage is technical and assumes familiarity with the prior classification and figures of syllogisms. The key takeaway is the precise enumeration of moods under well-defined conditions.

Moods Under the Second Condition and Proof by Contradiction
Enumerates the eight moods under the second condition and demonstrates validity using khulf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the eight moods under the second condition and explain how khulf demonstrates their validity.

Core Idea
Eight moods arise from universal minors, producing universal conclusions, validated by assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction.

The second condition of the fourth division introduces eight more productive moods, derived from universal minors (affirmative and negative). The results are universal, not particular. The author proves their validity using reductio ad absurdum (khulf), showing that assuming the contradictory leads to an absurdity. Examples using the second and first figures illustrate the method.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the eight moods
List the eight combinations: four from universal affirmative minor with the four forms (major affirmative/negative, universal/particular), and four from universal negative minor with the four forms.
The minor must be universal; the major can be of any form.
2
Identify the productive term
When the major is affirmative, the productive term is the second part of the major (the consequent or antecedent depending on the figure).
This term, combined with the result of the composition or its converse, produces the antecedent of the minor.
3
Understand the universal results
Unlike the first condition, the results here are universal, regardless of the quantity of the major.
This is a key difference between the two conditions.
4
Analyze the khulf demonstration
In the second figure example, the author assumes the opposite of the desired conclusion and shows that it leads to a contradiction with the premises.
This indirect proof validates the mood.
5
Apply the reduction to first figure
The second example with a negative minor reduces to a first-figure syllogism, also using khulf.
This shows the versatility of the method.

As for the increase over these moods, it is invalid and of no account, even if it is productive according to some. The number of moods in each of these ten moods with respect to the state of the sharikat is among what is not hidden from the student after examining what has passed. The fourth division of this division [i.e., of the second division, which is incomplete] is that in which the middle term is shared between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major. The condition for it is also of two types: one of them is that the two sharikat contain a productive composition with the affirmation of the major and the universality of one of them. The second is the production of the result of the composition or its converse in its entirety with the consequent of the major itself, or in its entirety for the universal antecedent of the minor, or the production of the universal antecedent of the minor with the result of the composition for the negative consequent of the major. The result of the composition in this division is the antecedent of the antecedent of the conclusion and the consequent of its consequent. The productive moods according to the first condition are six: the universal affirmative major with the four minors, and the particular affirmative major with the two universal ones. The number of productive moods in each figure in each of these six moods is the number of moods specific to that figure. Their results are particular conditionals from the third [figure], and the middle term in it is a consequent of the universal of one of the two ends of the other, as in our saying: 'It may be that whenever every A is B then C is D, and whenever W is Z then every B is H' produces 'It may be that whenever every A is H then C is D, so it may be that whenever W is Z then every A is H' because whenever every A is B then W is Z; so it may be that whenever every A is H then C is D, and whenever every A is B then W is Z, so it may be that whenever W is Z then every A is H, and they both are produced from the third [figure]; as for the minor, because whenever every A is B then every A is H, and whenever it is so then whenever every A is B then every A is H, and it with the minor produces 'It may be that whenever every A is H then C is D'. As for the major, because our saying 'Whenever every A is B then every A is H' produces its antecedent from its consequent from the third [figure]. It can also be demonstrated by another way: that is known by reflection on what has passed. If the minor is negative, then it produces the mentioned result, but its antecedent is particular negative. And so on for the rest of the moods in each of the six moods. As for the productive moods according to the second condition, they are eight: four from the universal affirmative minor with the four [forms], and four from the universal negative minor with the four [forms]. Those whose major is affirmative among these eight are according to the productive term being from the major, which is the second of it itself, or in its entirety with the result of the composition or its converse in its entirety producing the antecedent of the minor. Their results are universal, whether the major is universal or particular, as is said in the form of the second figure: 'Whenever every A is B then C is D, and it may be that whenever W is Z then every H is B' produces 'Whenever it is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then C is D, then it may be that whenever C is D then every A is H' because then it is true that 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then every A is B', otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely our saying: 'It may be that whenever every A is H then every A is B'; and it produces with the minor 'It may be that whenever every A is H then C is D', and it was 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then C is D'; this is a contradiction. And if it is true, then our saying 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then every H is B' is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true; that also leads to a contradiction. If the minor is universal negative and the major is as it is, and the syllogism is in the form of the first figure, as in our saying: 'It is absolutely not the case that if no A is B then C is D, and it may be that whenever W is Z then no B is H' produces 'Whenever every A is H then C is D, so it may be that whenever C is D then every A is H' because then it is true that 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then no A is B', so then it is true that 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A is H then no B is H', otherwise its contradictory would be true; that leads to a contradiction.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the fourth division of the incomplete syllogism in mixed conditional and categorical logic. The author begins by stating that any additional moods beyond the ten already discussed are invalid, even if some consider them productive—this is a firm rejection, establishing the sufficiency of the enumerated moods. He then defines the fourth division: it occurs when the middle term (al-awsat) is shared between the antecedent of the minor premise (muqaddam al-sughra) and the consequent of the major premise (tali al-kubra). The condition for this division is twofold. The first condition requires that the two sharikat (the premises that share the middle term) together form a productive composition, with the major being affirmative and at least one of the two premises universal. Under this condition, the productive moods are six: the major is universal affirmative with all four types of minor (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), and the major is particular affirmative with the two universal minors. The author notes that the number of moods in each figure corresponds to the known moods of that figure. The results are particular conditional propositions from the third figure, where the middle term is a consequent of the universal of one of the two ends of the other premise. He illustrates with a complex example using variables: 'It may be that whenever every A is B then C is D, and whenever W is Z then every B is H' produces several conclusions, demonstrating the third-figure derivation. He then addresses the case where the minor is negative, stating that the result is the same but its antecedent becomes particular negative. Next, he turns to the second condition, which yields eight productive moods: four from a universal affirmative minor and four from a universal negative minor. When the major is affirmative, the productive term from the major (the second term of the major) produces the antecedent of the minor, either directly or via the result of the composition or its converse. The results are universal. He provides examples using the second figure and the first figure, each time using a reduction to contradiction (khulf) to show the validity. In the first example, he shows that assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction. In the second, with a negative minor in the first figure, he again employs khulf. The passage is technical and assumes familiarity with the prior classification and figures of syllogisms. The key takeaway is the precise enumeration of moods under well-defined conditions.

The Syllogism

Eight Productive Moods of Conditional Syllogisms Under the Second Condition
Author enumerates the eight productive moods of conditional syllogisms based on the second condition with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight productive moods of conditional syllogisms under the second condition and determine the total number of distinct productive moods for conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
The second condition yields eight productive moods, but only ten are truly distinct when combined with the first condition.

In the previous lesson, we learned about the first condition for productive conditional syllogisms. Now the author introduces a second condition that expands the number of productive moods. This passage systematically lists all eight moods, explains how they work with examples using symbolic variables, and finally refines the count to ten by accounting for overlaps with the first condition. This is a technical but essential part of mastering conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic structure
Note that the second condition considers the major premise's term taken precisely or universally. The minor premise is either universal affirmative or universal negative. This yields two groups of four moods each.
The second condition is about the 'productive term' in the major, not about the minor's type.
2
List the eight moods
From the universal affirmative minor, there are four moods with the four possible majors. Similarly, from the universal negative minor, there are four moods. This makes eight.
The four majors are based on how the productive term is taken and the quality/quantity.
3
Examine the productive term
The 'productive term' from the major (the second term of the major) can be taken either precisely (bi-ʿaynihi) or with its universality (bi-kulliyyatihi). This, along with the result of composition, determines the conclusion.
This choice affects whether the conclusion is universal or particular.
4
Understand the conclusion type
When the major is affirmative, the conclusion is universal. When the major is negative, the conclusion is a partial (juzʾiyya) conditional. Also note the quality of the consequent.
The author explains that the consequent of the desired conclusion from six moods is negative, and the antecedent differs in quality from the minor.
5
Reduce to ten total moods
The first four of the eight moods are also productive under the first condition. The last four are exclusive to the second condition. Adding the two moods exclusive to the first condition gives ten total productive moods.
This enumeration is important for a complete understanding of conditional syllogisms.

وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب. ولما كانت الضروب الأربعة الأولى من هذه الثمانية هي بعينها أربعة من الستة المنتجة بحسب الشرط الأول كان اعتبار الضروب المنتجة باعتبار المقدمتين عشرة: ضربان بحسب الشرط الأول وهما الموجبة الكلية مع الجزئيتين، وأربعة بحسب الشرط الثاني وهي الأربعة الأخيرة من الثمانية، وأربعة بحسبهما جميعًا وهي الأربعة الأولى من الثمانية.
As for the productive moods according to the second condition, they are eight: four from the universal affirmative minor with the four, and four from the universal negative minor with the four. Those whose major is affirmative among these eight are such that the productive term from the major, which is the second of it, either precisely, or with its universality together with the result of composition or its converse in its universality, produces the minor premise; and their results are universal whether the major is universal or particular, as is said in the form of the second figure: whenever every A is B, then C is D; and it may be that when it is W and Z, then every H is B; it produces: whenever it is not at all that every A is H, then C is D; then it may not be that when J is D, then every A is H; because then it is true: it is not at all that when every A is H, then every A is B; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying: it may be that when every A is H, then every A is B; and it produces with the minor: it may be that when every A is H, then C is D; and it was not at all that when every A is H, then C is D? This is a reductio. And when it is true, then our saying is true: it is not at all that when every A is H, then every H is B; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to a reductio as well. And if the minor is universal negative and the major as it is, and the syllogism is in the form of the first figure, as in our saying: it is not at all that when no A is B, then C is D; and it may be that when it is W and Z, then no B is H; it produces: whenever every A is H, then C is D? Then it may not be that when J is D, then every A is H; because then it would be true: it is not at all that when J is C, then every A is H, then no A is B; thus it is true then: it is not at all that when every A is H, then no B is H; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to a reductio. This negative together with the major produces the consequent of the desired conclusion; so it appears that the consequent of the desired conclusion from these six moods is negative, and the antecedent differs from the minor in quality. Then when the major is universal, the consequent is universal negative; and so on for the other moods in every figure. As for the four with negative major as mentioned, their results are partial conditionals whose antecedent agrees with the minor in quality, and whose consequent agrees with the major; whether the antecedent of the minor together with the result of composition produces the consequent of the major, or the consequent together with it produces the antecedent of the minor, or not; so it is said: it is not at all that when every A is B, then C is D; and it may not be that when it is W and Z, then some B is H; it produces: it may be that when it is not at all that when every A is H, then C is D, then it may not be that when it is W and Z, then every A is H. And if the minor is affirmative, it produces: it may be that when whenever every A is H, then C is D, then it may not be that when it is W and Z, then every A is H. The proof for all is from the third as known. As for what is produced from the shared terms as consequent of the major, that is: we say: whenever no A is B, then C is D; and it is not at all that when it is W and Z, then no B is H; it produces: it may be that when whenever every A is H, then C is D, then it is not at all that when it is W and Z, then every A is H. And so on for the other moods. Since the first four of these eight are exactly four of the six productive according to the first condition, the count of productive moods considering the two premises becomes ten: two according to the first condition, namely the universal affirmative with the two particulars; four according to the second condition, namely the last four of the eight; and four according to both conditions, namely the first four of the eight.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage enumerates and explains the eight productive moods of conditional syllogisms under the second condition. The author categorizes them based on the quality and quantity of the minor premise: four with universal affirmative minor and four with universal negative minor. Within each group, the productive term in the major premise can be taken precisely or with its universality, and the resulting conclusion may be universal or particular. The author provides detailed examples using symbolic variables A, B, C, D, H, W, Z, illustrating the logical relationships and reductio proofs (khalf) to verify the validity. The conclusion's quality depends on the major premise. Finally, the author summarizes that considering both the first and second conditions, there are ten productive moods in total for conditional syllogisms, merging the four common moods that satisfy both conditions.

The Ten Productive Moods in Conditional Syllogisms
Author enumerates the ten productive moods in conditional syllogisms and their properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the ten productive moods of conditional syllogisms and identify the conditions under which each mood yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Only ten specific combinations of conditional premises produce valid conclusions.

This passage from a classical logic text enumerates the productive moods in conditional syllogisms. Understanding these moods is essential for evaluating complex arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh, where conditional reasoning is frequent. We will break down the classification and its underlying conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for productivity
The author first discusses moods based on two conditions: the first condition involves a universal affirmative major with two particulars; the second condition involves the last four of eight moods. Recognize that these conditions are not mutually exclusive.
The conditions determine which conclusions follow.
2
Distinguish between the six and eight mood groupings
Note that the first four of the eight moods are also among the six productive moods from the first condition. This overlap leads to a total of ten productive moods: two from the first condition alone, four from the second alone, and four from both.
The ten are not all distinct; they are counted by considering the conditions separately.
3
Apply the rules to a concrete Islamic example
Take a conditional syllogism: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it requires a cause. If it requires a cause, then it has a creator.' This is a chain of conditionals. Determine if it fits any of the ten moods by checking the quality and quantity of premises.
Use the rules from the passage: universal/particular, affirmative/negative.
4
Verify productivity using the author's method
The author proves productivity via the third figure. For your example, check if the composition of premises matches one of the described patterns (e.g., universal negative major with shared consequent).
This step requires careful mapping of variables; the passage uses Arabic letters.

With the major premise, the consequent of the desired conclusion is produced; so it appears that the consequent of the desired conclusion from these six moods is negative, and the antecedent disagrees with the minor premise in quality. Then if the major premise is universal, the consequent is universally negative, and so on in the rest of the moods in every figure. As for the four negative major moods as mentioned, their conclusions are particular conditionals, their antecedent agreeing with the minor premise in quality, and their consequent agreeing with the major premise, whether the antecedent of the minor premise with the result of the composition produces the consequent of the major, or the consequent with it for the antecedent of the minor, or not. As for what is produced from the two sharing the consequent of the major, that is to say: 'Whenever no A is B then J D, and not at all if W Z then no B H' produces 'it may be if whenever every A H then J D, then not at all if W Z then every A H'. And the antecedent of the universal conclusion in the third section is always affirmative, and the consequent is like the major. Among the properties of the third and fourth sections is the production of the universal conclusion, and among the properties of the fourth is the production of the universal affirmative conclusion; that is in the first four of this section and their counterparts from the third section, considering that the consequent from an affirmative with the result of the composition produces the antecedent of a universal premise. As for the number of productive moods, that is by considering the placement of the two extremes in the syllogism. If it were otherwise, it would be possible that some moods other than the productive ones mentioned could be productive. Similarly if the major is universal negative but the consequent of the conclusion is negative. Likewise in the fourth section, it is said: 'Whenever every A B then J D, and not at all if W Z then every B H' produces 'it may be if every A H then J D, then it may not be if every A H then W Z'. Similarly if the minor is universal negative but the antecedent of the conclusion is negative. The proof for all is from the third as mentioned.

Commentary
This passage is an advanced classification of productive moods in conditional syllogisms (qiyas sharti). The author begins by stating that when the major premise is combined with the minor, the consequent of the desired conclusion is produced, and from six specific moods the consequent is negative while the antecedent opposes the minor in quality. If the major is universal, the consequent becomes universally negative. He then focuses on four moods where the major is universally negative: their conclusions are particular conditionals, and he describes the possible relationships between the components. Using symbolic variables (A, B, J, D, etc.), he illustrates cases where the composition yields 'it may be' or 'it may not be'. He emphasizes that the proof for all these comes from the third figure. Next, he discusses the case where the two premises share the consequent of the major, again providing a rule with variables. He then summarizes that the first four of the eight moods are a subset of six moods from the first condition, leading to a total of ten productive moods: two under the first condition (universal affirmative with two particulars), four under the second condition (the last four of eight), and four under both (the first four of eight). He explains how to derive the number of moods by noting the symmetry between sections: the minor in this section behaves like the major in the third section, and vice versa. He then states properties: in the third section, the antecedent of the universal conclusion is always affirmative; in the fourth, the consequent is always negative. Both sections can produce universal conclusions, and the fourth specifically produces universal affirmative conclusions. He revisits the count of productive moods based on the arrangement of extremes, noting that if it were otherwise, other moods could be productive. He explains that when both premises contain a productive composition, the result has one universal negative premise and a shared consequent, giving examples for the second, third, and fourth sections. The passage concludes by stating that the proof for all these is from the third figure, as previously established.

Properties of Third and Fourth Sections in Conditional Syllogisms
Properties of third and fourth sections of conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third and fourth sections of conditional syllogisms and state their key properties regarding universal conclusions.

Core Idea
Only specific sections of conditional syllogisms yield universal conclusions.

Conditional syllogisms have multiple sections or figures, each with distinct properties. The third and fourth sections are special because they can produce universal conclusions. Understanding these properties helps you evaluate the strength of arguments in Islamic sciences that rely on conditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of conditional syllogisms
A conditional syllogism involves at least one conditional premise (e.g., 'If A then B'). The third and fourth sections are defined by the arrangement of terms in the antecedents and consequents of the premises.
2
Identify the key property of the fourth section
The passage states: 'the consequent of the conclusion in the fourth section is always negative, and its antecedent differs from the minor in quality.' This means if the minor is affirmative, the antecedent is negative, etc.
3
Note the universal conclusion property
Both the third and fourth sections produce universal conclusions. The fourth section specifically produces universal affirmative conclusions in its first four moods and their analogues in the third section.
4
Understand the condition for productivity
The number of productive moods depends on the arrangement of terms. The passage explains that if both premises contain a productive composition, they produce a result where one premise is a universal negative with a shared consequent.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider a conditional syllogism in usul al-fiqh: 'If the act is commanded, it is obligatory. If it is not commanded, it is not obligatory.' This is a conditional syllogism. Determine whether it belongs to a section that yields a universal conclusion.

Regarding quality, the consequent of the conclusion in the fourth section is always negative, and its antecedent differs from the minor in quality. Among the properties of the third and fourth sections is producing a universal conclusion, and among the properties of the fourth is producing a universal affirmative conclusion; that is in the first four of this section and their analogues from the third section, considering that the consequent of an affirmative with the result of the composition produces an antecedent for a universal premise. As for the number of productive moods, that is by considering the position of the two terms in the syllogism, and if that were the case, it would be possible that from these moods a product could be other than the productive moods that have been mentioned. That is because when the two premises contain a productive composition that produces something whose one premise is a universal negative and its consequent is shared: as for the second section, the productive syllogism from two universal negatives has passed; as for the third, it is said not at all if (C)(D) then every (A)(B), and whenever every (B)(H) then (W)(Z) produce 'it may be' if not whenever every (A)(H) then (J)(D), then 'it may be' if every (A)(H) then (W)(Z). Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the properties of the third and fourth sections (or figures) of conditional (muttasila) syllogisms. The author begins by noting that in the fourth section, the consequent of the conclusion is always negative, and its antecedent differs from the minor premise in quality (i.e., if the minor is affirmative, the antecedent is negative, etc.). A key property of both the third and fourth sections is that they produce universal conclusions, and the fourth section specifically produces universal affirmative conclusions. This occurs in the first four moods of this section and their analogues in the third section, under the condition that the consequent of an affirmative premise, together with the result of the composition (i.e., the hypothetical combination), yields an antecedent for a universal premise. Next, the author addresses the number of productive moods. He clarifies that the count depends on the arrangement of the two terms in the syllogism, and if that were the only factor, then all moods other than those already listed might be productive. The reason is that if the two premises contain a productive composition, they produce a result where one of the premises is a universal negative with a shared consequent. For the second section, the productive mood from two universal negatives has already been mentioned. For the third section, the author provides examples: a complex conditional statement involving the variables (A, B, C, D, H, W, Z) to illustrate the possible products. Similarly, if the major premise is universal negative, the consequent of the conclusion will be negative. An analogous rule applies to the fourth section, with variables given. The passage concludes by stating that the proof for all these claims is drawn from the third section, as previously explained. The commentary uses Islamic science examples: for instance, in a conditional syllogism, if the major premise is 'If Zayd is not a prophet (salba kulliyya), then he is not infallible (salba)' and the minor is 'If Zayd is a prophet, then he is infallible', the conclusion may be negative. This illustrates the rules about negative conclusions.

Converting Negative Conditionals to Affirmative: Ibn Sina's Method
Explains Ibn Sina's method of deriving conclusions from negative conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a negative conditional proposition into an equivalent affirmative one and determine whether it yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
A negative conditional becomes productive when converted to an affirmative with contradictory consequent.

Ibn Sina shows that negative conditionals, often thought unproductive, can be transformed into productive affirmative syllogisms. This lesson walks through the conversion process and its application in Islamic logic, using examples from kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative conditional structure
Recognize a proposition of the form 'If A then not B' (al-muttasila al-saliba). For example, in kalam: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it is not eternal.'
The conditional is negative because the consequent is negated.
2
Determine the contradictory of the consequent
Find the opposite of the negative consequent. For 'not eternal', the contradictory is 'eternal'? But the passage says 'the opposite of what must be from its affirmative'. Actually, the affirmative conditional would have a positive consequent. So if the negative has 'not B', the affirmative's consequent is B (the contradictory of not B). In our example, 'eternal' is the contradictory of 'not eternal'? Better: if the negative is 'If world is temporally originated, then it is not eternal', then the affirmative is 'If world is temporally originated, then it is created' (since 'created' is the opposite of 'eternal' in this context).
The exact opposite depends on the terms; the key is that the consequent becomes the contradictory of the original consequent.
3
Convert to the equivalent affirmative conditional
Apply the rule: two conditionals differing in quality (affirmative vs negative) but agreeing in quantity (both universal or both particular), and with contradictory consequents, are equivalent and converse. So the negative 'If A then not B' is equivalent to the affirmative 'If A then B' (with B being the contradictory of not B). In our example: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it is created.'
This equivalence is the core of Ibn Sina's method.
4
Use the converted conditional in a syllogism
Now the affirmative conditional can serve as a premise in a productive syllogism. For instance, combine with 'The world is temporally originated' to conclude 'Therefore, it is created.' This yields a valid conclusion, whereas the original negative conditional might not have been directly productive.
The conversion makes the syllogism 'essentially productive'.
5
Verify the validity and note the increase in productive moods
Check that the conclusion follows necessarily. Ibn Sina notes that this method greatly increases the number of productive moods beyond the originally listed ones in each of the four sections.
This shows the power of transformation in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الشيخ يستنتج من المتّصلة السالبة كلية كانت أو جزئية إذا كان تاليها نقيض ما يجب أن يكون من موجبها لارتدادها إلى الموجبة الموافقة المقدم المتناقضة التالي التي هي منتجة بالذات. وذلك لأن الموجبة إذا كان تاليها ما يجب أن يكون في الموجبة كان القياس منتجا نتجية جزئية لارتداد كل واحدة من هاتين القضيتين إلى غيرها بناءً على أن كل متصلتين مختلفتين في الكيف متفقتين في الكم. والمقدم متناقضتين في التالي فهما متلازمتان متعاكستان، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون المنتج على رأيه زائدا على الضروب المنتجة المذكورة في كل قسم من الأقسام الأربعة بكثير.
Then the Sheikh deduces from the negative conditional, whether universal or particular, when its consequent is the opposite of what must be from its affirmative, because it reverts to the affirmative that agrees in antecedent and is contradictory in consequent, which is productive essentially. That is because if the consequent of the affirmative is what must be in the affirmative, then the syllogism produces a particular conclusion, because each of these two propositions reverts to another, based on the fact that any two conditionals differing in quality and agreeing in quantity, and whose antecedents are contradictory in consequent, are two equivalent and converse propositions. Then, according to his view, the produced conclusion increases greatly over the productive moods mentioned in each of the four sections.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage explains the method of the Sheikh (Ibn Sina) for deriving conclusions from negative conditional propositions (al-muttasila al-saliba). The key idea is that a negative conditional ('If A then not B') can be converted into an affirmative conditional ('If A then not-B's opposite') because its consequent is the negation of what the affirmative would have. This conversion is based on the logical equivalence: two conditionals that differ in quality but agree in quantity, and whose antecedents are contradictory in consequent, are equivalent and converse (متلازمتان متعاكستان). For example, in Islamic theology: 'If the world is temporally originated (A), then it is not eternal (not B)' is equivalent to 'If the world is temporally originated (A), then it is created (B's opposite)'. Once converted to an affirmative, the syllogism becomes productive essentially, yielding a particular conclusion. This process greatly increases the number of productive moods beyond those originally listed in the four sections. The author uses terms like 'reverts' (ارتداد) and 'equivalent and converse' to describe the transformation.

Key Terms
المتّصلة السالبة
الموجبة
الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Reduce it to a categorical proposition
B) Convert it to an affirmative conditional with contradictory consequent
C) Increase its quantity
D) Change its antecedent

Key Takeaway
Negative conditionals become productive by converting them into equivalent affirmative conditionals, based on the rule of equivalence between conditionals differing in quality with contradictory consequents.
