The Six Combinations of Conditional Syllogism Sections
Enumerates the six combinations of the four sections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six possible combinations of the four sections in conditional syllogisms and describe the sharing pattern for each combination.

Core Idea
Six combinations, each with a unique pattern of shared parts between minor and major premises.

In conditional syllogisms, the four sections (figures) can combine in various ways. The author presents all six possibilities, each defined by which parts of the minor premise share with which parts of the major premise. Understanding these combinations is essential for analyzing complex reasoning chains, such as those found in usul al-fiqh debates about conditional obligations.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four sections
The four sections (first, second, third, fourth) refer to the arrangement of antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in the minor and major premises. Identify that there are exactly six pairings because we choose 2 out of 4 without repetition and order matters (first-second, first-third, etc.).
The passage says 'it combines some with another in six divisions'.
2
Work through the first three combinations
First with second: the antecedent of the minor shares with both the antecedent and consequent of the major. First with third: each part of the minor shares with the antecedent of the major. First with fourth: each part of the minor shares with each part of the major.
Focus on the Arabic terms: 'مقدم الصغرى' (antecedent of minor) and 'تالي الكبرى' (consequent of major).
3
Work through the last three combinations
Second with third: the consequent of the minor shares with each part of the major. Second with fourth: each part of the minor shares with the consequent of the major. Third with fourth: the consequent of the minor shares with the antecedent of the major, and the antecedent of the minor shares with the consequent of the major.
These patterns are symmetric in a sense; notice how the sharing spreads from one part to multiple.
4
Visualize each combination
Draw a table or diagram for each combination showing which parts of the minor (antecedent A, consequent B) and major (antecedent C, consequent D) share. For example, combination 1: A shares with C and D; combination 2: A and B each share with C.
This helps internalize the pattern without memorizing each one verbatim.
5
Connect to a real example
Take a fiqh example: 'If an act is wajib (minor antecedent), then it is rewarded (minor consequent). If an act is rewarded (major antecedent), then it is pleasing to Allah (major consequent).' The major antecedent (rewarded) matches the minor consequent, which corresponds to combination 1? Actually, check: minor antecedent (wajib) shares with major? Not exactly. Better: use the pattern: combination 1: minor antecedent shares with major antecedent and consequent. Example: 'If it is wajib, then it is rewarded; if it is wajib, then it is commanded.' Here minor antecedent (wajib) shares with both parts of major (wajib and commanded).
Ensure the example matches the sharing pattern precisely.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأقسام الأربعة التي مرَّ ذكرها فإنه يتركب بعضها مع بعض؛ وذلك على ستة أقسام لأنه إما أن يتركب الأول مع الثاني بأن يشارك مقدم الصغرى مقدم الكبرى وتاليها ، أو مع الثالث بأن يشارك كل واحد من جزأي الصغرى مقدم الكبرى، أو مع الرابع بأن يشارك كل واحد من جزأي الصغرى كل واحد من جزأي الكبرى. أو يتركب الثاني مع الثالث بأن يشارك تالي الصغرى كل واحد من جزئي الكبرى أو مع الرابع بأن يشارك كل واحد من جزأي الصغرى تالي الكبرى أو يتركب الثالث مع الرابع بأن يشارك تالي الصغرى مقدم الكبرى ومقدمها تاليها؛ وكل واحد من هذه الأقسام الستة ينتج باعتبار كل واحد من التأليفين على بساطة النتيجة التي عرفتها في ذلك القسم. وباعتبار التركيب أعني مجموع التأليفين نتيجة هي متّصلة مركبة من نتيجة التأليفين؛ والبرهان من الثالث. والأوسط فيه مجموع مقدمي الصغرى والكبرى؛ وإنه من جملة ما يعرف من بعد على التفصيل إن شاء الله. وأما إنتاج أحد المتشاركين إما بعينه أو بكليته مع نتيجة التأليف بينهما، أو عكسها بكليته لمقدم مقدمة كلية كما مرَّ تشترك فيه الأقسام دون الثاني منها. وإنتاج نتيجة التأليف مع تالي أحد المقدمين المتوافقين في الكيف تالي الأخرى يختص بالقسم الثاني. وإنتاج نتيجة التأليف مع أحد طرفي كلية تالي سالبة تشترك فيه الثلاثة الأخيرة؛ والنتيجة متّصلة مقدمها متّصلة من الطرف الغير المشارك من الصغرى ونتيجة التأليف، وتاليهما متصلة من الطرف الغير
Then the four sections that have been mentioned combine with one another; that is in six divisions, because either the first combines with the second, such that the antecedent of the minor shares with the antecedent and consequent of the major; or with the third, such that each of the two parts of the minor shares with the antecedent of the major; or with the fourth, such that each of the two parts of the minor shares with each of the two parts of the major. Or the second combines with the third, such that the consequent of the minor shares with each of the two parts of the major; or with the fourth, such that each of the two parts of the minor shares with the consequent of the major; or the third combines with the fourth, such that the consequent of the minor shares with the antecedent of the major and its antecedent shares with its consequent. Each of these six divisions produces, considering each of the two compositions, a simple conclusion as you know in that section. And considering the composition, i.e., the sum of the two compositions, the conclusion is a compound conditional consisting of the result of the two compositions; and the proof is from the third. The middle term in it is the sum of the antecedents of the minor and major; and that is among what will be known later in detail, God willing. As for the production of one of the two participants, either itself or its whole, together with the result of the composition between them, or its whole converse for a premise of a universal premise as has passed, the sections share in it except the second. And the production of the result of the composition with the consequent of one of the two premises that agree in quality as the consequent of the other is specific to the second section. And the production of the result of the composition with one of the two parts of a universal negative consequent is shared by the last three; and the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is a conditional from the non-participating part of the minor and the result of the composition, and both of their consequents are a conditional from the non-participating part
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage discusses how the four sections (or figures) of conditional syllogisms can be combined with each other, resulting in six distinct combinations. The four sections are named: first, second, third, fourth. The author enumerates the six combinations: (1) first with second: the antecedent of the minor shares with both the antecedent and consequent of the major; (2) first with third: each part of the minor shares with the antecedent of the major; (3) first with fourth: each part of the minor shares with each part of the major; (4) second with third: the consequent of the minor shares with each part of the major; (5) second with fourth: each part of the minor shares with the consequent of the major; (6) third with fourth: the consequent of the minor shares with the antecedent of the major, and its antecedent shares with its consequent. For each combination, a conclusion can be drawn in two ways: considering each composition separately yields a simple conclusion (like in that section), or considering the compound composition yields a compound conditional conclusion (a conditional whose parts are themselves conditionals). The proof for these composite conclusions is derived from the third section. The middle term in such composite syllogisms is the sum of the antecedents of the minor and major premises. The author then discusses further properties: all sections except the second share the production of one of the participants (either itself or its whole) together with the result of the composition. The second section is unique in producing the result of the composition with the consequent of one premise that agrees in quality with the consequent of the other. The last three sections share the production of the result of the composition with one of the two parts of a universal negative consequent. The resulting conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is itself a conditional composed of the non-participating part of the minor and the result of the composition, and both consequents are conditionals from the non-participating part (the text cuts off). An Islamic example: consider a conditional syllogism in fiqh: 'If an act is wajib, then it is rewarded; if it is rewarded, then it is not sinful.' Combining sections yields compound conclusions like 'If an act is wajib, then it is not sinful.' This illustrates the complexity of combining conditional syllogisms.

Simple vs Compound Conclusions in Combined Syllogisms
Describes how simple and compound conclusions are derived from the six combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple and compound conclusions in combined conditional syllogisms and apply the production rules to derive compound conclusions.

Core Idea
Each combination yields a simple conclusion from each composition, and a compound conclusion from the sum of compositions.

Having learned the six combinations, we now explore what conclusions they produce. The author explains that each composition within a combination gives a simple conditional conclusion, but the two compositions together can be combined into a compound conditional conclusion. This compound conclusion is itself a conditional whose antecedent and consequent are conditionals. This is a powerful tool for expressing complex nested reasoning, such as in multi-premise arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate between 'each composition' and 'sum of compositions'
The phrase 'considering each of the two compositions' means looking at the shared parts in isolation. 'Considering the sum of the two compositions' means combining the two resulting conditionals into one meta-conditional.
Think of it as two separate syllogisms vs. one combined syllogism.
2
Identify the simple conclusion from one composition
For any combination, the first composition (e.g., using the first shared pair) yields a simple conditional conclusion. For example, if the minor antecedent shares with the major antecedent, the conclusion might be 'If A then C' from that pair.
The passage says 'simple conclusion as you know in that section'.
3
Construct the compound conclusion from two compositions
The compound conclusion is itself a conditional: its antecedent is a conditional from the first composition, and its consequent is a conditional from the second composition. So it takes the form: 'If (A implies B) then (C implies D)' where A,B,C,D are the non-participating parts.
The passage: 'the conclusion is a compound conditional consisting of the result of the two compositions'.
4
Apply to a specific combination
Take combination 1: minor antecedent (let's call it P) shares with major antecedent (Q) and major consequent (R). The first composition (P with Q) yields 'If P then Q'. The second composition (P with R) yields 'If P then R'. The compound conclusion is 'If (if P then Q) then (if P then R)'.
This is a meta-conditional that can be used in further reasoning.
5
Understand the middle term in compound syllogisms
The 'middle term' for compound conclusions is the sum of the antecedents of the minor and major (majmu' muqaddamay al-sughra wa al-kubra). This is the shared element that links the two compositions.
The author says 'the proof is from the third' – likely meaning the third section provides the template for such compound syllogisms.

Six Combinations of Conditional Syllogism Divisions
Enumerates six combinations of conditional syllogism divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six possible combinations of the four divisions of conditional syllogisms and identify which parts of the premises are shared in each combination.

Core Idea
The four divisions combine in six distinct patterns of shared terms.

This passage builds on the four basic divisions of conditional syllogisms (presumably based on quality and quantity of antecedent and consequent) and shows how they can be combined. There are exactly six possible combinations, each defined by which parts of the minor and major premises share the same term. Understanding these combinations is essential for constructing compound conditional syllogisms and deriving valid conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Four Divisions
The passage refers to 'the four divisions' (الأقسام الأربعة) of conditional syllogisms, which are based on the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the antecedent and consequent. Briefly identify these divisions as a foundation.
If unfamiliar, review the four basic types of conditional premises.
2
Understand 'Sharing' (مشاركة)
Two premises share a part if that part (antecedent or consequent) is the same term in both. The minor premise has an antecedent and a consequent; the major premise also has these. Sharing means the same term appears in both premises.
3
Enumerate the Six Combinations
The author lists six combinations: (1) first with second: minor antecedent shares major antecedent and consequent; (2) first with third: each part of minor shares major antecedent; (3) first with fourth: each part of minor shares each part of major; (4) second with third: minor consequent shares each part of major; (5) second with fourth: each part of minor shares major consequent; (6) third with fourth: minor consequent shares major antecedent and minor antecedent shares major consequent.
The 'first', 'second', etc., refer to the four divisions in a specific order assumed from earlier content.
4
Identify Shared and Free Parts
For each combination, note which parts of the minor and major are shared (identical terms) and which are free (different terms). The free parts determine the form of the conclusion.
For example, in combination (1), the minor antecedent is shared with both parts of the major, so the minor antecedent is the middle term.
5
Recognize Simple vs. Compound Conclusions
Each combination can yield a simple conclusion (using one of the two possible ways of combining) or a compound conclusion (using both ways together). When compound, the conclusion is itself a conditional whose antecedent and consequent are the results of the two combinations, and the middle term is the sum of the antecedents of the minor and major.
The proof for the compound conclusion is from the third figure.

وتاليها، أو مع الثالث بأن يشارك كل واحد من جزأي الصغرى مقدم الكبرى، أو مع الرابع بأن يشارك كل واحد من جزأي الصغرى كل واحد من جزأي الكبرى. أو يتركب الثاني مع الثالث بأن يشارك تالي الصغرى كل واحد من جزئي الكبرى، أو مع الرابع بأن يشارك كل واحد من جزأي الصغرى تالي الكبرى، أو يتركب الثالث مع الرابع بأن يشارك تالي الصغرى مقدم الكبرى ومقدمها تاليها؛ وكل واحد من هذه الأقسام الستة ينتج باعتبار كل واحد من التأليفين على بساطة النتيجة التي عرفتها في ذلك القسم. وباعتبار التركيب أعني مجموع التأليفين نتيجة هي متصلة مركبة من نتيجة التأليفين؛ والبرهان من الثالث. والأوسط فيه مجموع مقدمي الصغرى والكبرى؛ وإنه من جملة ما يعرف من بعد على التفصيل إن شاء الله. وأما إنتاج أحد المتشاركين إما بعينه أو بكليته مع نتيجة التأليف بينهما، أو عكسها بكليته لمقدم مقدمة كلية كما مرَّ تشترك فيه الأقسام دون الثاني منها. وإنتاج نتيجة التأليف مع تالي أحد المقدمين المتوافقين في الكيف تالي الأخرى يختص بالقسم الثاني. وإنتاج نتيجة التأليف مع أحد طرفي كلية تالي سالبة تشترك فيه الثلاثة الأخيرة؛ والنتيجة متصلة مقدمها متصلة من الطرف الغير المشارك من الصغرى ونتيجة التأليف، وتاليهما متصلة من الطرف الغير المشارك من الكبرى ونتيجة التأليف، ومع الطرفين الغير المشاركين في طرفي النتيجة والبرهان من الثالث والأوسط في المنتج بحسب الشرط الأول ملازمة مقدم متصلة كلية لأحد طرفي الأخرى الموجبة أو للطرف المشارك من الأخرى يعني إذا كانت سالبة فالأوسط ملازمة الطرف المشارك من الكلية للطرف المشارك من الأخرى. وفي المنتج بحسب الشرط الثاني ملازمة المنتج من المتشاركين لنتيحة التأليف أو بالعكس أو ملازمة عكس نتيجة التأليف للمنتج من المتشاركين أو سلب ملازمة النتيجة من المتشاركين لنتيجة التأليف. والمراد بالمنتج من المتشاركين الطرف الذي مع نتيجة التأليف ينتج الطرف الآخر، وبالنتيجة من المتشاركين النتيجة اللازمة من أحد المتشاركين مع نتيجة التأليف. وقد مرّ من قبل أن النتيجة جزئية أبدا سواء كانت كل واحدة من المقدمتين كلية أو إحديهما كلية دون الأخرى. غير أنه لا يكون لازما فإن من الممكن أن تكون كلية في القسمين الآخرين إذا كان تالي المقدمة الموجبة كلية كانت أو جزئية مع نتيجة التأليف منتجا لمقدم متصلة كلية موجبة كانت أو سالبة؛ والبرهان قد يكون من الشكل الأول، وقد يكون من الثاني يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
From these two propositions to others, based on the fact that every two conditionals differing in quality and agreeing in quantity, with the antecedent contradictory in the consequent, are two concomitants and converses. Then it necessarily follows that the product according to his view is much more than the productive moods mentioned in each of the four divisions. Then the four divisions that were mentioned combine with each other; that is in six divisions, because either the first combines with the second by the antecedent of the minor sharing the antecedent of the major and its consequent, or with the third by each of the two parts of the minor sharing the antecedent of the major, or with the fourth by each of the two parts of the minor sharing each of the two parts of the major. Or the second combines with the third by the consequent of the minor sharing each of the two parts of the major, or with the fourth by each of the two parts of the minor sharing the consequent of the major, or the third combines with the fourth by the consequent of the minor sharing the antecedent of the major and its antecedent sharing its consequent. And each of these six divisions produces according to each of the two combinations on the simplicity of the conclusion you know in that division. And according to the combination, i.e., the sum of the two combinations, the conclusion is a conditional composed of the result of the two combinations; and the proof is from the third. And the middle term in it is the sum of the antecedents of the minor and major; and it is among what will be known later in detail, God willing. As for the production of one of the two sharers either specifically or universally with the result of the combination between them, or its universal converse for the antecedent of a universal premise as previously, the divisions share in it except the second. And the production of the result of the combination with the consequent of one of the two premises agreeing in quality with the consequent of the other is specific to the second division. And the production of the result of the combination with one of the two extremes of a universal negative consequent is shared by the last three; and the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is a conditional from the non-participating extreme of the minor and the result of the combination, and its consequent is a conditional from the non-participating extreme of the major and the result of the combination, and with the two non-participating extremes in the extremes of the conclusion and the proof from the third and the middle in the product according to the first condition is a concomitant of a universal conditional antecedent to one of the extremes of the other affirmative or to the participating extreme of the other meaning if it is negative then the middle is a concomitant of the participating extreme of the universal to the participating extreme of the other. And in the product according to the second condition, a concomitant of the product from the two sharers to the result of the combination or vice versa, or a concomitant of the converse of the result of the combination to the product from the two sharers, or a negation of the concomitant of the result from the two sharers to the result of the combination. And by 'the product from the two sharers' is meant the extreme which together with the result of the combination produces the other extreme, and by 'the result from the two sharers' is meant the conclusion necessary from one of the two sharers together with the result of the combination. And it has preceded that the conclusion is always particular whether each of the two premises is universal or one of them is universal and the other not. However, it is not necessary, for it is possible that it be universal in the other two divisions if the consequent of the affirmative premise, whether universal or particular, together with the result of the combination produces a universal conditional antecedent, affirmative or negative; and the proof may be from the first figure or from the second, known by reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on compound conditional syllogisms, specifically how the four basic divisions of conditional syllogisms can combine. The author begins by noting that from two conditional propositions differing in quality but agreeing in quantity, if the antecedents are contradictory in the consequents, they are concomitant and converse. This leads to many more productive moods than the ones previously enumerated. The main part of the passage then enumerates six possible combinations of the four divisions (first with second, first with third, first with fourth, second with third, second with fourth, third with fourth). For each combination, the author explains how the middle term shares parts. The conclusion can be simple (using one combination) or compound (using both combinations). When compound, the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent and consequent are the results of the two combinations, and the proof is from the third figure with the middle term being the sum of the antecedents. The passage then discusses various conditions for producing results: when one of the sharers is taken with the combination result, or the combination result with a consequent, etc. It also notes that the conclusion is usually particular but can be universal in some cases. The proof may be from the first or second figure. The author uses technical terms like 'result of the combination' (نتيجة التأليف) and 'sharers' (المتشاركين). This passage is dense and assumes prior knowledge of the basic divisions and conditional propositions. A student should first understand the four basic types of conditional syllogisms and then follow the combinations described.

The Third Division of Compound Conditional Syllogisms
Defines third division of compound syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third division of compound conditional syllogisms and distinguish it from other divisions based on the completeness of the middle term.

Core Idea
The middle term can be fully present in one premise but only partial in the other.

In the classification of compound conditional syllogisms, the third division presents a unique case where the middle term is complete in one premise and incomplete in the other. This structure has specific conditions and sub-divisions that must be understood to correctly form valid syllogisms. The passage outlines these conditions, though it cuts off before completing the enumeration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic
The passage states that the middle term is a complete part (juz' tamm) in one premise and not complete in the other. Compare this to the first two divisions where the middle term is complete in both or incomplete in both.
2
Recognize the two main conditions
The first condition requires that the two premises (sharers) include a productive combination with an affirmative conditional consequent. The second condition is further divided into two sub-cases: either producing the result of the combination or its universal converse.
The passage cuts off, so only part of the second condition is described.
3
Understand the conclusion structure
Under the first condition, the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the minor premise and whose consequent is the result of combining that conditional with the other premise, matching in quantity.
This conclusion form is specific to this division.
4
Apply to a sample argument
Consider a fiqh example: 'If the sale is valid, ownership transfers. The sale is valid. Therefore, ownership transfers.' The middle term 'valid' is complete in the minor premise (as the predicate) but only part of the conditional's antecedent in the major premise. This illustrates the third division.
Ensure the middle term is indeed partial in one premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثالث: هو ما يكون الأوسط جزءا تاما من إحدى المقدمتين غير تام من الأخرى، وإنه على أقسام ينعقد كل واحد منها في كل شكل، والشرط على قسمين أيضًا: أحدهما اشتمال المتشاركين على تأليف منتج مع إيجاب المتصلة الشرطية التالي؛ والنتيجة حينئذ متصلة مقدمها مقدم الصغرى، وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين تلك الشرطية وبين المقدّمة الأخرى موافقة للشرطية في الكم. والثاني على قسمين أيضًا إما إنتاج نتيجة التأليف أو عكسها بكليته مع المتّصلة البسيطة مقدم المتّصلة الكلية الشرطية الجزء، أو إنتاج نتيجة التأليف
And the third division: it is that in which the middle term is a complete part of one of the two premises and incomplete in the other. And it has divisions, each of which occurs in every figure. And the condition is also two: one of them is that the two sharers include a productive combination with the affirmation of the conditional consequent; and the conclusion then is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the minor, and its consequent is the result of the combination between that conditional and the other premise, agreeing with the conditional in quantity. And the second is also two: either producing the result of the combination or its universal converse with the simple conditional, the antecedent of the universal conditional being the part, or producing the result of the combination.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines the third division of compound conditional syllogisms. In this division, the middle term is completely present in one premise (i.e., as a full part) but only partially present in the other premise. The author states that this division has sub-divisions, each of which can occur in any syllogistic figure. There are two main conditions. The first condition requires that the two sharers (the premises that share the middle term) combine productively with an affirmative conditional consequent. The resulting conclusion is a conditional proposition whose antecedent is the antecedent of the minor premise, and whose consequent is the result of the combination between that conditional and the other premise, matching the conditional in quantity. The second condition is further divided into two: either the result of the combination is produced, or its universal converse is produced together with the simple conditional, where the antecedent of the universal conditional is the part. The text cuts off before completing the enumeration. This division is part of a larger classification of compound syllogisms and requires careful attention to the technical terms 'middle term', 'sharers', and 'result of the combination'.

Key Terms
قسم ثالث (qism thalith): The third division of compound conditional syllogisms where the middle term is complete in one premise and incomplete in the other.
أوسط جزء تام (awsat juz' tamm): The middle term that is a complete part in one premise.
متشاركين (mutasharikayn): The two premises that share the middle term in the compound syllogism.
تأليف منتج (ta'lif muntij): A productive combination of premises that yields a valid conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The third division of compound conditional syllogisms is characterized by the middle term being fully present in one premise and only partially in the other, with specific conditions governing the conclusion.

The Third Division: Definition and Conditions
Definition and conditions of the third division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third division of compound syllogisms and state its two conditions in your own words.

Core Idea
A middle term complete in one premise but not the other defines the third division.

After covering syllogisms where the middle term is complete in both premises (first two divisions), the author now turns to the third division, where the middle term is fully present in only one premise. This division has its own rules and conditions that determine how the conclusion is formed. Understanding these conditions is key to handling compound conditional syllogisms in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of the third division
Find the sentence beginning with 'والقسم الثالث' and note how the author describes the middle term (al-awsat): it is a complete part (juz'un tammun) of one premise but not complete in the other.
Compare this with the first two divisions where the middle is complete in both or incomplete in both.
2
Identify the first condition
The author states that the two shared terms (al-mushtarakin) must include a productive composition (ta'lif muntij) along with an affirmative conditional consequent. This means the antecedent of the major premise must be coextensive with one end of the other premise.
The condition ensures the syllogism yields a valid conclusion.
3
Identify the second condition and its subdivisions
The second condition has two parts: either producing the conclusion of the composition (natijat al-ta'lif) or its converse. This involves the relationship between the product from the shared terms and the overall conclusion.
This condition allows for different forms of the conclusion.
4
Analyze the conclusion types
The author notes that the conclusion is usually particular, but can be universal if the consequent of an affirmative premise combined with the conclusion of the composition yields a universal conditional. This is an exception to the general rule.
Reflect on why the conclusion might be universal in some cases – it depends on the quantity of the premises.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشارك من الكبرى ونتيجة التأليف، ومع الطرفين الغير المشاركين في طرفي النتيجة والبرهان من الثالث والأوسط في المنتج بحسب الشرط الأول ملازمة مقدم متّصلة كلية لأحد طرفي الأخرى الموجبة أو للطرف المشارك من الأخرى يعني إذا كانت سالبة فالأوسط ملازمة الطرف المشارك من الكلية للطرف المشارك من الأخرى. وفي المنتج بحسب الشرط الثاني ملازمة المنتج من المتشاركين لنتيحة التأليف أو بالعكس أو ملازمة عكس نتيجة التأليف للمنتج من المتشاركين أو سلب ملازمة النتيجة من المتشاركين لنتيجة التأليف. والمراد بالمنتج من المتشاركين الطرف الذي مع نتيجة التأليف ينتج الطرف الآخر، وبالنتيجة من المتشاركين النتيجة اللازمة من أحد المتشاركين مع نتيجة التأليف. وقد مرّ من قبل أن النتيجة جزئية أبدا سواء كانت كل واحدة من المقدمتين كلية أو إحديهما كلية دون الأخرى. غير أنه لا يكون لازما فإن من الممكن أن تكون كلية في القسمين الآخرين إذا كان تالي المقدمة الموجبة كلية كانت أو جزئية مع نتيجة التأليف منتجا لمقدم متصلة كلية موجبة كانت أو سالبة؛ والبرهان قد يكون من الشكل الأول، وقد يكون من الثاني يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى. والقسم الثالث: هو ما يكون الأوسط جزءا تاما من إحدى المقدمتين غير تام من الأخرى، وإنه على أقسام ينعقد كل واحد منها في كل شكل، والشرط على قسمين أيضًا: أحدهما اشتمال / المتشاركين على تأليف منتج مع إيجاب المتصلة الشرطية التالي؛ والنتيجة حينئذ متّصلة مقدمها مقدم الصغرى، وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين تلك الشرطية وبين المقدّمة الأخرى موافقة للشرطية في الكم. والثاني على قسمين أيضًا إما إنتاج نتيجة التأليف أو عكسها بكليته مع المتّصلة البسيطة مقدم المتّصلة الكلية الشرطية الجزء، أو إنتاج نتيجة التأليف مع المتّصلة البسيطة الجزء من الثالث على التقدير الأول، ومن الأول على التقدير الثاني. والأوسط على التقديرين مقدم المتّصلة الشرطية الجزء: مثال ما تكون المتصلة الشرطية التالي متّصلة الجزء كلما كان «ج ده فكلما كان «أب» فـ هـ ط»، وكلما كان «هـط» فـوز» ينتج كلَّما كان «ج د) فكلما كان هـ د» «أب» فـ «وز» لانتظام ما تكون نتيجة التأليف مع المتّصلة البسيطة منتجا لمقدم المتّصلة الكلية الشرطية الجزء من متصلة الجزء كلما كان «ج د» فـ «أب»، وكلما كان قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فاهـط فـ «اوز» ينتج كلما كان (ج) د) و (هـ ط» فـ هـ ز مثال ما يكون عكس
The shared [term] from the major premise and the conclusion of the composition, and with the two non-shared terms in the two ends of the conclusion and the demonstration from the third and the middle in the productive [syllogism] according to the first condition: a universal conditional antecedent is coextensive with one of the two ends of the other affirmative [premise] or with the shared term of the other, meaning if it is negative, then the middle is coextensive with the shared term of the universal to the shared term of the other. And in the productive [syllogism] according to the second condition: coextensive of the product from the two shared [terms] to the conclusion of the composition, or the converse, or coextensive of the converse of the conclusion of the composition to the product from the two shared [terms], or negation of coextensive of the conclusion from the two shared [terms] to the conclusion of the composition. And what is meant by 'product from the two shared [terms]' is the term that with the conclusion of the composition produces the other term, and by 'conclusion from the two shared [terms]' is the conclusion necessary from one of the two shared [terms] along with the conclusion of the composition. It has passed before that the conclusion is always particular, whether each of the two premises is universal or one of them is universal without the other. However, it is not necessary, for it is possible that it is universal in the other two divisions, if the consequent of the affirmative premise, whether universal or particular, with the conclusion of the composition produces a universal conditional antecedent, whether affirmative or negative; and the demonstration may be from the first figure, and may be from the second, known by reflection, God willing. And the third division: it is that where the middle is a complete part of one of the two premises, not complete from the other, and it is divided into sections each of which occurs in every figure, and the condition is also in two divisions: one of them is that the two shared [terms] include a productive composition with affirmation of the conditional [premise's] consequent; and the conclusion then is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the minor premise, and whose consequent is the conclusion of the composition between that conditional and the other premise, agreeing with the conditional in quantity. And the second is also in two divisions: either producing the conclusion of the composition or its converse entirely with the simple conditional [as] the antecedent of the universal conditional [premise's] part, or producing the conclusion of the composition with the simple conditional [as] the part from the third on the first estimation, and from the first on the second estimation. And the middle in both estimations is the antecedent of the conditional [premise's] part: an example of what the conditional [premise's] consequent is a conditional part [such that] 'Whenever (ج د), then whenever (أ ب), then (هـ ط)' and 'Whenever (هـ ط), then (وز)' produces 'Whenever (ج د), then whenever (أ ب), then (وز)' because of the arrangement of the two conditionals then into a productive syllogism for the consequent of this conclusion: an example from a disjunctive part [such that] 'Whenever (ج د), then either (أ ب) or (هـ ط)', and 'Whenever (هـ ط), then (وز)' produces a conditional consequent and a disjunctive consequent, as our saying 'Whenever (ج د), then whenever (أ ب), then (وز)'. As for the disjunctive consequent, as our saying 'Whenever (ج د), then either (أ ب) or (وز)'. And an example of what the two shared [terms] are on a productive composition, and the conditional [premise] is the antecedent of the conditional from a conditional part [such that] 'Whenever (ج د), then (أ ب)', and 'Whenever (أ ب), then (هـ ط)' [then] '(وز)' produces, it may be if whenever (ج د) implies (هـ ط) then (وز), otherwise its contradictory is true, which is our saying 'Not at all if whenever (ج د) then (هـ ط) then (وز)'; and it, with the minor premise of the syllogism, produces from the second. And the minor premise is the minor [premise] 'Not at all if whenever (أ ب) then (هـ ط) then whenever (ج د) then (هـ ط)': and that is a contradiction because whenever (أ ب) implies (هـ ط) and whenever (وز) implies (هـ ط), then the simple conditional is true; its example from a disjunctive part of the exclusive [type] 'Whenever (ج د) [is] in (أ ب)', and 'Whenever either (أ ب) or (هـ ط) then (وز)' produces, it may be if whenever (ج د) then either (أ ب) or (هـ ط) then (وز) due to what passed of the contradiction. And if the conditional [premise] is negative, then the conclusion is also negative: an example of what the conclusion of the composition with the simple conditional produces the antecedent of the universal conditional [premise's] part from a conditional part [such that] 'Whenever (ج د), then (أ ب)', and 'Whenever it may be if (أ ب) then (هـ ط) then (وز)' produces 'Whenever (ج د) and (هـ ط) then (هـ ز)' example of what is the converse
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an advanced commentary on the third division of compound syllogisms, specifically those involving conditional premises. The author begins by clarifying the roles of shared terms in the first and second conditions for productive syllogisms. Under the first condition, the middle term is coextensive with one end of the other premise, either affirmatively or negatively. Under the second condition, the product of the shared terms relates to the conclusion of the composition in various ways: directly, conversely, or negatively. The author then reminds that the conclusion is usually particular, but can be universal in certain cases when the consequent of an affirmative premise combines with the conclusion of the composition to produce a universal conditional. The demonstration can be in the first or second figure. The author then defines the third division proper: the middle term is a complete part of one premise but incomplete in the other. This division has subcases in every figure. Two main conditions are given: first, the shared terms must include a productive composition with an affirmative conditional consequent, yielding a conditional conclusion whose antecedent is the minor premise's antecedent and whose consequent is the conclusion of the composition. The second condition splits into two: either the conclusion of the composition or its converse is produced with the simple conditional as part of a universal conditional. The author provides examples using logical variables (ج, د, أ, ب, هـ, ط, ز, و) to illustrate muttasila (conditional) and munfasila (disjunctive) forms. For instance, from 'Whenever (ج د) then whenever (أ ب) then (هـ ط)' and 'Whenever (هـ ط) then (وز)', we derive 'Whenever (ج د) then whenever (أ ب) then (وز)'. A disjunctive example: 'Whenever (ج د) then either (أ ب) or (هـ ط)' and 'Whenever (هـ ط) then (وز)' yields 'Whenever (ج د) then whenever (أ ب) then (وز)'. The author also discusses negative conditionals, stating they produce negative conclusions similarly, and uses a reductio ad absurdum (خلف) argument to show necessary conditionality. The passage ends with an example of the converse scenario.

The Syllogism

Examples of Third Division Syllogisms with Conditional and Disjunctive Premises
Examples of third division with conditional and disjunctive consequents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a third-division syllogism given two conditional or disjunctive premises using the correct procedure.

Core Idea
Arranging two conditionals or a conditional with a disjunction yields the product of the shared terms.

In the previous lesson we learned the definition and conditions of the third division. Now we see how it works in practice through the examples provided by the author. These examples use logical variables to show the structure of the syllogism when the consequent is itself a conditional or a disjunction. By working through these examples, you will be able to apply the same reasoning to real Islamic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first example with a conditional consequent
The author gives: 'Whenever (ج د), then whenever (أ ب) then (هـ ط)' and 'Whenever (هـ ط) then (وز)'. Identify the middle term (هـ ط) which is complete in the second premise but incomplete (part of the consequent) in the first.
The middle is (هـ ط) – it is the antecedent of the second premise and part of the consequents of the first.
2
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is 'Whenever (ج د), then whenever (أ ب) then (وز)'. Notice that the final consequent (وز) is the consequent of the second premise. The antecedent remains the same as the original first premise's antecedent.
This is the result of composing the two conditionals.
3
Examine the disjunctive example
Now consider: 'Whenever (ج د), then either (أ ب) or (هـ ط)' and 'Whenever (هـ ط) then (وز)'. The conclusion is 'Whenever (ج د), then whenever (أ ب) then (وز)' – the same pattern as before.
The disjunction in the first premise leads to the same conclusion because (هـ ط) is eliminated by the second premise.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Replace the variables with terms from, say, fiqh: 'Whenever there is doubt, then either performance is obligatory or it is not.' and 'Whenever performance is not obligatory, then it is allowed.' The conclusion: 'Whenever there is doubt, then whenever performance is obligatory, then it is allowed.' (This is a simplified illustration.)
The step requires care to maintain the logical form.

Commentary
This passage is an advanced commentary on the third division of compound syllogisms, specifically those involving conditional premises. The author begins by clarifying the roles of shared terms in the first and second conditions for productive syllogisms. Under the first condition, the middle term is coextensive with one end of the other premise, either affirmatively or negatively. Under the second condition, the product of the shared terms relates to the conclusion of the composition in various ways: directly, conversely, or negatively. The author then reminds that the conclusion is usually particular, but can be universal in certain cases when the consequent of an affirmative premise combines with the conclusion of the composition to produce a universal conditional. The demonstration can be in the first or second figure. The author then defines the third division proper: the middle term is a complete part of one premise but incomplete in the other. This division has subcases in every figure. Two main conditions are given: first, the shared terms must include a productive composition with an affirmative conditional consequent, yielding a conditional conclusion whose antecedent is the minor premise's antecedent and whose consequent is the conclusion of the composition. The second condition splits into two: either the conclusion of the composition or its converse is produced with the simple conditional as part of a universal conditional. The author provides examples using logical variables (ج, د, أ, ب, هـ, ط, ز, و) to illustrate muttasila (conditional) and munfasila (disjunctive) forms. For instance, from 'Whenever (ج د) then whenever (أ ب) then (هـ ط)' and 'Whenever (هـ ط) then (وز)', we derive 'Whenever (ج د) then whenever (أ ب) then (وز)'. The Syllogism
advanced

Composing Syllogisms with Conditional Consequents
Author shows how to form syllogisms when the consequent is itself a conditional, with reductio proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a compound syllogism where the consequent of a conditional is itself a conditional, and verify its validity using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
When a conditional's consequent is itself a conditional, the middle term is the antecedent of the inner conditional, and reductio proves the conclusion.

This passage shows how to handle syllogisms where the consequent is a conditional proposition. The author uses abstract letters to illustrate the composition and then demonstrates a reductio proof. Understanding this allows you to deal with complex chain conditionals in Islamic theological arguments, such as those linking divine attributes to necessary conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall structure
Notice that the major premise ends with a conditional consequent: 'if A then (if B then C)'. The minor premise provides another conditional linking terms.
The pattern is: Major: P → (Q → R), Minor: R → S → Conclusion: P → (Q → S)
2
Locate the middle term
The middle term is the antecedent of the inner conditional in the major premise, which appears as the antecedent in the minor premise. For example, in 'If D then (if J then H)' and 'If H then Z', the middle term is H.
The author states: 'The middle term in both estimations is the antecedent of the conditional part.'
3
Apply the reductio method
To prove the conclusion, assume its negation: 'It is not the case that if D then (if J then Z)'. From this and the minor premise, derive a contradiction using the given premises, thus establishing the original conclusion.
This is the standard method of reductio (al-khalf) used throughout the passage.
4
Verify the compound chain
Check that the two conditionals form a productive syllogism: the first conditional's consequent becomes the antecedent of the second, allowing the chain to transmit the implication from the first antecedent to the final consequent.
The author calls this 'the two conditionals then form a productive syllogism for the consequent of this conclusion.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع المتّصلة البسيطة الجزء من الثالث على التقدير الأول، ومن الأول على التقدير الثاني. والأوسط على التقديرين مقدم المتّصلة الشرطية الجزء: مثال ما تكون المتصلة الشرطية التالي متّصلة الجزء كلما كان «ج د» فكلما كان «أب» فـ «هـ ط»، وكلما كان «هـ ط» فـ «و ز» ينتج كلَّما كان «ج د» فكلما كان «أب» فـ «و ز» لانتظام المتصلتين حينئذ قياسًا منتجا لتالي هذه النتيجة: مثاله من منفصلة الجزء كلما كان «ج د» فإما أن يكون «أب» أو «هـ ط»، وكلما كان «هـ ط» فـ «و ز» ينتج متّصلة التالي ومنفصلة التالي كقولنا كلما كان «ج د» فكلما كان «أب» فـ «و ز». وأما منفصلة التالي كقولنا كلما كان «ج د» فإما «أب» أو «و ز». ومثاله ما يكون المتشاركان على تأليف منتج، والشرطية مقدم المتّصلة من متّصلة الجزء كلَّما كان «ج د» فـ «أب»، وكلَّما كان «أب» فـ «هـ ط» فـ «و ز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كلما كان «ج د» فـ «ه ط» فـ «و ز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان كلما كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ط» فـ «و ز»؛ وإنه مع صغرى القياس ينتج من الثاني. والصغرى هي الصغرى ليس البتة إذا كان كلَّما كان «أب» فـ «هـ ط» فكلما كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ط»: وذلك خلف لأنه كلَّما كان «أب» فـ «هـ ط» وكلَّما كان «و ز» فـ «هـ ط» فقد صدقت المتصلة البسيطة؛ مثاله من منفصلة الجزء المانعة الجمع كلما كان «ج د» في «أب»، وكلَّما كان إما «أب» وإما «ه ط» فـ «و ز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كلما كان «ج د» فإما «أب» أو «هـ ط» فـ «و ز» لما مر من الخلف.
With the simple conditional, the part from the third on the first estimation, and from the first on the second estimation. The middle term in both estimations is the antecedent of the conditional part. Example: the conditional whose consequent is a conditional part: Whenever J D, then whenever A B, then H T, and whenever H T, then W Z, yields: Whenever J D, then whenever A B, then W Z, because the two conditionals then form a productive syllogism for the consequent of this conclusion. Its example from a disjunctive part: Whenever J D, then either A B or H T, and whenever H T, then W Z, yields a conditional consequent and a disjunctive consequent, like our saying: Whenever J D, then whenever A B, then W Z. As for the disjunctive consequent, like our saying: Whenever J D, then either A B or W Z. And its example is when the two participants form a productive composition, and the conditional is the antecedent of the conditional from a conditional part: Whenever J D, then A B, and whenever A B, then H T, then W Z, yields: Possibly, if whenever J D, then H T, then W Z; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying: Absolutely not, if whenever J D, then H T, then W Z. And it, with the minor premise of the syllogism, produces from the second. The minor premise is that it is absolutely not that if whenever A B, then H T, then whenever J D, then H T. That is a contradiction because whenever A B, then H T, and whenever W Z, then H T, so the simple conditional is true. Its example from a disjunctive part that prevents conjunction: Whenever J D, then A B, and whenever either A B or H T, then W Z, yields: Possibly, if whenever J D, then either A B or H T, then W Z, due to the previous reductio.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how to compose syllogisms when the consequent of a conditional proposition is itself a conditional. The author begins by stating the role of the middle term: it is the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional part. Then he gives an example: 'Whenever J D, then whenever A B, then H T' and 'Whenever H T, then W Z' produce the conclusion 'Whenever J D, then whenever A B, then W Z'. This works because the two conditionals together form a productive syllogism for the consequent. The author then illustrates with a disjunctive minor, showing the same structure. Next, he presents a case where the major is a conditional whose antecedent is itself a conditional: 'Whenever J D, then A B' and 'Whenever A B, then H T, then W Z' yields the conclusion 'Whenever J D, then H T, then W Z' by reductio: if not, the contradictory 'It is never the case that if whenever J D then H T, then W Z' would hold. He then shows that with a minor premise (the negation of the contradictory), the syllogism produces the desired result. The minor is 'It is never the case that if whenever A B, then H T, then whenever J D, then H T', which is false because both 'Whenever A B, then H T' and 'Whenever W Z, then H T' are true. The example uses a disjunctive that prevents conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), reinforcing the method. Throughout, the author uses placeholder letters to abstract the logical structure, typical of advanced texts in Islamic logic.

الشرطية الجزء
الخلف

In compound conditional syllogisms, the middle term is the antecedent of the inner conditional, and validity can be shown by reductio.

Identifying the Middle Term in Mixed Syllogisms
Author explains how negative conditionals yield negative conclusions and compares these mixed syllogisms to categorical ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent of the conditional as the middle term in a mixed syllogism and determine whether the conclusion follows with necessity or possibility.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the middle term is the antecedent of the conditional, not a categorical term.

This passage from the logic text explains how mixed syllogisms, which combine a categorical premise and a conditional premise, are analyzed. The key insight is that the antecedent of the conditional functions as the middle term, analogous to the middle term in pure categorical syllogisms. The author also shows how negative conditionals affect the modality of the conclusion, leading to negative or possible conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Look for one premise that is a categorical proposition (often expressed as a universal conditional, e.g., 'Every A is B') and one that is a genuine conditional (if...then...). In Arabic, the categorical may be marked by 'kullamā kāna...fa-...'.
Categorical premises can be disguised as conditionals; examine their logical form.
2
Locate the antecedent of the conditional premise
The conditional premise has the form 'If P then Q'. The antecedent is P. This antecedent will be the middle term that links the two premises.
The author explicitly states: 'al-awsaṭ ... huwa muqaddam al-muttaṣila al-sharṭiyya al-juz''.
3
Find the antecedent in the categorical premise
The antecedent (P) must appear as a term (subject or predicate) in the categorical premise. This establishes the connection between the two premises.
For example, if the categorical is 'Every JD is AB' and the conditional's antecedent is AB, then AB appears as the predicate of the categorical.
4
Determine the conclusion
The conclusion will relate the consequent of the conditional (Q) to the other term from the categorical premise (the subject, if the antecedent was the predicate). The modality of the conclusion depends on the premises: if the conditional is negative, the conclusion may be negative or possible.
The author gives examples where negative conditionals yield 'possibly' conclusions.
5
Apply the general rule
Recognize that the rules for mixed syllogisms mirror those for categorical syllogisms, with the antecedent playing the role of the middle term. The number of valid moods in each figure corresponds to the categorical case.
This generalization is stated at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت الشرطية سالبة كانت النتيجة سالبة كذلك: مثال ما تكون نتيجة التأليف مع المتّصلة البسيطة منتجا لمقدم المتّصلة الكلية الشرطية الجزء من متصلة الجزء كلما كان «ج د» فـ «أب»، وكلما كان إذا كان «أب» فـ «هـ ط» فـ «و ز» ينتج كلما كان «ج د» و «هـ ط» فـ «و ز» مثال ما يكون عكس نتيجة التأليف بكليته مع المتصلة البسيطة منتجا لمقدم المتصلة الكلية ليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» فـ «أب»، وكلَّما كان ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هـ ط» فـ «و ز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ط» فـ «و ز». وعلى هذا إذا كانت منفصلة الجزء مثال ما تكون نتيجة التأليف مع المتصلة البسيطة منتجا لتالي السالبة الشرطية الجزء كلما كان «ج د» فـ «أب»، وليس البتة إذا كان «و ز» فكلما كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ط» ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان «و ز» فكلما كان «أب» فـ «هـ ط». ثمَّ الأوسط فيما يكون مفيدًا للزوم النتيجة هو مقدم المتّصلة الشرطية الجزء. وبالجملة فحكم هذا القسم هو حكم القياس المؤلف من الحملي والمتّصل إلا أن المشارك مكان الحملية ثمة شرطية هنا. ولو كان كذلك لكان عدد ضروب كل شكل من كل قسم هو عدد الضروب هناك؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
And if the conditional is negative, the conclusion is negative as well. Example: The result of composition with the simple conditional produces the antecedent of the universal conditional part from a conditional part: Whenever J D, then A B, and whenever if A B, then H T, then W Z, yields: Whenever J D and H T, then W Z. Example of the inverse: The result of composition in its entirety with the simple conditional produces the antecedent of the universal conditional: It is never the case that if J D, then A B, and whenever it is never the case that if A B, then H T, then W Z, yields: Possibly, if J D, then H T, then W Z. And likewise if it is a disjunctive part, example: The result of composition with the simple conditional produces the consequent of the negative conditional part: Whenever J D, then A B, and it is never the case that if W Z, then whenever J D, then H T, yields: It is never the case that if W Z, then whenever A B, then H T. Then the middle term, in what is useful for the necessitation of the conclusion, is the antecedent of the conditional part. In summary, the rule of this section is the rule of the syllogism composed of categorical and conditional, except that the participant in place of the categorical here is a conditional. If that were the case, the number of moods of each figure in each section would be the number of moods there; that will be known later.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where the conditional proposition in the syllogism is negative, and the conclusion is also negative. The author gives an example with a conditional part: 'Whenever J D, then A B' and 'Whenever if A B, then H T, then W Z' yields 'Whenever J D and H T, then W Z'. Then he shows the inverse case: the entire composition with the simple conditional produces the antecedent of the universal conditional, using negative conditionals to yield a possible conclusion. He also provides an example with a disjunctive part, where the conclusion is negative. After these examples, the author states a general rule: the middle term that is useful for necessitating the conclusion is the antecedent of the conditional part. He then summarizes that this entire section follows the same rules as syllogisms composed of categorical and conditional propositions, except that the 'participant' (the term that appears in both premises) is here a conditional instead of a categorical term. Consequently, the number of valid moods in each figure would correspond to the number of moods in that earlier system, which will be explained later. The passage thus links the mixed syllogisms to previously established classifications.

Conditional Part Must Be Universal Affirmative
Rule for conditional parts in compound syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a conditional used as a part in a compound syllogism must be universal affirmative and verify when this condition is met.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative in conditional parts prevents gaps in inference.

The author introduces a key rule for mixed syllogisms: the conditional that serves as a component of another conditional must be universal affirmative. Without this, the chain of implication breaks. Let's examine the example he gives to see why this is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional premises
The first premise is 'Whenever J D, then H T'. The second premise is 'Whenever W Z, then whenever H T, then A B'. Note the second premise's consequent is itself a conditional.
2
Check the quantifier and quality of the first premise
The author requires the conditional part (the consequent of the second premise, i.e., 'whenever H T, then A B') to be universal affirmative. In the example, 'whenever H T, then A B' is indeed universal affirmative: for all H T, then A B.
If it were particular or negative, the inference would fail.
3
Derive the two conclusions
From the premises, two conclusions follow: first, 'Whenever J D, then whenever W Z, then A B' (conditional chain). Second, 'Whenever J D and W Z, then whenever J D, then A B' (by combining antecedents).
Both conclusions are valid only because the conditional part is universal affirmative.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Let J D = 'a person has hadath (ritual impurity)', H T = 'must perform wudu', W Z = 'intends to pray', A B = 'prayer is valid'. Premise 1: Whenever hadath, wudu is required (universal affirmative). Premise 2: Whenever one intends to pray, then whenever wudu is required, prayer is valid. Conclusion: Whenever hadath, then whenever one intends to pray, prayer is valid. This holds only because the conditional part ('whenever wudu is required, prayer is valid') is universal affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتصلة التي هي جزء المتصلة الشرطية الجزء فإنه يجب أن تكون موجبة كلية فلو قلت كلما كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ط»، وكلَّما كان «و ز» فكلما كان «هـ ط» فـ «أب» ينتج قولنا كلَّما كان «ج د» فكلما كان «و ز» فـ «أب» وهي المتصلة الأولى، وينتج أيضًا كلما كان «ج د» فـ «و ز» فكلما كان «ج د» فـ «أب»، وهي المتّصلة الثانية. وأما المنفصلة فإنها إذا كانت هي الصغرى للمتصلة فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثالث، والثاني عن الرابع والأول عن الثاني والثالث عن الرابع إن كان بالعكس؛ لأن الاعتبار بحسب وضع الحد الأوسط في المتّصلة فقط دون المنفصلة على ما ستعرفه في المؤلف من المتصل والمنفصل. أما مثال الأول والثالث في القسم الأول؛ والمنفصلة مانعة الخلو كلما كان «ج د» فإما أن يكون «أب» أو «هـ ط»، وكلما كان «هـ ط» فـ «و ز» ينتج كلَّما كان «ج د» فإما أن يكون «أب» أو «و ز» لامتناع الخلو حينئذ عن «أب» وغيره.
As for the conditional that is part of the conditional part, it must be universal affirmative. So if you say: Whenever J D, then H T, and whenever W Z, then whenever H T, then A B, yields our saying: Whenever J D, then whenever W Z, then A B, which is the first conditional; and it also yields: Whenever J D and W Z, then whenever J D, then A B, which is the second conditional. As for the disjunctive, if it is the minor of the conditional, then the first figure is not distinguished from the third, nor the second from the fourth, nor the first from the second, nor the third from the fourth if it is the opposite; because the consideration is according to the position of the middle term in the conditional only, not in the disjunctive, as you will learn in the composition of conditional and disjunctive. As for example of the first and third in the first section: the disjunctive that prevents vacuity: Whenever J D, then either A B or H T, and whenever H T, then W Z, yields: Whenever J D, then either A B or W Z, because vacuity is then impossible from A B and other.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage lays down two important rules for mixed syllogisms involving conditional and disjunctive parts. First, the conditional that is part of the conditional part must be universal affirmative. The author illustrates with an example: 'Whenever J D, then H T' and 'Whenever W Z, then whenever H T, then A B' generate two conclusions: the first is 'Whenever J D, then whenever W Z, then A B', and the second is 'Whenever J D and W Z, then whenever J D, then A B'. This demonstrates the productivity when the conditional part is universal affirmative. Second, when a disjunctive proposition is the minor premise of a conditional syllogism, the usual distinctions between figures (first vs third, second vs fourth, etc.) collapse because the middle term is considered only in the conditional part, not in the disjunctive. The author provides an example using a disjunctive that prevents vacuity (mani'at al-khuluw): 'Whenever J D, then either A B or H T' and 'Whenever H T, then W Z' yield 'Whenever J D, then either A B or W Z' because if the disjunction were not true, vacuity would occur. This rule emphasizes that the logical form depends on the conditional component, not the disjunctive one.

Figures Collapse When Disjunctive Is Minor
Indistinguishability of figures when disjunctive is minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the distinctions between syllogistic figures vanish when a disjunctive proposition serves as the minor premise in a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
In mixed disjunctive-conditionals, only the conditional's middle term determines figure.

The author turns to disjunctives: when a disjunctive proposition is the minor premise in a conditional syllogism, the usual figure distinctions (first vs third, etc.) become irrelevant. This is because the middle term, which determines the figure, is considered only in the conditional part. Let's analyze the example to see why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise and the conditional premise
The example: 'Whenever J D, then either A B or H T' (disjunctive, preventing vacuity) and 'Whenever H T, then W Z' (conditional). The disjunctive is the minor (it has the subject term of the conclusion), and the conditional is the major.
2
Locate the middle term in the conditional
In the conditional premise 'Whenever H T, then W Z', the middle term is 'H T' (the antecedent). The figure of the syllogism would normally depend on the position of this middle term relative to the minor term. However, since the minor premise is a disjunctive, the middle term is not part of the disjunctive; it appears only in the conditional.
3
Understand why figures are not distinguished
The author explains that 'the consideration is according to the position of the middle term in the conditional only, not in the disjunctive.' Because the disjunctive introduces multiple possibilities (A B or H T), the middle term (H T) appears as one of the disjuncts, but its position relative to the minor term in the disjunctive is ambiguous. Hence, figure classification loses meaning.
This applies whether the disjunctive is 'preventing vacuity' (exclusive) or 'preventing conjunction' (inclusive) – the author specifically mentions 'man'at al-khuluw'.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Consider: 'Whenever something is created, it is either eternal (qadim) or temporally originated (hadith)' (disjunctive), and 'Whenever it is temporally originated, it has a cause.' Conclusion: 'Whenever something is created, it is either eternal or has a cause.' Here, the middle term 'temporally originated' appears only in the conditional, not as a term in the disjunctive. The figure is not defined as first, second, etc.

Overview of Mixed Disjunctive-Conditional Syllogisms
Author compares mixed disjunctive-conditional to categorical-conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a mixed syllogism combining a disjunctive and a conditional premise and classify its mood.

Core Idea
Mixed disjunctive-conditional syllogisms mirror categorical-conditional ones, but only the conditional determines the figure.

This passage introduces compound syllogisms that pair a disjunctive premise (either/or) with a conditional premise (if/then). The author draws an analogy to the simpler categorical-conditional mix, but here the categorical is replaced by a conditional. The key is that the middle term resides only in the conditional, not the disjunctive, which affects how we count their moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the disjunctive (manfasila) and the conditional (muttasila). The disjunctive is either exclusive (مانعة الخلو) or inclusive (مانعة الجمع).
In this passage, the disjunctive often uses 'إما... أو'.
2
Determine which premise is the minor and which is the major
The order matters. The author considers cases where the disjunctive is the minor and the conditional is the major, and vice versa.
The middle term is only in the conditional, so the figure is based solely on the conditional's structure.
3
Classify the disjunction type
Note whether the disjunction is exclusive (مانعة الخلو) or inclusive (مانعة الجمع). This affects the conclusion.
Exclusive means at least one disjunct is true, not both; inclusive means at most one is true.
4
Apply the analogous ruling
The author says the ruling is the same as for categorical-conditional syllogisms, but with a conditional in place of the categorical. So the number of moods per figure matches.
This analogy is key to understanding the system.

Source Text

نتيجة التأليف بكليته مع المتصلة البسيطة منتجا لمقدم المتصلة الكلية ليس البتة إذا كان (ج د) فـ (أب)، وكلما كان ليس البتة إذا كان (أب) فـ (هـ ط) فوز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان (ج د) فـ (هـ ط) فـ (وز). وعلى هذا إذا كانت منفصلة الجزء مثال ما تكون نتيجة التأليف مع المتصلة البسيطة منتجا لتالي السالبة الشرطية الجزء كلما كان (ج) د فـ (أب)، وليس البتة إذا كان (وز) فكلما كان (ج) د فـ (هـ ط) ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان (و ز) فكلما كان (أب) فـ (هـ ط). ثم الأوسط فيما يكون مفيدا للزوم النتيجة هو مقدم المتصلة الشرطية الجزء. وبالجملة فحكم هذا القسم هو حكم القياس المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل إلا أن المشارك مكان الحملية ثمة شرطية هنا. ولو كان كذلك لكان عدد ضروب كل شكل من كل قسم هو عدد الضروب هناك؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد. وأما المتصلة التي هي جزء المتصلة الشرطية الجزء فإنه يجب أن تكون موجبة كلية فلو قلت كلما كان (ج د) فـ (هـ ط)، وكلما كان (وز) فكلما كان (هـ ط) فـ (أب) ينتج قولنا كلما كان (ج د) فكلما كان (وز) فـ (أب) وهي المتصلة الأولى، وينتج أيضا كلما كان (ج د) فـ (وز) فكلما كان (ج) د فـ (أب)، وهي المتصلة الثانية. وأما المنفصلة فإنها إذا كانت هي الصغرى للمتصلة فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثالث، والثاني عن الرابع والأول عن الثاني والثالث عن الرابع إن كان بالعكس؛ لأن الاعتبار بحسب وضع الحد الأوسط في المتصلة فقط دون المنفصلة على ما ستعرفه في المؤلف من المتصل والمنفصل. أما مثال الأول والثالث في القسم الأول؛ والمنفصلة مانعة الخلو كلما كان (ج د) فإما أن يكون (أب) أو (هـ ط)، وكلما كان (هـ ط) فـ (وز) ينتج كلما كان (ج د) فإما أن يكون (أب) أو (و ز) لامتناع الخلو حينئذ عن (أب) وغيره. وأما مثال الثاني والرابع؛ والمنفصلة مانعة الجمع كلما كان (ج د) فإما أن يكون (أب) أو (هـ ط)، وكلما كان (وز) و (هـ ط) ينتج كلما كان (ج د) فإما أن يكون (أب) أو (و ز). وأما مثال الأول والثالث في القسم الثاني؛ والمنفصلة مانعة الخلو كلما كان إما (أب) أو (هـ ط) فـ (ج) د، والكبرى بحالها ينتج قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د فإما (أب) أو (و ز) لما مر من امتناع الخلو. ومثال الثاني والرابع كلما كان (وز) فـ ه ط؛ والصغرى بحالها، والمنفصلة مانعة الجمع ينتج قد يكون إذا كان (ج د) فإما (أب) أو (وز). وأما نتيجة الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول والثاني في القسمين الآخرين أعني ما تكون المتصلة فيه تالي الكبرى أو مقدمها. والثالث والرابع فيهما دون القياس أيضا : مثال الأول والثاني في القسم الأول من هذين القسمين؛ والمنفصلة فيه مانعة الجمع كلما كان (ج) د فـ (هـ ط)، وكلما كان (وز) فإما (أب) أو (هـ ط) ينتج قد يكون إذا كان إما (أب) أو (ج) د فـ (وز) مانعة الجمع من الثالث والأوسط المنفصلة ومثال الثالث والرابع؛ والمنفصلة مانعة الخلو كلما كان (هـ ط) فـ (ج د)، وكلما كان (و ز) فإما (ه ط) أو (أب) ينتج قد يكون إذا كان إما (أب) أو (ج) د مانعة الخلو من الثالث؛ والأوسط فيه أيضا المنفصلة. ومثال الأول والثاني في القسم الثاني من هذين القسمين؛ والمنفصلة مانعة الجمع كلما كان (ج د) فـ (هـ ط)، وكلما كان إما (هـ ط) أو (أب) فـ (وز) قد يكون إذا كان إما (أب) أو (ج) د فـ (وز) من الثالث؛ والأوسط فيه المنفصلة. ومثال الثالث والرابع؛ والمنفصلة مانعة الخلو كلما كان (هـ ط) فـ (ج د)، وكلما كان إما (أب) أو (هـ ط) فـ (وز) ينتج قد يكون إذا كان إما (أب) أو (ج) د فـ (وز) من الثالث والأوسط المنفصلة أيضا، والنتيجة مانعة الخلو كذلك. ويتبين كل واحد منها بالخلف؛ وذلك يعرف
The result of the compound (syllogism) entirely with the simple conditional produces a premise of the universal conditional absolutely not (or: not at all) when (J is D) so (A is B), and whenever absolutely not when (A is B) so (H is T) then (W is Z) produces it may be that when (J is D) so (H is T) then (W is Z). And in this way, if the disjunctive is a part, example of what the result of the compound with the simple conditional produces is the consequent of the negative conditional part: whenever (J) is D so (A is B), and absolutely not when (W is Z) so whenever (J) is D so (H is T) produces absolutely not when (W is Z) so whenever (A is B) so (H is T). Then the middle in what is useful for the necessity of the conclusion is the antecedent of the conditional part. In sum, the ruling of this section is the ruling of the syllogism composed of the categorical and the conditional, except that the partner stands in place of the categorical there, a conditional here. And if it were so, the number of moods of each figure from each section would be the number of moods there; and that is known afterwards. As for the conditional that is a part of the conditional part, it must be affirmative universal: so if you say whenever (J is D) so (H is T), and whenever (W is Z) so whenever (H is T) so (A is B) produces our statement whenever (J is D) so whenever (W is Z) so (A is B) – that is the first conditional – and it also produces whenever (J is D) so (W is Z) so whenever (J) is D so (A is B) – that is the second conditional. As for the disjunctive, if it is the minor of the conditional, then the first figure is not distinguished from the third, nor the second from the fourth, nor the first from the second, nor the third from the fourth if it is reversed; because the consideration is only according to the position of the middle term in the conditional, not in the disjunctive, as you will know in the compound of the conditional and the disjunctive. As for the example of the first and third in the first division: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw): whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (H is T), and whenever (H is T) then (W is Z) produces whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z) due to the impossibility of emptiness then regarding (A is B) and other than it. As for the example of the second and fourth: the disjunctive inclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam'): whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (H is T), and whenever (W is Z) and (H is T) produces whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z). As for the example of the first and third in the second division: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction: whenever it is either (A is B) or (H is T) then (J) is D, and the major as it is produces it may be that when (J) is D then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z) due to what preceded of the impossibility of emptiness. And the example of the second and fourth: whenever (W is Z) then H is T; and the minor as it is, and the disjunctive inclusive disjunction produces it may be that when (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z). As for the result of the first mood of the first and second figures in the other two divisions – I mean those in which the conditional is either the consequent of the major or its antecedent. And the third and fourth in them, also without the syllogism: example of the first and second in the first division of these two divisions: and the disjunctive in it is inclusive disjunction: whenever (J) is D then (H is T), and whenever (W is Z) then either (A is B) or (H is T) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D then (W is Z) – inclusive disjunction – from the third, and the middle is the disjunctive. And the example of the third and fourth: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction: whenever (H is T) then (J is D), and whenever (W is Z) then either (H is T) or (A is B) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D – exclusive disjunction – from the third; and the middle in it is also the disjunctive. And the example of the first and second in the second division of these two divisions: the disjunctive inclusive disjunction: whenever (J is D) then (H is T), and whenever it is either (H is T) or (A is B) then (W is Z) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D then (W is Z) from the third; and the middle in it is the disjunctive. And the example of the third and fourth: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction: whenever (H is T) then (J is D), and whenever it is either (A is B) or (H is T) then (W is Z) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D then (W is Z) from the third, and the middle is also the disjunctive, and the conclusion is exclusive disjunction as well. And each one of them is clarified by reductio ad absurdum (khalf); and that is known.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is an advanced exposition of mixed syllogisms that combine a disjunctive premise (manfasila) with a conditional premise (muttasila). The author is expanding on the rules for such compounds, drawing an analogy with the previously discussed mixed syllogisms of categorical and conditional premises. The text presents several cases based on whether the disjunctive is the minor or major premise, and whether the conditional is the antecedent or consequent of the major. It also considers two types of disjunctions: exclusive (man'at al-khuluw, 'preventing emptiness') and inclusive (man'at al-jam', 'preventing conjunction'). The author first notes that when the disjunctive is part of the compound with a simple conditional, the result depends on the structure, and the middle term is crucial for the necessity of the conclusion. He then states that the ruling for this section is the same as for the categorical-conditional compound, with the categorical replaced by a conditional here, and the number of moods corresponds. Next, he addresses the case where a conditional is part of another conditional (nested conditionals), requiring the innermost conditional to be affirmative universal, and gives two nested conditional conclusions. Then he turns to the disjunctive as minor premise of the conditional, noting that in such cases the distinction between figures (first/third, second/fourth) disappears because the middle term is only in the conditional. He provides examples for the first division (disjunctive minor, conditional major) with both exclusive and inclusive disjunctions, and for the second division (disjunctive major, conditional minor). Finally, he addresses the remaining divisions where the conditional is either the consequent or antecedent of the major disjunctive, and gives examples for the first/second and third/fourth moods, again distinguishing exclusive and inclusive disjunctions. Each example is a complete syllogism yielding a disjunctive conclusion. The author mentions that each such inference can be proved by reductio ad absurdum (khalf).

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the key difference between a mixed disjunctive-conditional syllogism and a mixed categorical-conditional syllogism?
A) The disjunctive replaces the categorical premise with a conditional.
B) The categorical premise is replaced by a conditional, but the figure count remains the same.
C) The middle term is in the disjunctive instead of the conditional.
D) The number of moods per figure is halved.

Key Takeaway
Mixed disjunctive-conditional syllogisms follow the same rules as categorical-conditional ones, with the middle term always in the conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a mixed syllogism on a fiqh topic: use a conditional premise (e.g., 'If the contract is riba-based, it is invalid') and a disjunctive premise (e.g., 'Either it is bay' or it is riba'), then state the conclusion. Identify which disjunction type (exclusive/inclusive) you used.
?
Take an example from the passage: 'Whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (H is T)' and explain how the middle term is only in the conditional part.
