The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Figure Merging When the Disjunctive Is the Minor
Author explains figure indistinguishability when disjunctive is minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first and third figures are not distinguished when the disjunctive premise is the minor in a mixed disjunctive-conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
When the disjunctive is the minor, the middle term is only in the conditional, so the figure is determined solely by the conditional.

The passage addresses a surprising feature: when the disjunctive premise serves as the minor and the conditional as the major, the usual distinction between first and third figures (and between second and fourth) collapses. This is because the figure of a syllogism is defined by the position of the middle term, but here the middle term resides exclusively in the conditional premise, not the disjunctive. The author illustrates this with examples involving both exclusive and inclusive disjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of figure
In a categorical syllogism, the figure is determined by the position of the middle term in the premises (subject or predicate). In these mixed syllogisms, the only relevant premise is the conditional, because the disjunctive contains no middle term.
The middle term is what connects the two premises.
2
Identify the premises in the example
Example: 'Whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (H is T)' (conditional major? Actually here the conditional is the major? Wait, it's disjunctive minor, conditional major). The disjunctive is the minor: 'إما (أب) أو (هـ ط)'.
The order matters: minor first, then major.
3
Locate the middle term
The middle term here is 'H is T'? Actually, in the passage, the middle is the antecedent of the conditional part. In this example, the conditional does not have a clear 'middle term' in the Aristotelian sense because it is a proposition, not a term. But the author treats the whole antecedent and consequent as playing roles. The key is that the disjunctive does not contain the middle.
This is a subtle point: in propositional logic, the 'middle term' is not a term but a propositional component.
4
Understand why figures merge
Since the figure is based only on the conditional, the position of the disjunctive (whether it is the minor or major) does not affect the figure. Thus, the first and third figures (which differ by the position of the middle in the major) become indistinguishable when the conditional is the major? Actually, the author says 'first and third' and 'second and fourth' are not distinguished. This is because when the disjunctive is minor, the only possible figure distinctions come from the conditional, but since the disjunctive has no middle, the conditional's structure is the same irrespective of which figure you would assign in a categorical system. So they collapse.
Read the Arabic: 'فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثالث، والثاني عن الرابع... لأن الاعتبار بحسب وضع الحد الأوسط في المتصلة فقط دون المنفصلة'.

The result of the compound (syllogism) entirely with the simple conditional produces a premise of the universal conditional absolutely not (or: not at all) when (J is D) so (A is B), and whenever absolutely not when (A is B) so (H is T) then (W is Z) produces it may be that when (J is D) so (H is T) then (W is Z). And in this way, if the disjunctive is a part, example of what the result of the compound with the simple conditional produces is the consequent of the negative conditional part: whenever (J) is D so (A is B), and absolutely not when (W is Z) so whenever (J) is D so (H is T) produces absolutely not when (W is Z) so whenever (A is B) so (H is T). Then the middle in what is useful for the necessity of the conclusion is the antecedent of the conditional part. In sum, the ruling of this section is the ruling of the syllogism composed of the categorical and the conditional, except that the partner stands in place of the categorical there, a conditional here. And if it were so, the number of moods of each figure from each section would be the number of moods there; and that is known afterwards. As for the conditional that is a part of the conditional part, it must be affirmative universal: so if you say whenever (J is D) so (H is T), and whenever (W is Z) so whenever (H is T) so (A is B) produces our statement whenever (J is D) so whenever (W is Z) so (A is B) – that is the first conditional – and it also produces whenever (J is D) so (W is Z) so whenever (J) is D so (A is B) – that is the second conditional. As for the disjunctive, if it is the minor of the conditional, then the first figure is not distinguished from the third, nor the second from the fourth, nor the first from the second, nor the third from the fourth if it is reversed; because the consideration is only according to the position of the middle term in the conditional, not in the disjunctive, as you will know in the compound of the conditional and the disjunctive. As for the example of the first and third in the first division: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw): whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (H is T), and whenever (H is T) then (W is Z) produces whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z) due to the impossibility of emptiness then regarding (A is B) and other than it. As for the example of the second and fourth: the disjunctive inclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam'): whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (H is T), and whenever (W is Z) and (H is T) produces whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z). As for the example of the first and third in the second division: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction: whenever it is either (A is B) or (H is T) then (J) is D, and the major as it is produces it may be that when (J) is D then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z) due to what preceded of the impossibility of emptiness. And the example of the second and fourth: whenever (W is Z) then H is T; and the minor as it is, and the disjunctive inclusive disjunction produces it may be that when (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z). As for the result of the first mood of the first and second figures in the other two divisions – I mean those in which the conditional is either the consequent of the major or its antecedent. And the third and fourth in them, also without the syllogism: example of the first and second in the first division of these two divisions: and the disjunctive in it is inclusive disjunction: whenever (J) is D then (H is T), and whenever (W is Z) then either (A is B) or (H is T) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D then (W is Z) – inclusive disjunction – from the third, and the middle is the disjunctive. And the example of the third and fourth: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction: whenever (H is T) then (J is D), and whenever (W is Z) then either (H is T) or (A is B) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D – exclusive disjunction – from the third; and the middle in it is also the disjunctive. And the example of the first and second in the second division of these two divisions: the disjunctive inclusive disjunction: whenever (J is D) then (H is T), and whenever it is either (H is T) or (A is B) then (W is Z) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D then (W is Z) from the third; and the middle in it is the disjunctive. And the example of the third and fourth: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction: whenever (H is T) then (J is D), and whenever it is either (A is B) or (H is T) then (W is Z) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D then (W is Z) from the third, and the middle is also the disjunctive, and the conclusion is exclusive disjunction as well. And each one of them is clarified by reductio ad absurdum (khalf); and that is known.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is an advanced exposition of mixed syllogisms that combine a disjunctive premise (manfasila) with a conditional premise (muttasila). The author is expanding on the rules for such compounds, drawing an analogy with the previously discussed mixed syllogisms of categorical and conditional premises. The text presents several cases based on whether the disjunctive is the minor or major premise, and whether the conditional is the antecedent or consequent of the major. It also considers two types of disjunctions: exclusive (man'at al-khuluw, 'preventing emptiness') and inclusive (man'at al-jam', 'preventing conjunction'). The author first notes that when the disjunctive is part of the compound with a simple conditional, the result depends on the structure, and the middle term is crucial for the necessity of the conclusion. He then states that the ruling for this section is the same as for the categorical-conditional compound, with the categorical replaced by a conditional here, and the number of moods corresponds. Next, he addresses the case where a conditional is part of another conditional (nested conditionals), requiring the innermost conditional to be affirmative universal, and gives two nested conditional conclusions. Then he turns to the disjunctive as minor premise of the conditional, noting that in such cases the distinction between figures (first/third, second/fourth) disappears because the middle term is only in the conditional. He provides examples for the first division (disjunctive minor, conditional major) with both exclusive and inclusive disjunctions, and for the second division (disjunctive major, conditional minor). Finally, he addresses the remaining divisions where the conditional is either the consequent or antecedent of the major disjunctive, and gives examples for the first/second and third/fourth moods, again distinguishing exclusive and inclusive disjunctions. Each example is a complete syllogism yielding a disjunctive conclusion. The author mentions that each such inference can be proved by reductio ad absurdum (khalf).

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Exclusive vs Inclusive Disjunctions in Mixed Syllogisms
Author contrasts exclusive and inclusive disjunctions in examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between exclusive (مانعة الخلو) and inclusive (مانعة الجمع) disjunctions in mixed syllogisms and predict the form of the conclusion.

Core Idea
The type of disjunction (exclusive or inclusive) determines whether the conclusion excludes both false or both true.

The passage repeatedly pairs examples with two types of disjunctions: exclusive (مانعة الخلو) which prevents both disjuncts from being false, and inclusive (مانعة الجمع) which prevents both from being true. In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion mirrors the disjunction type of the premise. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the logical force of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definitions of exclusive and inclusive
Exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو): exactly one disjunct is true (not both, not none). Inclusive disjunction (مانعة الجمع): at least one is false (not both true, but possibly both false? Actually, inclusive means at most one true, so both can be false. In Arabic logic, man'at al-jam' prevents both being true, but allows both false. Man'at al-khuluw prevents both being false, so at least one true.
These are semantic definitions; they affect the conclusions that can be drawn.
2
Identify the disjunction type in the example
Look for the phrase 'مانعة الخلو' (exclusive) or 'مانعة الجمع' (inclusive) in the passage. For instance, the first example after the figure merging section uses exclusive.
The author states 'والمنفصلة مانعة الخلو' or 'والمنفصلة مانعة الجمع'.
3
Compare the conclusion's form
In the exclusive examples, the conclusion also uses 'إما... أو' (either/or) with the same exclusive force. In the inclusive examples, the conclusion is also inclusive. For example, with 'إما (أب) أو (هـ ط)' as an exclusive disjunction, the conclusion is 'إما (أب) أو (وز)' exclusive.
The type is preserved through the inference.
4
Check the justification in the passage
When the disjunction is exclusive, the author says the conclusion is due to 'امتناع الخلو' (impossibility of emptiness). When inclusive, the reasoning is implicit but the form remains.
This shows that the inference relies on the semantic property of the disjunction.

The result of the compound (syllogism) entirely with the simple conditional produces a premise of the universal conditional absolutely not (or: not at all) when (J is D) so (A is B), and whenever absolutely not when (A is B) so (H is T) then (W is Z) produces it may be that when (J is D) so (H is T) then (W is Z). And in this way, if the disjunctive is a part, example of what the result of the compound with the simple conditional produces is the consequent of the negative conditional part: whenever (J) is D so (A is B), and absolutely not when (W is Z) so whenever (J) is D so (H is T) produces absolutely not when (W is Z) so whenever (A is B) so (H is T). Then the middle in what is useful for the necessity of the conclusion is the antecedent of the conditional part. In sum, the ruling of this section is the ruling of the syllogism composed of the categorical and the conditional, except that the partner stands in place of the categorical there, a conditional here. And if it were so, the number of moods of each figure from each section would be the number of moods there; and that is known afterwards. As for the conditional that is a part of the conditional part, it must be affirmative universal: so if you say whenever (J is D) so (H is T), and whenever (W is Z) so whenever (H is T) so (A is B) produces our statement whenever (J is D) so whenever (W is Z) so (A is B) – that is the first conditional – and it also produces whenever (J is D) so (W is Z) so whenever (J) is D so (A is B) – that is the second conditional. As for the disjunctive, if it is the minor of the conditional, then the first figure is not distinguished from the third, nor the second from the fourth, nor the first from the second, nor the third from the fourth if it is reversed; because the consideration is only according to the position of the middle term in the conditional, not in the disjunctive, as you will know in the compound of the conditional and the disjunctive. As for the example of the first and third in the first division: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw): whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (H is T), and whenever (H is T) then (W is Z) produces whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z) due to the impossibility of emptiness then regarding (A is B) and other than it. As for the example of the second and fourth: the disjunctive inclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam'): whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (H is T), and whenever (W is Z) and (H is T) produces whenever (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z). As for the example of the first and third in the second division: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction: whenever it is either (A is B) or (H is T) then (J) is D, and the major as it is produces it may be that when (J) is D then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z) due to what preceded of the impossibility of emptiness. And the example of the second and fourth: whenever (W is Z) then H is T; and the minor as it is, and the disjunctive inclusive disjunction produces it may be that when (J is D) then it is either (A is B) or (W is Z). As for the result of the first mood of the first and second figures in the other two divisions – I mean those in which the conditional is either the consequent of the major or its antecedent. And the third and fourth in them, also without the syllogism: example of the first and second in the first division of these two divisions: and the disjunctive in it is inclusive disjunction: whenever (J) is D then (H is T), and whenever (W is Z) then either (A is B) or (H is T) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D then (W is Z) – inclusive disjunction – from the third, and the middle is the disjunctive. And the example of the third and fourth: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction: whenever (H is T) then (J is D), and whenever (W is Z) then either (H is T) or (A is B) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D – exclusive disjunction – from the third; and the middle in it is also the disjunctive. And the example of the first and second in the second division of these two divisions: the disjunctive inclusive disjunction: whenever (J is D) then (H is T), and whenever it is either (H is T) or (A is B) then (W is Z) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D then (W is Z) from the third; and the middle in it is the disjunctive. And the example of the third and fourth: the disjunctive exclusive disjunction: whenever (H is T) then (J is D), and whenever it is either (A is B) or (H is T) then (W is Z) produces it may be that if it is either (A is B) or (J) is D then (W is Z) from the third, and the middle is also the disjunctive, and the conclusion is exclusive disjunction as well. And each one of them is clarified by reductio ad absurdum (khalf); and that is known.

Commentary
This passage is an advanced exposition of mixed syllogisms that combine a disjunctive premise (manfasila) with a conditional premise (muttasila). The author is expanding on the rules for such compounds, drawing an analogy with the previously discussed mixed syllogisms of categorical and conditional premises. The text presents several cases based on whether the disjunctive is the minor or major premise, and whether the conditional is the antecedent or consequent of the major. It also considers two types of disjunctions: exclusive (man'at al-khuluw, 'preventing emptiness') and inclusive (man'at al-jam', 'preventing conjunction'). The author first notes that when the disjunctive is part of the compound with a simple conditional, the result depends on the structure, and the middle term is crucial for the necessity of the conclusion. He then states that the ruling for this section is the same as for the categorical-conditional compound, with the categorical replaced by a conditional here, and the number of moods corresponds. Next, he addresses the case where a conditional is part of another conditional (nested conditionals), requiring the innermost conditional to be affirmative universal, and gives two nested conditional conclusions. Then he turns to the disjunctive as minor premise of the conditional, noting that in such cases the distinction between figures (first/third, second/fourth) disappears because the middle term is only in the conditional. He provides examples for the first division (disjunctive minor, conditional major) with both exclusive and inclusive disjunctions, and for the second division (disjunctive major, conditional minor). Finally, he addresses the remaining divisions where the conditional is either the consequent or antecedent of the major disjunctive, and gives examples for the first/second and third/fourth moods, again distinguishing exclusive and inclusive disjunctions. The Syllogism

Examples of Compound Syllogisms with Mixed Premises
Author enumerates examples of compound syllogisms combining disjunctive and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the configuration of disjunctive and conditional premises in a compound syllogism and verify the conclusion using the proof technique of khalf (reductio ad absurdum).

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms combine disjunctive and conditional premises; their validity is proven through khalf.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates examples of compound syllogisms that mix disjunctive and conditional propositions. These examples cover various combinations of exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) disjunctives with conditionals in different positions. Understanding these configurations is essential for constructing complex proofs in Islamic logic. We will walk through one example in detail to see how the conclusion is derived.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunctive premise
Determine whether the disjunctive is exclusive (mani'at al-jam') or exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). Look for the wording: 'mani'at al-jam' means both cannot be true together; 'mani'at al-khuluw' means both cannot be false together.
The type affects how the conclusion is derived.
2
Extract the premises
Identify the conditional (muttasila) and the disjunctive (munfasila). Note which is the minor and which is the major premise, and the position of the conditional (antecedent or consequent of the disjunctive).
3
Determine the figure and mood
The passage specifies figures (first, second, third, fourth) and moods. Identify which figure and mood the example belongs to based on the arrangement of terms.
This classification helps in applying systematic rules.
4
Derive the conclusion
Using the rules of compound syllogisms, infer the conclusion as stated in the text. For example, with an exclusive disjunctive, if the conditional is affirmed, the negation of one disjunct yields the other.
The conclusion is often a conditional itself.
5
Verify by khalf
Understand that each compound syllogism can be proven by assuming the opposite of the conclusion and deriving a contradiction. This is the standard method of proof in Islamic logic.
Khalf is a powerful technique to establish validity.

(Example of) the second and fourth; and the disjunctive is exclusive (mani'at al-jam'): whenever 'J D' (is true), then either 'A B' or 'H T', and whenever 'W Z' and 'H T' (are true), it yields: whenever 'J D' (is true), then either 'A B' or 'W Z'. As for the example of the first and third in the second division; and the disjunctive is exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw): whenever either 'A B' or 'H T', then 'J D', and the major premise remains as it is, it yields: it may be that if 'J D' (is true) then either 'A B' or 'W Z' due to the impossibility of emptiness as mentioned. And the example of the second and fourth: whenever 'W Z' then 'H T'; and the minor premise remains as it is, and the disjunctive is exclusive, it yields: it may be that if 'J D' (is true) then either 'A B' or 'W Z'. As for the result of the first mood of the first and second figures in the other two sections, namely those where the conditional is the consequent of the major or its antecedent, and the third and fourth (figures) in them are also not part of the syllogism: example of the first and second in the first section of these two sections: the disjunctive therein is exclusive: whenever 'J D' then 'H T', and whenever 'W Z' then either 'A B' or 'H T', yields: it may be that if either 'A B' or 'J D' then 'W Z'—exclusive from the third and the middle is the disjunctive. And the example of the third and fourth: the disjunctive is exhaustive: whenever 'H T' then 'J D', and whenever 'W Z' then either 'H T' or 'A B', yields: it may be that if either 'A B' or 'J D' then 'W Z'—exhaustive from the third, and the middle is also the disjunctive. And the example of the first and second in the second section of these two sections: the disjunctive is exclusive: whenever 'J D' then 'H T', and whenever either 'H T' or 'A B' then 'W Z', yields: it may be that if either 'A B' or 'J D' then 'W Z' from the third, and the middle is the disjunctive. And the example of the third and fourth: the disjunctive is exhaustive: whenever 'H T' then 'J D', and whenever either 'A B' or 'H T' then 'W Z', yields: it may be that if either 'A B' or 'J D' then 'W Z' from the third, and the middle is also the disjunctive, and the result is also exhaustive. Each one of them is verified by reduction to absurdity (khalf); that is known by reflection. As for the relation of the parts of the minor and major premises in the syllogism to each other, and the benefit of each one by itself or with another, that is among the matters that are not hidden after acquaintance with the reality of each one; so take heed of what you have learned, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of examples of compound syllogisms that combine disjunctive and conditional premises in various figures and moods. The author systematically works through the possibilities, showing how the conclusion is derived from the premises. The examples are organized by the type of disjunctive (exclusive, exhaustive) and the position of the conditional relative to the disjunctive. The passage emphasizes that each of these compound syllogisms can be verified by the proof technique of 'khalf' (reductio ad absurdum). The author concludes by noting that once the nature of each part is understood, their relations and utility become evident.

Key Terms
منفصلة مانعة الجمع (munfasila mani'at al-jam'): A disjunctive proposition that excludes the combination of the two disjuncts (i.e., 'exclusive or'), meaning both cannot be true together.
منفصلة مانعة الخلو (munfasila mani'at al-khuluw): A disjunctive proposition that excludes the emptiness of both disjuncts (i.e., 'exhaustive or'), meaning they cannot both be false together.
متصلة (muttasila): A conditional (implicational) proposition, typically of the form 'if P then Q'.
خلف (khalf): A proof technique: assuming the opposite of the desired conclusion and deriving a contradiction, thereby establishing the original conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It may be that if J D then either A B or W Z
C) It may be that if W Z then J D
D) It may be that if H T then J D

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms that mix conditionals and disjunctives can be verified by the method of khalf (reductio ad absurdum).

The Six Types of Disjunctive-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates six types of syllogisms from two disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six types of syllogisms composed of two disjunctives and determine the type based on the exclusive and exhaustive properties of each premise.

Core Idea
Six types emerge from the combination of exclusive/exhaustive properties in both premises.

In this passage, the author categorizes syllogisms made of two disjunctives. He identifies six distinct kinds based on whether each disjunction is both exclusive and exhaustive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), exclusive only, exhaustive only, or a mixture. Understanding these types is crucial for analyzing compound syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjunctive premises
In any such syllogism, both the major and minor are disjunctive propositions. Note that they may share a complete part (an entire disjunct) or an incomplete part (a term within a disjunct).
2
Classify each premise by its exclusive/exhaustive property
Determine if each premise is exclusive (cannot have both true), exhaustive (cannot have both false), or both. The Arabic terms are: mani'at al-jam' (exclusive) and mani'at al-khuluw (exhaustive).
3
Count the six combinations
The text lists six: (1) both premises are exclusive and exhaustive, (2) both are exclusive only, (3) both are exhaustive only, (4) one is both, the other exclusive only, (5) one is both, the other exhaustive only, (6) one is exclusive only, the other exhaustive only.
4
Apply in an Islamic reasoning context
For example, in fiqh, consider a disjunctive syllogism: 'Either this transaction is halal or haram (exclusive/exhaustive). Either it is a sale or a gift (exclusive/exhaustive).' This is type 1. Recognizing the type helps verify validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني من الأقسام الخمسة فيما يتألف من منفصلتين، وإنه على أقسام كذلك بحسب التركيب من القضايا المنفصلة، والاشتراك في جزء تام وغير تام؛ فإنه يمكن أن تكون كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى مشتملة على جزء تام وعلى جزء غير تام، أو كانت إحديهما مشتملة على جزء تام والأخرى على جزء غير تام. والقسم الأول منها على ستة أقسام لأن كل واحد منهما [١] إما أن يكون مانعة الجمع والخلو، [۲] أو مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، [٣] أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، [٤] أو إحديهما مانعة الجمع والخلو، والأخرى مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، [٥] أو إحديهما مانعة الجمع والخلو، والأخرى مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، [٦] أو إحديهما مانعة الجمع، والأخرى مانعة الخلو. والأقسام كلها مشتركة في اشتراك كلية إحدى مقدميه وموجبيتها أيضًا. وقد مرّ من قبل أن أجزاء المنفصلة لا يتميز بعضها عن البعض بالطبع بل بالوضع. ولو كان كذلك فلا يتميز الشكل من الشكل ولا الصغرى من الكبرى ولا أحد طرفي النتيجة عن الطرف الآخر إلا بالوضع.
The second division of the five divisions is concerning what is composed of two disjunctives, and it too has divisions according to the composition from disjunctive propositions, and the sharing of a complete part and an incomplete part. For it is possible that each of the minor and major (premises) includes a complete part and an incomplete part, or one of them includes a complete part and the other an incomplete part. And the first type among them is of six kinds, because each one of them [1] is either exclusive and exhaustive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), [2] or exclusive without exhaustive, [3] or exhaustive without exclusive, [4] or one of them is exclusive and exhaustive and the other is exclusive without exhaustive, [5] or one of them is exclusive and exhaustive and the other is exhaustive without exclusive, [6] or one of them is exclusive and the other is exhaustive. And all of these kinds share in that each premise is universal and affirmative. And it has passed before that the parts of the disjunctive are not distinguished from each other by nature but by stipulation. And if that is the case, then no figure is distinguished from another, nor the minor from the major, nor one extremity of the conclusion from the other, except by stipulation.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major category of compound syllogisms: those composed of two disjunctive premises. The author explains that these are further subdivided based on whether the premises share a complete part or an incomplete part. The first type within this division has six sub-types, defined by the nature of the two disjunctives: whether each is exclusive, exhaustive, both, or a mix. The author also notes that all of these require universal affirmative premises. A key observation is that in disjunctive premises, the parts are not logically distinguished but are distinguished by the logician's stipulation (wad'): this means that in such syllogisms, identification of figures, minor/major premises, and conclusion endpoints depends on how we choose to assign them, not on inherent nature.

مانعة الجمع دون الخلو
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع

Key Takeaway

The Role of Stipulation (Wad') in Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author discusses the role of stipulation in disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the parts of a disjunctive premise are distinguished by stipulation (wad') and how this affects the identification of figures, premises, and conclusions.

Core Idea
In disjunctive syllogisms, logical structure depends on the logician's stipulation, not nature.

The author makes a critical observation: in disjunctive premises, the parts are not inherently distinct; they are distinguished by the logician's stipulation (bil-wad'). This has profound consequences: without stipulation, we cannot distinguish figures, minor from major premises, or even the terms of the conclusion. This lesson explores that insight.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: parts are by stipulation
The passage recalls that earlier it was established that the components of a disjunctive proposition are not naturally ordered; they are labeled by the logician. For example, in 'Either A or B', A and B are not inherently first or second.
2
See the consequence: if parts are by stipulation, then so are figures
Since figures depend on the ordering of terms, and ordering is arbitrary, the figure of a disjunctive syllogism is not fixed by nature.
3
Extend to premises and conclusion
Similarly, which premise is minor and which is major, and which term is the subject or predicate of the conclusion, are all matters of stipulation.
4
Apply to an Islamic logical discussion
In a debate about whether an act is obligatory or recommended, the disjunctive 'Either it is obligatory or recommended' has parts that can be swapped. The logician must stipulate which part is the first disjunct to form a valid inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Non-Productivity of Syllogisms with Two True Disjunctives
Author states that when both premises are true disjunctives, the syllogism is non-productive according to the majority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a syllogism composed of two true disjunctives (exclusive and exhaustive) is non-productive according to the majority of logicians.

Core Idea
Two true disjunctives yield no conclusion because they lack a shared middle term.

In compound syllogisms, not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. This lesson examines a critical case: when both premises are true disjunctives (haqiqiyya), i.e., exclusive and exhaustive. According to the majority, including Avicenna, such a syllogism is non-productive. Why? Because the structure of a true disjunctive leaves no logical room for a middle term to connect the two premises.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of a true disjunctive (haqiqiyya)
A true disjunctive is both exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). Exactly one disjunct is true, and no third option exists. This is a complete partition of possibilities.
The Arabic term 'haqiqiyya' here specifically means the perfect disjunctive, not 'categorical'.
2
Consider the structure of a syllogism with two true disjunctives
Take two premises: 'Either A or B' and 'Either C or D'. These premises share no common term. In a valid syllogism, a middle term must appear in both premises to connect them.
Even if the disjuncts were related, the true disjunctive's exhaustiveness prevents any overlap.
3
Identify why no middle term exists
Since each premise exhausts its own partition, the two premises are logically isolated. There is no term that appears in both to serve as a middle. Hence, no conclusion can be deduced from the combination alone.
This is the key reason for non-productivity: absence of a middle term.
4
Understand the authoritative stance
The passage states that the majority (al-jumhur) and Avicenna (al-Shaykh) hold this view, citing al-Shifa'. This is a settled classical position, though some later logicians may have nuanced it.
The argument from al-Shifa' is incomplete in the source, but the claim is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القسم الأول وهو الذي تكون كل واحدة من مقدمتيه حقيقية: إنه غير منتج عند الجمهور والشيخ من جملتهم فإنه ذكر في الشفاء أنك إذا قلت لا يخلو إما أن يكون (أ ب) وإما أن يكون (ج) د فهذا القول إنما يكون صدقا إذا لم يكن قسم ثالث لأن معنى قولنا لا يخلو إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن يكون
Then the first type, which is that each of its two premises is a true (disjunctive): it is non-productive according to the majority (al-jumhur) and the Shaykh (Avicenna) among them, for he mentioned in al-Shifa' that if you say 'it cannot be that either (A B) or (J D) is not the case?' actually the phrase is incomplete. The meaning of our saying 'it cannot be that either (A B) or (J D) is not the case' is that the statement is only true if there is no third division, because the meaning of 'it cannot be' is that either 'A B' or... (text cuts off).
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a critical claim: when both premises are 'true' disjunctives (i.e., exclusive and exhaustive), the syllogism does not produce a conclusion. The author cites Avicenna's al-Shifa' as the source of this view. The argument begins by examining the meaning of a true disjunctive: it claims that the only way such a proposition can be true is if there are exactly two alternatives (no third possibility). The text then starts to explain the meaning but cuts off. This is a key point in the theory of compound syllogisms, as it limits the applicability of certain forms. The majority (jumhur) of logicians accept this non-productivity.

Key Terms
غير منتج (ghayr muntij): Non-productive; meaning that no valid conclusion follows from the premises.
حقيقية (haqiqiyya): True disjunctive: a disjunctive proposition that is both exclusive and exhaustive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw). [Note: In some contexts, 'haqiqiyya' can mean 'categorical' or 'real', but here it specifically refers to the perfect disjunctive.]
الشفاء (al-Shifa'): The book 'al-Shifa' by Avicenna, a major source for logical and philosophical doctrines.

Key Takeaway
When both premises are true (exclusive and exhaustive) disjunctives, the syllogism is non-productive because no middle term can connect them.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Six Types of Compound Disjunctive Syllogisms
Classification of compound disjunctive syllogisms into six types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and distinguish the six types of compound disjunctive syllogisms based on the nature of their disjunctive premises.

Core Idea
Six types arise from combining three disjunction kinds in two premises.

This lesson unpacks the second division of syllogisms—those composed of two disjunctive premises. The author explains that the parts of a disjunction are distinguished only by position, not by nature, and then enumerates six specific types based on the kinds of disjunctions used. Understanding these types prepares you to evaluate the validity of disjunctive reasoning in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three fundamental disjunction kinds
Recognize the three types of disjunctive propositions: true disjunction (both cannot be true nor false), preventing conjunction only (cannot both be true but can both be false), and preventing disjunction only (cannot both be false but can both be true).
These are the building blocks for the six types.
2
Understand the composition of the syllogism
A compound disjunctive syllogism has two premises (minor and major). Each premise can be any of the three disjunction types. The author lists six combinations: three where both premises are the same type, and three where the two premises are different (unordered pairs).
The number six comes from 3 identical pairs + 3 mixed pairs (order of premises not distinguished in the classification).
3
Enumerate the six specific types
These are: (1) both true disjunctions; (2) both preventing conjunction; (3) both preventing disjunction; (4) one true, one preventing conjunction; (5) one true, one preventing disjunction; (6) one preventing conjunction and the other preventing disjunction.
The text lists these as 'the first of these [divisions] has six divisions'.
4
Note the common feature and positional nature
In all six types, at least one premise is universal and affirmative. Also, the parts of a disjunction are not distinguished by nature but by their position, which affects how terms are identified in the syllogism.
This underscores the formal nature of logical reasoning.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider a fiqh argument: 'Either this liquid is wine (najis) or it is vinegar (tahir). It is not wine. Therefore it is vinegar.' Identify which type this represents if the first premise is a true disjunction (the two are mutually exclusive and exhaustive).
Valid reasoning depends on the correct disjunction type.

القسم الثاني من الأقسام الخمسة فيما يتألف من منفصلتين، وإنه على أقسام كذلك بحسب التركيب من القضايا المنفصلة، والاشتراك في جزء تام وغير تام؛ فإنه يمكن أن تكون كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى مشتملة على جزء تام وعلى جزء غير تام، أو كانت إحديهما مشتملة على جزء تام والأخرى على جزء غير تام. والقسم الأول منها على ستة أقسام: ١- أن تكون كل واحدة من مقدمتيه مانعة الجمع والخلو، ٢- أو مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، ٣- أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، ٤- أو إحديهما مانعة الجمع والخلو والأخرى مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، ٥- أو إحديهما مانعة الجمع والخلو والأخرى مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، ٦- أو إحديهما مانعة الجمع والأخرى مانعة الخلو. والأقسام كلها مشتركة في اشتراك كلية إحدى مقدميه وموجبيتها أيضًا. وقد مرّ من قبل أن أجزاء المنفصلة لا يتميز بعضها عن البعض بالطبع بل بالوضع. ولو كان كذلك فلا يتميز الشكل من الشكل ولا الصغرى من الكبرى ولا أحد طرفي النتيجة عن الطرف الآخر إلا بالوضع.
The second division of the five divisions concerns what is composed of two disjunctives. It too has divisions according to the composition from disjunctive propositions, and the sharing in a complete part and an incomplete part. For it is possible that each of the minor and major [premises] includes a complete part and an incomplete part, or one of them includes a complete part and the other an incomplete part. The first of these [divisions] has six divisions: 1- that each of its two premises is a true disjunction, 2- or a disjunction preventing conjunction without preventing disjunction, 3- or a disjunction preventing disjunction without preventing conjunction, 4- or one of them is a true disjunction and the other a disjunction preventing conjunction without preventing disjunction, 5- or one of them is a true disjunction and the other a disjunction preventing disjunction without preventing conjunction, 6- or one of them is a disjunction preventing conjunction and the other a disjunction preventing disjunction. All the divisions share in the universality and affirmation of one of their premises. It has passed before that the parts of a disjunction are not distinguished from each other by nature but by position. If that is so, then the figure is not distinguished from the figure, nor the minor from the major, nor one end of the conclusion from the other end, except by position.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage defines the second division of the five arts (the syllogism) as that composed of two disjunctive premises. The author explains that the composition can vary based on whether each premise (minor and major) contains a complete part and an incomplete part. This refers to the fact that disjunctive premises can themselves be compound or simple. Then the author enumerates six specific types of this compound disjunctive syllogism, based on the nature of the two disjunctions: they can be true disjunctions (preventing both conjunction and disjunction, i.e., both alternatives cannot be true together nor both false), or one-sided disjunctions (preventing conjunction but not disjunction, i.e., they cannot both be true but can both be false; or preventing disjunction but not conjunction, i.e., they cannot both be false but can both be true), or a mix. The six types are the permutations of these three types for the two premises. The author then notes a common feature: in all these types, at least one premise is universal and affirmative. Finally, a crucial point is reiterated: the parts of a disjunctive proposition are not distinguished by their logical nature but by the position they occupy in the statement. Consequently, in a syllogism, the figure, the minor premise, the major premise, and the terms of the conclusion are identified only by their position, not by inherent characteristics. This emphasizes the formal nature of syllogistic reasoning: the role of a term depends on its placement, not its content. For example, in a disjunctive syllogism about a liquid being either wine or grape juice, the two alternatives are distinguished positionally, not by some innate quality.

Key Takeaway
Compound disjunctive syllogisms come in six types based on the nature of the disjunctions, and the role of terms is determined by position, not intrinsic meaning.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Sina's Objection: Why Two True Disjunctions Yield No Conclusion
Author presents Ibn Sina's argument for non-productivity of disjunctive compound syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Sina's argument that a syllogism composed of two true disjunctions is non-productive, by analyzing the conditions under which the premises can be simultaneously true.

Core Idea
Two true disjunctions sharing a term either collapse or introduce a third alternative — neither yields a valid syllogism.

In this passage, the author presents the majority view, led by Ibn Sina, that a syllogism with two true disjunctions (مانعة الجمع والخلو) produces no necessary conclusion. This is a surprising claim, since disjunctions seem to provide clear alternatives. Ibn Sina's reasoning from the Shifā' demonstrates that the structure forces either a collapse of the premises or an additional third option, making proper syllogistic inference impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two true disjunctions
Each premise is a true disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya), meaning exactly one of its two alternatives must be true. They share a common middle term (here 'C D' in the variables).
The example uses abstract letters: 'لا يخلو إما أن يكون (أ ب) أو (ج د)' and 'لا يخلو إما أن يكون (ج د) أو (هـ د)'.
2
Consider the case where the middle term is true
If 'C D' holds, then both disjunctions are satisfied solely by that truth; they become identical in content—a single proposition. No inference can be drawn from one premise to another.
3
Consider the case where the middle term is false
If 'C D' is false, then to maintain the truth of the first disjunction, 'A B' must be true; and to maintain the truth of the second, 'H D' must be true. But this implies that 'A B' and 'H D' can both be true, which violates the exclusivity requirement of the true disjunction (which demands exactly one). Moreover, the premises allow a third alternative (something other than 'C D'), making them non-exhaustive and thus false as true disjunctions.
Ibn Sina states: 'إذا لم يكن قسم ثالث... فإن كان قد لا يكون ج د فالقضية كاذبة'.
4
Conclude non-productivity
Since in both scenarios the premises fail to produce a valid syllogism, the majority deems this form غير منتج (non-productive).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القسم الأول وهو الذي تكون كل واحدة من مقدمتيه حقيقية: إنه غير منتج عند الجمهور والشيخ من جملتهم فإنه ذكر في الشفاء أنك إذا قلت لا يخلو إما أن يكون (أ ب) وإما أن يكون (ج د) فهذا القول إنما يكون صدقا إذا لم يكن قسم ثالث لأن معنى قولنا لا يخلو إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن يكون ج د هو أنه إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن لا يكون كان لا محالة جد، فإن كان قد لا يكون ج د فالقضية كاذبة. اللهم إلا أن تجعل جزئية وحينئذ لا يكون لها قسم ثالث وبالجملة فكلمة إما كما يدل على العناد بين الشيئين فكذلك يدل على أن لا يخلو عن أحدهما فلو قلت دائما إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن يكون ج د، ودائمًا إما أن يكون (ج) د وإما أن يكون «هـ د» فـ «وز»، إن كان هو «أب» فالقضيتان هما قضية واحدة؛ ولا مجال للإنتاج حينئذ. وإن كان غير «أب» فههنا قسم ثالث؛ والقضيتان كاذبتان حينئذ. وعند قوم من المتقدمين إذا كانت مقدمتاه حقيقيتين وهما موجبتان كليتان ينتج متّصلة كلية من الطرفين لاستلزام كل واحد من الطرفين نقيض الأوسط استلزاما كليًّا، واستلزام نقيض الأوسط للطرف الآخر. وينتج أيضًا منفصلة سالبة حقيقية وغير حقيقية كما في قولنا دائمًا إما أن يكون «أب» أو «ج د»، ودائما إما أن يكون (ج) د» أو «هز» ينتج دائما إما ليس «أب» أو «هز» مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلوّ ومانعة الجمع والخلو، ودائمًا إما ليس «هز» أو «أب» مانعة الجمع والخلو ومانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو لما أن الطرفين متساويان. وذلك لأن «أب» مستلزم لعدم جد المستلزم لوجود «هز» وهما مستلزمان استلزام «أب» له «ز» . وكذلك «هز» مستلزم لعدم ج د المستلزم لا «ب»؛ وهما مستلزمان استلزام «هـ ز » لا «ب»؛ فظهر أن الطرفين متساويان. والنتائج المذكورة من لوازم التساوي: إما استلزام «أب» له «ز» فإنه يستلزم امتناع الخلو بين عدم «أب» و «هـز» و امتناع الجمع بين عدم «هز» و «أب». وإما استلزام «هــز» ولا «ب» فإنه يستلزم امتناع الخلو بين عدم «هــز» و «أب» وامتناع الجمع بين هـز وعدم «أب». ولما صدق كل واحدة من مانعتي الجمع
Then the first type, which is that each of its two premises is true [disjunction]: It is non-productive according to the majority and the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] among them. For he mentioned in the Shifa’: If you say ‘It cannot be but either it is (A B) or it is (C D)’, this statement is only true if there is no third division, because the meaning of our saying ‘It cannot be but either it is ‘A B’ or it is ‘C D’’ is that either it is ‘A B’ or it is not, it would necessarily be ‘C D’? [unclear]. If it is possible that it is not ‘C D’, then the proposition is false. Unless you make it particular, and then it has no third division. In general, the word ‘either’ indicates mutual exclusivity and also indicates that you cannot be free from one of them. So if you say always: either it is ‘A B’ or it is ‘C D’, and always: either it is ‘C D’ or it is ‘H D’, then ‘W’? If it is ‘A B’, then the two propositions are one proposition, and there is no room for production then. And if it is other than ‘A B’, then there is a third division, and the two propositions are then false. According to some of the earlier [scholars], if both its premises are true [disjunctions] and they are universal affirmative, it produces a universal categorical from the two sides, because each of the two sides necessarily implies the contradictory of the middle, and the contradictory of the middle necessarily implies the other side. And it also produces a negative true disjunction and non-true [disjunction], as in our saying: always either it is ‘A B’ or ‘C D’, and always either it is ‘C D’ or ‘H Z’, it produces: always it is either not ‘A B’ or ‘H Z’ – a preventing conjunction, a preventing emptiness, and a preventing conjunction and emptiness – and always either not ‘H Z’ or ‘A B’ – a preventing conjunction and emptiness, a preventing conjunction, and a preventing emptiness – because the two sides are equivalent. That is because ‘A B’ implies the non-existence of ‘C D’, which implies the existence of ‘H Z’, and they both imply the implication of ‘A B’ for ‘Z’. Likewise, ‘H Z’ implies the non-existence of ‘C D’, which implies the non-existence of ‘B’? and they both imply the implication of ‘H Z’ for the non-existence of ‘B’. So it appears that the two sides are equivalent. The mentioned results are from the requirements of equivalence: either the implication of ‘A B’ for ‘Z’ – for it necessitates the impossibility of emptiness between the non-existence of ‘A B’ and ‘H Z’, and the impossibility of conjunction between the non-existence of ‘H Z’ and ‘A B’. Or the implication of ‘H Z’ for the non-existence of ‘B’ – for it necessitates the impossibility of emptiness between the non-existence of ‘H Z’ and ‘A B’, and the impossibility of conjunction between ‘H Z’ and the non-existence of ‘A B’. And when each of the two preventing conjunctions is true... [text cuts off]
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage addresses the productivity of the first type of compound disjunctive syllogism, where both premises are true disjunctions (man‘at al-jam‘ wa al-khulū). The author presents two opposing views. First, the majority, including Ibn Sina, holds that such a syllogism yields no conclusion. Ibn Sina's argument from the Shifa’ is cited: if you have a true disjunction like 'Either A is B or C is D', then for the statement to be true, there must be no third alternative. The term 'either' (immā) implies both mutual exclusivity and that one of the two must hold. Now consider two such disjunctions sharing a common term: 'Either A is B or C is D' and 'Either C is D or H is D'. If the common term 'C is D' is true, then both disjunctions collapse into a single proposition; if it is false, then there is a third alternative (something besides C D), making one or both premises false. Hence, no valid syllogism arises. The author then presents an alternative view from some earlier scholars who claim that such a syllogism is productive. They argue that from two universal affirmative true disjunctions, a universal categorical conclusion can be drawn, linking the two extremes through the implication of each side for the contradictory of the middle. Furthermore, it produces a negative disjunction, either true or non-true. The author illustrates with an example using variables: from 'Always either A B or C D' and 'Always either C D or H Z', one can conclude 'Always either not A B or H Z' in all three forms of disjunction (preventing conjunction, preventing emptiness, and both). The proof relies on the equivalence of the two extremes after a series of implications: A B implies not C D, which implies H Z, so A B implies H Z. Conversely, H Z implies not C D, which implies not A B, so H Z implies not A B. Thus the extremes are equivalent; the resulting disjunctions reflect that equivalence. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is essentially complete. This debate highlights the complexity of disjunctive syllogisms: while the majority rejects their productivity under strict conditions, some early logicians found them valid through additional implications. For example, in Islamic law, one might say 'Either this liquid is wine or it is grape juice' and 'Either it is grape juice or it is vinegar'; here the common term 'grape juice' leads to the same issue Ibn Sina diagnosed—either the proposition collapses or a third alternative (vinegar) appears, making the premises false if the liquid is wine. The alternative view, however, would attempt to salvage a conclusion through implications about the nature of the substances.

غير منتج

Why does Ibn Sina argue that a syllogism with two true disjunctions (e.g., 'Either A or B' and 'Either B or C') is non-productive?
A) Because the premises are both negative, violating a rule of syllogism.
B) Because the middle term 'B' is not distributed in either premise.
C) Because the two premises either collapse into one proposition or introduce a third alternative, making one or both false.
D) Because the conclusion would be a tautology, which is not allowed.

Key Takeaway
A true disjunction demands both exclusivity and exhaustiveness; when two such disjunctions share a term, they cannot support a valid syllogism because the premises either collapse or introduce a third alternative.

The Early Logicians' Reconstruction: Valid Conclusions from Two True Disjunctions
Author presents an alternative view from earlier logicians that claims productivity from two universal true disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusions that some early logicians claimed follow from two universal affirmative true disjunctions, and explain the role of equivalence between the extremes in generating multiple disjunctive conclusions.

Core Idea
If both disjunctions are universal affirmative, the extremes are equivalent and yield a rich set of valid disjunctive conclusions.

Not all logicians agreed with Ibn Sina. Some earlier scholars argued that a compound disjunctive syllogism with two true disjunctions can be productive, provided both premises are universal affirmative. Through a chain of implications, the two extremes (the non-common terms) become equivalent, generating multiple valid conclusions. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the resulting disjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universal true disjunctions
The premises are: 'دائمًا إما أن يكون (أ ب) أو (ج د)' and 'دائمًا إما أن يكون (ج د) أو (هـ ز)'. Both are universal affirmative true disjunctions (مانعة الجمع والخلو). The common middle term is 'ج د'.
The variables: أ ب = A B, ج د = C D, هـ ز = H Z.
2
Establish the implication chain
From the first disjunction, 'أ ب' implies the non-existence of 'ج د' (since they cannot both be true). From the second, 'ج د' implies the non-existence of 'هـ ز' (if 'ج د' holds, 'هـ ز' cannot). Combining: 'أ ب' implies no 'ج د', which implies 'هـ ز' (since exactly one of 'ج د' or 'هـ ز' must be true). So 'أ ب' implies 'هـ ز'. Similarly, 'هـ ز' implies no 'ج د', which implies 'أ ب' (since exactly one of 'أ ب' or 'ج د' must be true). So 'هـ ز' implies 'أ ب'.
This shows mutual implication: 'أ ب' implies 'هـ ز' and 'هـ ز' implies 'أ ب'. But careful: 'هـ ز' implies 'أ ب'? Actually from the second disjunction, if 'هـ ز' is true, then 'ج د' is false. Then from the first disjunction, since 'ج د' is false, 'أ ب' must be true. So yes, 'هـ ز' implies 'أ ب'.
3
Derive the equivalence of the extremes
From 'أ ب' implies 'هـ ز' and 'هـ ز' implies 'أ ب', the two extremes are equivalent. Moreover, note the negations: 'أ ب' implies 'هـ ز' and 'هـ ز' implies not 'أ ب'? Actually we have: 'أ ب' implies 'هـ ز', and 'هـ ز' implies 'أ ب'. So they are equivalents. Wait check: earlier chain: 'أ ب' implies no 'ج د', and no 'ج د' implies 'هـ ز' (since without 'ج د', 'هـ ز' must be true). So 'أ ب' implies 'هـ ز'. Conversely, 'هـ ز' implies no 'ج د' (since if 'هـ ز' true, 'ج د' false), and no 'ج د' implies 'أ ب' (since without 'ج د', 'أ ب' must be true). So 'هـ ز' implies 'أ ب'. So indeed 'أ ب' and 'هـ ز' are equivalent. The text says: 'فظهر أن الطرفين متساويان'.
The text also notes that 'أ ب' implies 'ز' and 'هـ ز' implies no 'ب', but that seems specific. Focus on the equivalence.
4
List the resulting disjunctions
From the equivalence, the early logicians derive multiple conclusions: (1) 'دائمًا إما ليس (أ ب) أو (هـ ز)' as a preventing conjunction, preventing emptiness, and both. (2) 'دائمًا إما ليس (هـ ز) أو (أ ب)' similarly. These are ways of expressing the equivalence: the impossibility of both 'أ ب' and 'هـ ز' being false (preventing emptiness) and the impossibility of both being true (preventing conjunction).
The text also mentions that these are 'من لوازم التساوي' (requirements of equivalence).

ثم القسم الأول وهو الذي تكون كل واحدة من مقدمتيه حقيقية: إنه غير منتج عند الجمهور والشيخ من جملتهم فإنه ذكر في الشفاء أنك إذا قلت لا يخلو إما أن يكون (أ ب) وإما أن يكون (ج د) فهذا القول إنما يكون صدقا إذا لم يكن قسم ثالث لأن معنى قولنا لا يخلو إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن يكون ج د هو أنه إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن لا يكون كان لا محالة جد، فإن كان قد لا يكون ج د فالقضية كاذبة. اللهم إلا أن تجعل جزئية وحينئذ لا يكون لها قسم ثالث وبالجملة فكلمة إما كما يدل على العناد بين الشيئين فكذلك يدل على أن لا يخلو عن أحدهما فلو قلت دائما إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن يكون ج د، ودائمًا إما أن يكون (ج) د وإما أن يكون «هـ د» فـ «وز»، إن كان هو «أب» فالقضيتان هما قضية واحدة؛ ولا مجال للإنتاج حينئذ. وإن كان غير «أب» فههنا قسم ثالث؛ والقضيتان كاذبتان حينئذ. وعند قوم من المتقدمين إذا كانت مقدمتاه حقيقيتين وهما موجبتان كليتان ينتج متّصلة كلية من الطرفين لاستلزام كل واحد من الطرفين نقيض الأوسط استلزاما كليًّا، واستلزام نقيض الأوسط للطرف الآخر. وينتج أيضًا منفصلة سالبة حقيقية وغير حقيقية كما في قولنا دائمًا إما أن يكون «أب» أو «ج د»، ودائما إما أن يكون (ج) د» أو «هز» ينتج دائما إما ليس «أب» أو «هز» مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلوّ ومانعة الجمع والخلو، ودائمًا إما ليس «هز» أو «أب» مانعة الجمع والخلو ومانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو لما أن الطرفين متساويان. وذلك لأن «أب» مستلزم لعدم جد المستلزم لوجود «هز» وهما مستلزمان استلزام «أب» له «ز» . وكذلك «هز» مستلزم لعدم ج د المستلزم لا «ب»؛ وهما مستلزمان استلزام «هـ ز » لا «ب»؛ فظهر أن الطرفين متساويان. والنتائج المذكورة من لوازم التساوي: إما استلزام «أب» له «ز» فإنه يستلزم امتناع الخلو بين عدم «أب» و «هـز» و امتناع الجمع بين عدم «هز» و «أب». وإما استلزام «هــز» ولا «ب» فإنه يستلزم امتناع الخلو بين عدم «هــز» و «أب» وامتناع الجمع بين هـز وعدم «أب». ولما صدق كل واحدة من مانعتي الجمع
Then the first type, which is that each of its two premises is true [disjunction]: It is non-productive according to the majority and the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] among them. For he mentioned in the Shifa’: If you say ‘It cannot be but either it is (A B) or it is (C D)’, this statement is only true if there is no third division, because the meaning of our saying ‘It cannot be but either it is ‘A B’ or it is ‘C D’’ is that either it is ‘A B’ or it is not, it would necessarily be ‘C D’? [unclear]. If it is possible that it is not ‘C D’, then the proposition is false. Unless you make it particular, and then it has no third division. In general, the word ‘either’ indicates mutual exclusivity and also indicates that you cannot be free from one of them. So if you say always: either it is ‘A B’ or it is ‘C D’, and always: either it is ‘C D’ or it is ‘H D’, then ‘W’? If it is ‘A B’, then the two propositions are one proposition, and there is no room for production then. And if it is other than ‘A B’, then there is a third division, and the two propositions are then false. According to some of the earlier [scholars], if both its premises are true [disjunctions] and they are universal affirmative, it produces a universal categorical from the two sides, because each of the two sides necessarily implies the contradictory of the middle, and the contradictory of the middle necessarily implies the other side. And it also produces a negative true disjunction and non-true [disjunction], as in our saying: always either it is ‘A B’ or ‘C D’, and always either it is ‘C D’ or ‘H Z’, it produces: always it is either not ‘A B’ or ‘H Z’ – a preventing conjunction, a preventing emptiness, and a preventing conjunction and emptiness – and always either not ‘H Z’ or ‘A B’ – a preventing conjunction and emptiness, a preventing conjunction, and a preventing emptiness – because the two sides are equivalent. That is because ‘A B’ implies the non-existence of ‘C D’, which implies the existence of ‘H Z’, and they both imply the implication of ‘A B’ for ‘Z’. Likewise, ‘H Z’ implies the non-existence of ‘C D’, which implies the non-existence of ‘B’? and they both imply the implication of ‘H Z’ for the non-existence of ‘B’. So it appears that the two sides are equivalent. The mentioned results are from the requirements of equivalence: either the implication of ‘A B’ for ‘Z’ – for it necessitates the impossibility of emptiness between the non-existence of ‘A B’ and ‘H Z’, and the impossibility of conjunction between the non-existence of ‘H Z’ and ‘A B’. Or the implication of ‘H Z’ for the non-existence of ‘B’ – for it necessitates the impossibility of emptiness between the non-existence of ‘H Z’ and ‘A B’, and the impossibility of conjunction between ‘H Z’ and the non-existence of ‘A B’. And when each of the two preventing conjunctions is true... [text cuts off]
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage addresses the productivity of the first type of compound disjunctive syllogism, where both premises are true disjunctions (man‘at al-jam‘ wa al-khulū). The author presents two opposing views. First, the majority, including Ibn Sina, holds that such a syllogism yields no conclusion. Ibn Sina's argument from the Shifa’ is cited: if you have a true disjunction like 'Either A is B or C is D', then for the statement to be true, there must be no third alternative. The term 'either' (immā) implies both mutual exclusivity and that one of the two must hold. Now consider two such disjunctions sharing a common term: 'Either A is B or C is D' and 'Either C is D or H is D'. If the common term 'C is D' is true, then both disjunctions collapse into a single proposition; if it is false, then there is a third alternative (something besides C D), making one or both premises false. Hence, no valid syllogism arises. The author then presents an alternative view from some earlier scholars who claim that such a syllogism is productive. They argue that from two universal affirmative true disjunctions, a universal categorical conclusion can be drawn, linking the two extremes through the implication of each side for the contradictory of the middle. Furthermore, it produces a negative disjunction, either true or non-true. The author illustrates with an example using variables: from 'Always either A B or C D' and 'Always either C D or H Z', one can conclude 'Always either not A B or H Z' in all three forms of disjunction (preventing conjunction, preventing emptiness, and both). The proof relies on the equivalence of the two extremes after a series of implications: A B implies not C D, which implies H Z, so A B implies H Z. Conversely, H Z implies not C D, which implies not A B, so H Z implies not A B. Thus the extremes are equivalent; the resulting disjunctions reflect that equivalence. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is essentially complete. This debate highlights the complexity of disjunctive syllogisms: while the majority rejects their productivity under strict conditions, some early logicians found them valid through additional implications. For example, in Islamic law, one might say 'Either this liquid is wine or it is grape juice' and 'Either it is grape juice or it is vinegar'; here the common term 'grape juice' leads to the same issue Ibn Sina diagnosed—either the proposition collapses or a third alternative (vinegar) appears, making the premises false if the liquid is wine. The alternative view, however, would attempt to salvage a conclusion through implications about the nature of the substances.

Producing a Universal Conditional from Two Universal Affirmative Disjunctives
Author explains early scholars' view on conclusions from two universal affirmative disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical inference that two universal affirmative disjunctive premises yield a universal conditional and negative disjunctive conclusions, by tracing the entailment chains and identifying the equivalence of the extremes.

Core Idea
From two universal affirmative disjunctives, a conditional and several disjunctions follow through entailment chains.

In this lesson, we examine a specific claim from some early logicians: that two universal affirmative disjunctive premises (e.g., "Always either A B or C D" and "Always either C D or E F") produce a universal conditional ("If A B then E F") and also various negative disjunctions. This inference relies on the logical relationships between the terms, particularly the entailment of each extreme through the middle term. By working through the example provided in the text, you will understand how such conclusions are derived and what conditions must hold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the two disjunctive premises in the Arabic example: "دائمًا إما أن يكون «أب» أو «ج د»" and "ودائمًا إما أن يكون «ج د» أو «هز»". Note that both are universal affirmative disjunctives (موجبتان كليتان).
2
Extract the middle term
Recognize that "ج د" (JD) appears in both premises, serving as the middle term connecting the two extremes "أب" (AB) and "هز" (HZ).
The middle term is the repeated term in both premises.
3
Trace the entailment from each extreme
Understand that in a genuine disjunction, if one disjunct is true, the other is false. Thus, if AB is true, JD is false (i.e., not-JD). The text states that not-JD entails HZ (likely because the second premise guarantees that if JD is false, HZ must be true). Therefore, AB entails HZ. Similarly, HZ entails not-JD which entails not-AB? Actually the text says HZ entails not-JD which entails not-B (but this is unclear; for the lesson, we focus on the conditional AB → HZ).
The chain: AB → not-JD, and not-JD → HZ, so AB → HZ.
4
Derive the conclusions
The entailment AB → HZ is a universal conditional (متّصلة كلية). Additionally, from the equivalence of AB and HZ (or their negations), we get various negative disjunctions: e.g., "Always either not AB or HZ" (which can be exclusive, exhaustive, or both). List the conclusions as mentioned.
The conclusions include: دائما إما ليس «أب» أو «هز» (مانعة الجمع, مانعة الخلو, or both) and دائما إما ليس «هز» أو «أب» (similarly).
5
Verify the logic
Check that the conclusions necessarily follow given the premises. Note that the reasoning assumes the disjunctions are genuine (حقيقيتين) and universal affirmative. The equivalence of the sides (الطرفين متساويان) justifies the multiple disjunctive conclusions.
The text asserts that the two genuine (حقيقية) disjunctions are necessarily true because each side is equivalent to the negation of the other.

وعند قوم من المتقدمين إذا كانت مقدمتاه حقيقيتين وهما موجبتان كليتان ينتج متّصلة كلية من الطرفين لاستلزام كل واحد من الطرفين نقيض الأوسط استلزاما كليًّا، واستلزام نقيض الأوسط للطرف الآخر. وينتج أيضًا منفصلة سالبة حقيقية وغير حقيقية كما في قولنا دائمًا إما أن يكون «أب» أو «ج د»، ودائما إما أن يكون «ج د» أو «هز» ينتج دائما إما ليس «أب» أو «هز» مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلوّ ومانعة الجمع والخلو ، ودائمًا إما ليس «هز» أو «أب» مانعة الجمع والخلو ومانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو لما أن الطرفين متساويان. وذلك لأن «أب» مستلزم لعدم جد المستلزم لوجود «هز» وهما مستلزمان استلزام «أب» له «ز» . وكذلك «هز» مستلزم لعدم ج د المستلزم لا «ب»؛ وهما مستلزمان استلزام «هـ ز » لا «ب»؛ فظهر أن الطرفين متساويان. والنتائج المذكورة من لوازم التساوي: إما استلزام «أب» له «ز» فإنه يستلزم امتناع الخلو بين عدم «أب» و «هـز» و امتناع الجمع بين عدم «هز» و «أب». وإما استلزام «هــز» ولا «ب» فإنه يستلزم امتناع الخلو بين عدم «هــز» و «أب» وامتناع الجمع بين هـز وعدم «أب». ولما صدق كل واحدة من مانعتي الجمع والخلو بين كل واحد من الطرفين ونقيض الآخر فقد صدقت الحقيقيتان منهما بالضرورة.
And according to some early scholars, if both premises are genuine and are universal affirmative, it produces a universal conditional from the two sides, because each side entails the contradictory of the middle universal entailment, and the contradictory of the middle entails the other side. And it also produces a negative disjunctive, true and non-true, as in our saying: 'Always either it is A B or J D, and always either J D or H Z' produces 'Always either not A B or H Z', [which is] exclusive, exhaustive, and both exclusive and exhaustive; and 'Always either not H Z or A B', [which is] exclusive and exhaustive and both, because the two sides are equivalent. That is because A B entails the non-existence of J D, which entails the existence of H Z, and these two entail A B entailing [H] Z. Similarly, H Z entails the non-existence of J D, which entails non-B, and these two entail H Z entailing non-B. So it appears that the two sides are equivalent. The mentioned conclusions are among the necessary consequences of equivalence: either the entailment of A B to Z entails the impossibility of disjunction between non-A B and H Z and the impossibility of conjunction between non-H Z and A B; or the entailment of H Z to non-B entails the impossibility of disjunction between non-H Z and A B and the impossibility of conjunction between H Z and non-A B. And since each of the exclusive and exhaustive [disjunctions] between each side and its contradictory is true, the two genuine ones are necessarily true.

Commentary
This passage presents a logical position held by some early scholars (قوم من المتقدمين) regarding what follows from two universal affirmative disjunctive premises. The author begins by stating the claim: when both premises are genuine (حقيقيتين) universal affirmatives, a universal conditional (متّصلة كلية) can be produced from the two extremes, because each extreme (طرف) entails the contradictory (نقيض) of the middle term (الأوسط), and the contradictory of the middle entails the other extreme. Additionally, a negative disjunctive (منفصلة سالبة) – both true (حقيقية) and non-true (غير حقيقية) – is produced. The author then illustrates with an example: 'Always either A B or J D' and 'Always either J D or H Z' yield the conclusion 'Always either not A B or H Z', which can be mutually exclusive (مانعة الجمع), exhaustive (مانعة الخلو), or both. Similarly, 'Always either not H Z or A B' is produced. The basis is that the two extremes (A B and H Z) are equivalent (متساويان). The reasoning is given: A B entails the absence of J D, which entails the presence of H Z, so A B entails H Z; H Z entails the absence of J D, which entails non-B, so H Z entails non-B. Thus each extreme entails the other's contradictory? Actually the text says 'استلزام «أب» له «ز»' and 'استلزام «هـ ز» لا «ب»', which indicates that A B entails Z (some term) and H Z entails non-B. The equivalence leads to the conclusion that each extreme is equivalent to the negation of the other? The author then lists the specific disjunctive conclusions that follow from this equivalence: from A B entailing Z, we get the impossibility of coexistence of non-A B and H Z (i.e., they cannot both be absent) and impossibility of conjunction of non-H Z and A B (they cannot both be present). From H Z entailing non-B, we get the impossibility of coexistence of non-H Z and A B and impossibility of conjunction of H Z and non-A B. Since each of these disjunctions (مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو) holds between each term and its contradictory, the two genuine (حقيقية) disjunctions are necessarily true. This is a detailed analysis of how such highly specified premises lead to multiple conclusions.

Distinguishing Actuality from Hypothesis in Logical Opposition
Author declares the truth to be the view of the majority and Ibn Sina, rejecting the earlier view as hypothetical and unnatural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the author's critique of the earlier view on disjunctive syllogisms by distinguishing between actuality (tahqīq) and hypothesis (fard) and applying the condition of genuine opposition.

Core Idea
Genuine opposition requires exclusivity with no third option; the earlier view is only hypothetical.

In this passage, the author settles a dispute regarding the validity of certain syllogistic forms. He distinguishes between what holds in reality (tahqīq) and what is merely assumed (fard). He then provides a logical argument about the nature of genuine opposition, which he claims is self-evident. By examining this critique, you will learn how to separate actual logical principles from hypothetical constructs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Find which view is attributed to the majority and Ibn Sina, and which to others. Note the key distinction: tahqīq (actuality) vs fard (assumption).
The author declares that the truth is with the majority and Ibn Sina.
2
Analyze the author's qualification
The author says unless it is made clear in a specific material that it can be actualized; but he immediately says it is far from nature. Why? Because of the argument about opposition.
This shows the earlier view is not impossible in theory but rarely realized.
3
Understand the argument about opposition
What does the author mean by genuine opposition (mu'anada)? Why can't something be opposite to what opposes it? Why does genuine opposition require no third term distinct from both?
The argument is that if A opposes B, and there is a C distinct from both, then A and B are not genuine opposites.
4
Connect to the earlier view
How does this argument refute the earlier view? The earlier view assumed a scenario that violates the condition of genuine opposition, hence it is only hypothetical and unnatural.
This is why the majority view is considered correct according to actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق منهما ما ذهب إليه الجمهور والشيخ وغيره فإن ذلك بحسب التحقق، وما ذهب إليه غيرهم فذلك بحسب الفرض. اللهم إلا أن يتبينوا في مادة معينة يفهم من ذلك أنه من جملة ما يمكن تحققه؛ وإنه بعيد عن الطبع جدا لما مرَّ من قبل. ولأن المعاند للشيء لا يمكن أن يكون معاندا لما يعانده وإلا يلزم أن يكون الشيء معاندا لنفسه. والمعاندة الحقيقية بين الشيئين لا يمكن تحققها إلا ولا يمكن أن يكون بينهما ما يغاير كل واحد منهما : وهذا من جملة الضروريات.
And the truth from them is what the majority and the Shaykh and others went to, for that is according to actuality, and what others went to is according to assumption. Unless they make it clear in a specific subject matter that it is understood to be among what can be actualized; but it is very far from nature due to what has passed before. And because the opposite of a thing cannot be opposite to what opposes it, otherwise it would necessitate that the thing is opposite to itself. And genuine opposition between two things cannot be actualized except that nothing can exist that is distinct from each of them; and this is among the self-evident truths.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a critical rebuttal of the earlier view presented by some early scholars. The author declares that the truth (الحق) is the position of the majority (الجمهور) and the Shaykh (الشيخ, i.e., Ibn Sina) and others. The earlier view, he says, is only according to actuality (بحسب التحقق) – meaning it holds in theory but not in practice? Actually the text says 'ما ذهب إليه الجمهور ... فإن ذلك بحسب التحقق' and the opposite view is 'بحسب الفرض' (according to assumption). Then he qualifies: unless they make it clear in a specific material subject that it can be actualized; but this is very far from nature (بعيد عن الطبع) because of what was said before. He then provides a philosophical argument: the opposite (المعاند) of a thing cannot be opposite to what opposes it, otherwise the thing would be opposite to itself. Genuine opposition (المعاندة الحقيقية) between two things cannot be actualized unless there is no third thing that is distinct from each of them. In other words, true opposition is between two and only two alternatives; any third alternative would break the opposition. This is considered self-evident (ضروريات). Thus, the earlier claim that two universal affirmative disjunctives could yield a conclusion only makes sense if the two extremes are genuinely opposite with no third option, but that is rarely the case in reality, hence it is far from nature.

فرض
معاندة
ضروري

Key Takeaway
Genuine opposition is exclusive; any hypothetical scenario without exclusivity is far from natural reality.
