When Disjunctive Syllogisms Fail: Particular and Negative Premises
Author argues that no conclusion follows when one premise is particular or negative, refuting a false claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a disjunctive syllogism fails to produce a conclusion due to a particular or negative premise, and refute invalid derivations.

Core Idea
Not every two premises yield a conclusion – particular and negative premises break disjunctive syllogisms.

We have seen that disjunctive syllogisms can produce valid conclusions when both premises are universal and affirmative. However, the author of this passage makes a sharp point: when one premise is particular or negative, no conclusion follows. He even refutes a false claim that a particular conclusion can be derived. In this lesson, we will dissect his argument, understand why particular and negative premises are insufficient, and learn to spot invalid syllogistic moves in Islamic reasoning contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their types
Look at the example given: 'Sometimes it is either J D or H Z' (particular premise) and 'Always it is either J D or H Z or A B' (universal premise). Determine which premise is particular and which is universal. Note that the first premise is particular because it starts with 'sometimes' (قد يكون), indicating it is not always true.
The particular premise is not a full disjunction; it only asserts possibility.
2
Recognize the false claim
Someone claims that from these premises we can conclude 'Sometimes it is either H Z or A B'. This is the claim the author refutes. You need to see that this conclusion is not logically entailed by the premises.
The false claim attempts to eliminate the first disjunct (J D) from both premises, but the particular premise does not guarantee that J D is sometimes absent in the way needed.
3
Examine the author's refutation
The author says the claimed conclusion is not a judgment obtained by way of syllogism (لا يكون حكمًا محمولًا يحصل من جهة القياس). This means the conclusion does not follow necessarily from the premises. The syllogism requires that the conclusion be a predication derived from the premises, not an arbitrary selection.
The author emphasizes that a valid syllogistic conclusion must be obtained 'من جهة القياس' – through the process of syllogism.
4
Consider the case where the particular is the major premise
The author adds that even when the particular premise is the major (the universal premise contains the major term), the alleged conclusion is invalid. This shows that the invalidity is not about which premise is particular, but about having any particular premise at all.
This reinforces the general rule: one particular premise spoils the syllogism.
5
Understand the general rule for negative premises
Finally, the author notes that according to the majority, if one premise is negative, no conclusion follows either. The text is cut off, but the idea is that negativity introduces opposition between the terms, preventing a determinate conclusion. A negative disjunctive premise (e.g., 'It is not the case that either X or Y') cancels the disjunction and makes syllogistic inference impossible.
The opposition (تعاند) between sides means the disjunction is no longer exclusive or exhaustive in a way that supports a conclusion.

And likewise if one of them is particular, it also does not produce, as in our saying: 'Sometimes it is either J D or H Z, and always it is either J D or H Z or A B', and this is said such that the particular is truly particular. Commentary
This passage addresses the conditions under which disjunctive syllogisms fail to produce a conclusion. First, the author states that if one premise is particular (جزئية), no conclusion results. He gives an example: 'Sometimes it is either J D or H Z' (particular premise) and 'Always it is either J D or H Z or A B' (universal premise). This formulation ensures the particular is genuinely particular (i.e., not equivalent to a universal). He then refutes a false claim that a particular conclusion 'Sometimes it is either H Z or A B' can be derived; he says that claim is false because the conclusion is not a judgment obtained through syllogism (it is not implied). Similarly, if the particular is the major premise, the alleged conclusion is not valid. Next, the author mentions that according to those same early scholars (ذلك القوم), if one premise is particular, they claim a particular entailment can be produced, leading to six conclusions similar to the universal case. But the author implies this is not the accepted view. Finally, he notes that according to the majority (الجمهور), if one premise is negative (سالبة), no conclusion is produced either, because the disjunction is true with opposition between the two sides sometimes and without sometimes (the text cuts off). The incomplete sentence likely explains why negation frustrates the syllogism.

The Syllogism

Conditions for Valid Exclusive Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author discusses conditions for valid inference from exclusive disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a disjunctive syllogism with exclusive and exhaustive disjunction yields a valid conclusion, particularly when premises are particular or negative.

Core Idea
A truly exclusive and exhaustive disjunction requires true opposition; negative or particular premises break the inference.

This passage from the logical work examines the rules for drawing conclusions from a disjunctive syllogism that is both exclusive (mani'at al-jam) and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). The author argues that certain conditions must be met for the inference to be valid, and he refutes alternative views. This lesson will walk through the key conditions and test cases.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of disjunction
Identify that the disjunction in question is both 'mani'at al-jam' (cannot both be true) and 'mani'at al-khuluw' (cannot both be false). This is a true exclusive disjunction.
This is the strongest form of disjunction.
2
Recognize the condition of true opposition ('inad)
The author states that true opposition between the two disjuncts is required. The two must have nothing in common that differs from both. This is a necessary truth.
Otherwise, the disjunction is not genuinely exclusive.
3
Examine the case of a particular premise
When one premise is particular (juz'iyya), the author argues no conclusion follows because the middle term is not universal. He gives an example with variables.
The majority view is that no conclusion is produced.
4
Examine the case of a negative premise
When one premise is negative, the majority holds no conclusion follows, as the disjunction can be true with or without opposition. The author provides examples with 'five is odd or even'.
The refutation of the view that allows conclusions leads to contradiction.
5
Apply the conditions
Given a specific disjunctive syllogism from Islamic law (e.g., a ruling on whether an act is wajib or haram, with no third possibility), check if the conditions of exclusivity and exhaustiveness are met and if the premises are universal affirmative. Then determine if a valid conclusion can be drawn.

And the emptiness between each one of the two extremes and the negation of the other, then the two true propositions are necessarily true. And the truth among them is what the majority, the Shaykh, and others held, for that is according to actuality, while what others held is according to hypothesis. Unless they clarify in a specific matter that it is understood that it is among what can be actual; but it is very far from nature due to what preceded. And because the opponent of a thing cannot be opposed to what opposes it, otherwise the thing would be opposed to itself. And true opposition between two things cannot be actualized except when there cannot be between them anything that differs from each of them; and this is among necessary truths. Similarly, if one of them is particular, it also does not produce, as in our statement: It may be either J is D or H is Z, and always either J is D or H is Z or A is B, but it is said thus only so that the particular is truly particular. And whoever claims that it produces 'It may be either H is Z or A is B,' his claim is false, because it is not a predicative judgment obtained by means of syllogism. And on this, if the particular is the major premise, then the conclusion, namely our statement 'Either A is B or J is D,' is not by means of syllogism. And according to those people, if one of them is particular, then the particular's extreme necessitates the non-existence of the middle, which necessitates the other extreme, and they produce the entailment of the particular's extreme to the universal extreme as a particular entailment. And from it follows the six conclusions in the manner you know for the two universal premises: this is if the two premises are affirmative. As for if one of them is negative, it does not produce according to the majority either, because it is true sometimes with opposition between the two extremes and sometimes with non-opposition between them. As for with opposition, because our statement is true: 'Five is either odd or even,' but it is absolutely not the case that 'It is either even or not odd'; and the truth is the opposition between five's being odd and its not being odd. As for with non-opposition, like our statement: 'Five is either odd or even,' but it is absolutely not the case that 'It is either even or that it is not empty'; and the truth is the lack of opposition between its being odd and its not being empty. And the difference is among what indicates sterility. And on this, if one of them is particular. And according to those people, it produces whether the parts are universal affirmative or particular; and the conclusion is a particular negative whose premise is either the extreme of the negative or the extreme of the affirmative, otherwise each of them would be false. And each of the two extremes would necessitate the other a universal necessitation; then they would necessitate the equivalence of the two extremes; and that necessitates the realization of true opposition between the two parts of the negative, because true opposition is from the matter, and other than it necessitates true opposition between it and everything equivalent to that other, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for valid inference from a disjunctive syllogism that is both exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw)—i.e., a true disjunction where exactly one of the two alternatives is true. The author begins by stating that when there is emptiness (al-khulu) between each extreme and the negation of the other, both disjuncts are necessarily true. He then contrasts the view of the majority (al-jumhur), the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina), and others, which is based on actuality, versus the view of others based on hypothesis. He argues against the hypothetical view, saying it is very unnatural. The reason: a thing cannot be opposed to what opposes it, otherwise it would be self-opposed, and true opposition requires that there be nothing that differs from each of the two opposites. This is a necessary truth. Next, he examines the case where one premise is particular (juz'iyya). He provides an example with symbolic variables: J D, H Z, A B. He argues that no conclusion follows, because the particular premise does not yield a universal middle. Anyone claiming a conclusion like 'It may be either H Z or A B' is mistaken—it is not a proper syllogistic inference. If the particular is the major, the alleged conclusion is not from the syllogism. The author then mentions the view of 'those people' (al-qawm) who claim that if one premise is particular, the particular extreme necessitates the non-existence of the middle, which necessitates the other extreme, leading to six conclusions. But this is only when both premises are affirmative. When one premise is negative, the majority holds that no conclusion follows, because the disjunction can be true with or without opposition between the extremes. He illustrates with examples: 'Five is either odd or even' is a true exclusive disjunction, but 'It is either even or not odd' is false because the two disjuncts are actually opposed? Actually the author says: 'Five is either odd or even' is true, but 'It is either even or not odd' is not true—because the second disjunct 'not odd' is contradictory to 'odd', so the disjunction 'even or not odd' is not exclusive? The commentary should follow carefully. The author contrasts two cases: one with opposition (ta'and) and one without (la ta'and). In the first example, there is opposition between 'five is odd' and 'five is not odd' (which is just the contradictory). In the second example, 'five is either odd or even' is true, but 'either even or not empty' is not a valid disjunction because 'not empty' is irrelevant. This shows the argument is sterile. The passage concludes by considering the view of 'those people' (al-qawm) who claim that even with a negative premise, a conclusion can be drawn, a particular negative. But this leads to absurd consequences: the two extremes would become equivalent, necessitating true opposition between the parts of the negative, which is impossible. The author refutes that position, showing it leads to contradiction.

The Second Type of Disjunctive Syllogism: Exclusive of Conjunction Only
Author explains the second type of disjunctive syllogism producing a conditional conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid conclusion of a disjunctive syllogism using only man'at al-jam' and explain why it is a particular conditional rather than a universal one.

Core Idea
When a disjunction only excludes conjunction, its syllogism yields a particular conditional conclusion, not a universal one.

This lesson explores a special type of disjunctive syllogism where the disjunction prevents both disjuncts from being true together (man'at al-jam') but does not require at least one to be true (man'at al-khuluw). The author explains that such a syllogism produces a particular affirmative conditional statement from the negation of either extreme. Understanding this type is crucial for recognizing the limits of inference when disjunctions are not exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Ensure each premise is an exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam') that only prohibits both disjuncts being true simultaneously, not the case of both being false.
The text uses two such disjunctions: e.g., 'stone or animal' and 'animal or tree'.
2
Extract the two premises
Write down both disjunctive premises. Note the middle term (al-awsat) that appears in both disjunctions—this term mediates the inference.
In the example, 'animal' is the middle term.
3
Recognize the conclusion type
The syllogism produces a conditional statement (muttasila) where the antecedent is the negation of one extreme from the first disjunction, and the consequent is the negation of the corresponding extreme from the second disjunction.
The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, meaning it holds in some cases, not all.
4
Test with the example
Apply the rule to the given example: from 'not stone' (negation of first extreme) try to conclude 'not tree' (negation of second extreme). Note why this fails universally.
The author shows that 'not stone' is more general than 'not tree', so a universal conditional is invalid.
5
Understand the first figure role
The middle term necessitates each negation via the first figure of categorical syllogism when the premises are universal. This necessitation is the foundation of the conditional conclusion.
The first figure ensures the inference is valid in principle, but the conclusion's universality is blocked by the generality difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القسم الثاني وهو الذي يكون من مانعتي الجمع دون الخلو فإنه ينتج بالذات متّصلة موجبة جزئية من نقيض الطرفين سواء كانت موجبة كلية أو جزئية، ومقدمها أي واحد كان من نقيضي الطرفين لاستلزام عين الأوسط لكل واحد من نقيضي الطرفين استلزاما كليا إن كانت المقدمتان كليتين؛ وأحدهما جزئياً والآخر كليا إن كانت إحديهما جزئية. وإنتاج هذين الاستلزامين من الشكل الأول، ولا ينتج كلية لاحتمال أن يكون نقيض كل واحد من الطرفين أعم من نقيض الآخر، وامتناع استلزام العام ما يكون أخص منه. فيقال مثلا هذا الشيء إما أن يكون حجرًا أو حيوانًا، ودائمًا إما أن يكون حيوانا أو شجرًا مع أنه لا يصدق أن يقال كلَّما كان لا حجرًا كان لا شجرًا. وإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين سالبة كلية كانت كل واحدة منهما / أو لا كلية بل
Then the second division: that which is from the two exclusives without the exclusive of vacuity, for it essentially produces a particular affirmative conditional from the negation of the two extremes, whether they are universal affirmative or particular. And its antecedent is any one of the two negations of the extremes, because the same middle necessitates each one of the two negations of the extremes a universal necessitation if the two premises are universal; and one particular and the other universal if one of them is particular. And the production of these two necessitations is from the first figure. And it does not produce a universal, because it is possible that the negation of each one of the extremes is more general than the negation of the other, and it is impossible for the general to necessitate what is more specific than it. So it is said, for example: This thing is either a stone or an animal, and always it is either an animal or a tree, although it is not true to say that whenever it is not a stone, it is not a tree. And if one of the two premises is universal negative, then each one of them is... or not universal but
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of disjunctive syllogism: the one that uses two 'exclusive of conjunction' (mani'at al-jam') without 'exclusive of vacuity' (mani'at al-khuluw). In other words, the disjunction prevents both alternatives from being true together, but does not prevent both from being false (i.e., there may be a third option). The author explains that this type produces, by itself, a particular affirmative conditional (muttasila) from the negation of either extreme. The antecedent of the conditional is any one of the two negations of the extremes. This is because the middle term (al-awsat) — which is the term that appears in both premises and mediates the inference — necessitates each negation of the extremes universally if both premises are universal, or one particular and the other universal if one premise is particular. These two necessitations are produced via the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal) of categorical syllogism. However, the conclusion cannot be universal, because the negation of one extreme might be more general than the negation of the other. For example, consider: 'This thing is either a stone or an animal' (first premise is an exclusive disjunction: stone or animal, but not both). 'It is always either an animal or a tree' (second premise: animal or tree, but not both). From 'it is not a stone,' we cannot conclude 'it is not a tree' universally, because 'not stone' is more general than 'not tree' (something that is not a stone could still be a tree, e.g., a plant). The author concludes by noting that if one premise is universal negative, then the situation changes, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
النتيجة المتصلة
الأوسط
الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A universal categorical conclusion.
B) A particular affirmative conditional from the negation of either extreme.
C) A universal negative conditional.
D) A particular disjunctive conclusion.

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive syllogism with two exclusivity-only disjunctions yields a particular affirmative conditional, not a universal one, because the negation of one extreme is often more general than the negation of the other.

Third Division of Compound Syllogisms: Two Non-Exclusive Disjunctives
Author explains the third division of compound syllogisms with two non-exclusive disjunctive premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the valid conclusion form (particular affirmative conditional) from two non-exclusive disjunctive premises and identify when a negative premise leads to a valid or invalid conclusion.

Core Idea
Two non-exclusive disjunctives yield only a particular conditional truth because one side may be broader than the other.

This passage delves into the third division of compound syllogisms, where both premises are non-exclusive disjunctives (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'). Understanding this structure is crucial for analyzing arguments that involve disjunctions with overlapping possibilities. We will explore why the conclusion is always a particular affirmative conditional and the conditions under which negative premises can produce a valid negative conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Recognize that both premises are disjunctives where at least one must be true but both can be true (non-exclusive). This is the meaning of 'mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'.
2
Apply the rule: particular affirmative conditional
The text states that such a combination yields a particular affirmative conditional from both sides, regardless of whether premises are universal or particular.
The conclusion is of the form 'It may be that whenever X is, Y is.'
3
Understand the role of negation of the middle term
The negation of the middle term (naqīḍ al-awsaṭ) is implied by each side, which drives the inference. The middle term is the common element shared between the two disjunctives.
4
Recognize the limitation to particular
The conclusion cannot be universal because one side may be more general than the other, as shown in the stone/animal example: it is false that 'whenever it is not a stone, it is not a tree.'
This asymmetry prevents a universal conditional.
5
Handle negative premises
If one premise is universal negative, the conclusion can be negative but only when the antecedent is taken from the affirmative side; otherwise no conclusion follows. The majority view is that if one premise is negative, no consistent conclusion is produced.
The text provides examples with variables to illustrate valid and invalid cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] و القسم الثالث هو الذي يكون من مانعتي الخلو دون الجمع ينتج متصلة جزئية موجبة من الطرفين سواء كانت المقدمتان موجبتين كليتين أو إحديهما جزئية لاستلزام نقيض الأوسط لكل واحد من الطرفين؛ وإنتاجه من الثالث، ولا ينتج كلية لاحتمال أن يكون كل واحد من الطرفين أعم من الطرف الآخر كما في قولنا هذا الشيء إما أن يكون لا حجرًا وإما أن يكون لا حيوانا، ودائما إما أن يكون لا حيواناً وإما أن يكون لا حجرا؛ ولا يصدق كلَّما كان لا حجرًا كان لا شجرًا. والشيخ ردَّ هذه المتّصلة إلى المنفصلة لما أنها غير مناسبة لكيفية المقدّمات، وإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين سالبة كلية كانت السالبة أو جزئية ينتج عند قوم سالبة. ومقدمها طرف الموجبة كلية كانت أو جزئية؛ ولا ينتج ومقدمها طرف السالبة. أما الأول فذلك مثل قولنا قد يكون إما أن يكون «أب» أو (ج) د » ، وليس البتة إما أن يكون «ج د» أو «هز» فإنه ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ هـز» وإلا لصدق نقيضها وهو قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ هـز» مانعا للخلو عن (ج) د » و «أب»؛ فيلزم امتناع الخلو عن هز» و ج د لاستلزام امتناع خلو (ج) د » عن «أب» امتناع خلوه عن «هز» الذي هو لازم «أب». وأما الثاني وهو ما إذا كان مقدمها طرف السالبة فلاحتمال أن يكون طرف الموجبة أعم من الطرف السالبة كما في قولنا هذا الشيء إما أن يكون لا إنسانًا أو حيوانًا، وليس البتة إما أن يكون حيوانًا أو حجرًا؛ ولا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كان حجرًا كان لا إنسانًا . والمشهور عند الجمهور أن إحدى مقدمتيه إذا كانت سالبة لا ينتج : والحق هذا فإن إنتاجه لا يكون مطردًا. وقد كان من الممكن أن يكون الشيء منفصلا عن شيء انفصالا نحن بصدده، وكان مستلزما لما يلازمه والشيخ اقتصر في هذا الموضع على مثال واحد [٢٠٦, وهو قولنا إما أن يكون / الإنسان زوجًا وإما أن لا يكون زوج زوج، وليس البتة إما أن لا يكون الإنسان زوج زوج وإما أن يكون فردًا وهذا من جملة ما يكفي المعونة في بيان كونه عقيما . ثمَّ الشركة بين سالبة الجزأين وموجبة الجزأين مما يستحيل وجوده وكذلك بين سالبة الجزأين وموجبة الجزء في الجزء الموجب بل ينتج في الجزء السالب.
[3] And the third division is that which is from two non-exclusive disjunctives (mani'tay al-khuluww duna al-jam'): it yields a particular affirmative conditional from both sides, whether both premises are universal affirmative or one of them is particular, because the negation of the middle term is implied by each of the two sides; and its production is from the third [figure], and it does not yield a universal because it is possible that each of the two sides is more general than the other side, as in our saying: 'This thing is either not a stone or not an animal, and always it is either not an animal or not a stone'; and it is not true that 'whenever it is not a stone, it is not a tree.' And the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] reduced this conditional to a disjunctive because it is not suitable for the quality of the premises. And if one of the two premises is universal negative, whether it is negative or particular, it yields (according to some people) a negative [conclusion], and its antecedent is the side of the affirmative, whether universal or particular; and it does not yield when its antecedent is the side of the negative. As for the first, it is like our saying: 'It may be that either it is (A B) or (J D), and it is definitely not that either it is (J D) or (H Z); for it yields 'It may not be that when it is (A B) then it is (H Z), otherwise its negation would be true, which is our saying 'Whenever it is (A B) then it is (H Z), being a preventive of emptiness of (J D) and (A B); so the impossibility of emptiness of (H Z) and (J D) necessarily follows, because the impossibility of emptiness of (J D) from (A B) implies the impossibility of its emptiness from (H Z) which is a necessary consequence of (A B).' And as for the second, which is when its antecedent is the side of the negative, it is because it is possible that the side of the affirmative is more general than the side of the negative, as in our saying: 'This thing is either not a human or an animal, and it is definitely not that it is either an animal or a stone'; and it is not true that 'It may not be that when it is a stone, it is not a human.' And the well-known view among the majority is that if one of its two premises is negative, it does not yield; and the truth is this, because its production is not consistent. And it was possible that a thing is disjoined from something in the disjunction we are dealing with, and it was necessitating what accompanies it. And the Shaykh limited himself in this place to a single example [206: it is our saying 'Man is either even or not even, and it is definitely not that man is not even or is odd.' And this is among what suffices for assistance in explaining its being sterile. Then sharing between a negative of two parts and an affirmative of two parts is impossible to exist, and likewise between a negative of two parts and an affirmative of part in the affirmative part, but it yields in the negative part.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an in-depth explanation of the third division of compound syllogisms (qiyas murakkab) that involve two disjunctive premises that are non-exclusive (mani'at al-khuluw without al-jam'). The author first states the general rule: such a combination yields a particular affirmative conditional conclusion, regardless of whether the premises are universal or particular. The reason is that the negation of the middle term implies each of the two sides. However, it does not yield a universal conditional because one side may be more general than the other, as illustrated by the example: 'This thing is either not a stone or not an animal, and always it is either not an animal or not a stone' – the conclusion 'whenever it is not a stone, it is not a tree' is false. The author then notes that Ibn Sina reduces such a conditional to a disjunctive, indicating a methodological preference. Next, the author addresses cases where one premise is negative. According to some scholars, a negative conclusion is produced, with the antecedent being the side of the affirmative premise. But if the antecedent is taken from the negative side, no conclusion follows due to the possibility that the affirmative side is more general. Two examples are given: first, with variables (A B), (J D), (H Z) showing a valid inference; second, with 'not a human', 'animal', 'stone' showing an invalid inference. The author then critiques the common view that when one premise is negative no conclusion follows, but asserts that the truth is that it is not consistent, and he provides an example from Ibn Sina about 'man, even, odd' to illustrate sterility. Finally, the author notes combinations like a negative of two parts with an affirmative of two parts are impossible, while a negative of two parts with an affirmative of one part yields a conclusion in the negative part.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع
متصلة جزئية موجبة
نقيض الأوسط
الشيخ

According to the third division, if both premises are non-exclusive disjunctives (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'), what type of conclusion can be directly derived?
A) A universal affirmative conditional
B) A particular affirmative conditional
C) A particular negative disjunctive
D) No conclusion at all

Key Takeaway
Two non-exclusive disjunctives together yield only a particular affirmative conditional, never a universal, because the sides may differ in generality.

?

The Syllogism

Fourth Division: Universal Premises Combination
Author explains the universal case of the fourth division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a compound syllogism when both premises are universal affirmative (one true conditional, one exclusive disjunctive).

Core Idea
A true conditional and an exclusive disjunctive yield a new conditional via transitivity.

In this lesson, we explore how a true conditional (haqiqi) and an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam') combine to produce a universal conditional when both premises are universal affirmative. This is a powerful tool for deriving necessary implications from categorical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify premises
Find the true conditional (if A then B) and the exclusive disjunctive (either C or D, not both). Both must be universal affirmative.
Exclusive disjunctive means exactly one of C or D is true.
2
Determine the middle term
The side of the disjunctive (say C) implies the negation of the other side (not D) due to exclusivity. This 'not D' serves as the middle term connecting premises.
3
Apply transitivity
C implies not D, and from the true conditional, not D implies B (since the conditional is 'if not D then B' or if it's structured that way). Therefore, C implies B.
The true conditional's antecedent must be the negation of the middle term.
4
Formulate conclusion
The conclusion is a universal conditional: 'If C then B', where C is one side of the exclusive disjunctive.
The antecedent cannot be the other side; only the side that implies the middle term's negation works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] والقسم الرابع هو أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه حقيقية، والأخرى مانعة الجمع ينتج متّصلة كلية مقدمها طرف مانعة الجمع لا غير إن كانت كل واحدة منهما موجبة كلية لما أن طرفها مستلزم لنقيض الأوسط المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية؛ وكون المستلزم للمستلزم للشيء مستلزما لذلك الشيء. ثمّ هذه المتّصلة ترتد إلى المنفصلة وهي مانعة الخلو من نقيض المقدم وعين التالي. وكذلك إلى مانعة الجمع من غير المقدم ونقيض التالي، وإن كانت إحدى مقدمتيه جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية مقدمها أي واحد كان من الطرفين؛ فإن كانت الكلية منهما حقيقية ينتجها بالذات لاستلزام طرف مانعة الجمع نقيض الأوسط استلزاما جزئيا المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية استلزاما كليًّا؛
[4] And the fourth division is that one of its two premises is a true conditional (haqiqi) and the other is an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam'); it yields a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the exclusive disjunctive only, if both premises are universal affirmative, because its side implies the negation of the middle term which implies the side of the true conditional; and because whatever implies the implication of something implies that thing. Then this conditional reduces to a disjunctive, which is a non-exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-khuluw) from the negation of the antecedent and the very consequent. And also to an exclusive disjunctive from the non-antecedent and the negation of the consequent. And if one of its two premises is particular, it yields a particular affirmative whose antecedent is whichever of the two sides; for if the universal among them is the true one, it produces it essentially because the side of the exclusive disjunctive implies the negation of the middle term with a particular implication, which implies the side of the true conditional with a universal implication.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth division of compound syllogisms where one premise is a true conditional (haqiqiyya, i.e., a necessary conditional like 'if A then B') and the other is an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam', meaning 'either but not both'). The author gives the rule: if both premises are universal affirmatives, the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the exclusive disjunctive. The justification is that the side of the exclusive disjunctive implies the negation of the middle term, which in turn implies the side of the true conditional; by transitivity of implication, the side of the exclusive disjunctive implies the side of the true conditional. The author then notes that this resulting conditional can be reduced to a disjunctive: specifically, to a non-exclusive disjunction between the negation of the antecedent and the consequent, and also to an exclusive disjunction between the non-antecedent and the negation of the consequent. This shows the equivalence between different logical forms. Next, if one premise is particular, the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, and its antecedent can be taken from either side. If the universal premise is the true conditional, the conclusion is produced essentially because the side of the exclusive disjunctive implies the negation of the middle term partially, which then implies the side of the true conditional universally. The author thus provides complete rules for this division.

Key Terms
حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
المستلزم للمستلزم للشيء مستلزم لذلك الشيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fourth division, when both premises are universal affirmative, what is the form of the conclusion?
A) A universal conditional with antecedent from the true conditional
B) A universal conditional with antecedent from the exclusive disjunctive
C) A particular conditional
D) A universal disjunctive

Key Takeaway
When both premises are universal, the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the exclusive disjunctive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a compound syllogism using a true conditional from fiqh: 'If a woman is menstruating, she is exempt from prayer' and an exclusive disjunctive: 'Either the woman is menstruating or she is not menstruating (and thus must pray).' Write the conclusion and explain the role of the middle term.

Transitivity of Implication in the Fourth Division
Author invokes transitivity to justify the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of transitivity (al-mustalzim lil-mustalzim) to derive implications in compound syllogisms.

Core Idea
If X implies Y and Y implies Z, then X implies Z – the backbone of chained reasoning.

The fourth division relies on the logical principle that if one thing implies another, and that other implies a third, then the first implies the third. This principle is explicitly stated in the passage as 'whatever implies the implication of something implies that thing.' Understanding this is essential for validating compound arguments.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
For any three propositions P, Q, R: if P implies Q, and Q implies R, then P implies R. This is transitivity of implication.
2
Identify the three terms
In the fourth division, let P = side of exclusive disjunctive (e.g., C), Q = negation of middle term (not D), R = consequent of true conditional (B).
These are abstract; in concrete examples, substitute specific propositions.
3
Verify the implications
P implies Q because of exclusivity (C implies not D). Q implies R because the true conditional is 'if not D then B'. So both links are valid.
4
Derive the conclusion
By transitivity, P implies R. This is the new conditional that forms the conclusion of the compound syllogism.
This step is the core of the justification given by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] والقسم الرابع هو أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه حقيقية، والأخرى مانعة الجمع ينتج متّصلة كلية مقدمها طرف مانعة الجمع لا غير إن كانت كل واحدة منهما موجبة كلية لما أن طرفها مستلزم لنقيض الأوسط المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية؛ وكون المستلزم للمستلزم للشيء مستلزما لذلك الشيء. ثمّ هذه المتّصلة ترتد إلى المنفصلة وهي مانعة الخلو من نقيض المقدم وعين التالي. وكذلك إلى مانعة الجمع من غير المقدم ونقيض التالي، وإن كانت إحدى مقدمتيه جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية مقدمها أي واحد كان من الطرفين؛ فإن كانت الكلية منهما حقيقية ينتجها بالذات لاستلزام طرف مانعة الجمع نقيض الأوسط استلزاما جزئيا المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية استلزاما كليًّا؛
[4] And the fourth division is that one of its two premises is a true conditional (haqiqi) and the other is an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam'); it yields a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the exclusive disjunctive only, if both premises are universal affirmative, because its side implies the negation of the middle term which implies the side of the true conditional; and because whatever implies the implication of something implies that thing. Then this conditional reduces to a disjunctive, which is a non-exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-khuluw) from the negation of the antecedent and the very consequent. And also to an exclusive disjunctive from the non-antecedent and the negation of the consequent. And if one of its two premises is particular, it yields a particular affirmative whose antecedent is whichever of the two sides; for if the universal among them is the true one, it produces it essentially because the side of the exclusive disjunctive implies the negation of the middle term with a particular implication, which implies the side of the true conditional with a universal implication.

Reducing Conditionals to Disjunctives
Author explains reductions of the derived conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a conditional proposition into its equivalent disjunctive forms (mani'at al-khuluw and mani'at al-jam').

Core Idea
A conditional 'if P then Q' can be expressed as 'not-P or Q' (non-exclusive) and also as 'P or not-Q' (exclusive).

After deriving the conclusion conditional, the author shows that it can be reduced to two disjunctive forms. This demonstrates the interconvertibility of logical forms, a key skill for manipulating propositions in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Start with the derived conditional
Take the conclusion: 'If C then B'. This is the conditional we want to convert.
2
Convert to mani'at al-khuluw (non-exclusive)
The conditional is equivalent to 'Either not-C or B', meaning at least one of 'not-C' or 'B' is true; they cannot both be false.
This is the material implication.
3
Convert to mani'at al-jam (exclusive)
The conditional is also equivalent to 'Either not-C or not-B' exclusively? Actually the author says: 'مانعة الجمع من غير المقدم ونقيض التالي' which is 'exclusive disjunction between non-antecedent and negation of consequent'. So it's '(not-C) XOR (not-B)', meaning exactly one is true.
This form is less common but useful for certain analyses.

Source Text

[4] And the fourth division is that one of its two premises is a true conditional (haqiqi) and the other is an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam'); it yields a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the exclusive disjunctive only, if both premises are universal affirmative, because its side implies the negation of the middle term which implies the side of the true conditional; and because whatever implies the implication of something implies that thing. B) Antecedent and consequent
C) Negation of antecedent and exact consequent
D) Antecedent and negation of consequent

Key Takeaway
A conditional can be expressed as two different disjunctive forms, expanding analytical options.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the conditional: 'If the prayer is invalid, it must be repeated.' Convert it to the two disjunctive forms (mani'at al-khuluw and mani'at al-jam) as described in the passage. Write both forms in Arabic and English.

Fourth Division with a Particular Premise
Author explains the particular case of the fourth division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the form of the conclusion when one premise in the fourth division is particular.

Core Idea
When one premise is particular, the conclusion is a particular conditional, and the antecedent can be chosen from either side.

Not all compound syllogisms have universal premises. The author also covers the case where one premise is particular. The resulting conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, with flexibility in picking the antecedent. This adjusts the logical strength of the inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify which premise is universal
Determine whether the haqiqi (true conditional) or the mani'at al-jam (exclusive disjunctive) is universal. The other is particular.
Universal means applies to all cases; particular applies to some cases.
2
Apply the rule for the universal case
If the universal is the haqiqi, then the conclusion's antecedent is the side of the disjunctive (as before), but now the implication is particular: 'Some C implies B'.
If the universal is the disjunctive, the conclusion's antecedent can be either side, as the implication is still particular.
3
Formulate the particular conclusion
The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional: 'Some individuals that satisfy the antecedent also satisfy the consequent.'
The exact phrasing depends on the logical quantifiers involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] والقسم الرابع هو أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه حقيقية، والأخرى مانعة الجمع ينتج متّصلة كلية مقدمها طرف مانعة الجمع لا غير إن كانت كل واحدة منهما موجبة كلية لما أن طرفها مستلزم لنقيض الأوسط المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية؛ وكون المستلزم للمستلزم للشيء مستلزما لذلك الشيء. ثمّ هذه المتّصلة ترتد إلى المنفصلة وهي مانعة الخلو من نقيض المقدم وعين التالي. وكذلك إلى مانعة الجمع من غير المقدم ونقيض التالي، وإن كانت إحدى مقدمتيه جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية مقدمها أي واحد كان من الطرفين؛ فإن كانت الكلية منهما حقيقية ينتجها بالذات لاستلزام طرف مانعة الجمع نقيض الأوسط استلزاما جزئيا المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية استلزاما كليًّا؛
[4] And the fourth division is that one of its two premises is a true conditional (haqiqi) and the other is an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam'); it yields a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the exclusive disjunctive only, if both premises are universal affirmative, because its side implies the negation of the middle term which implies the side of the true conditional; and because whatever implies the implication of something implies that thing. Then this conditional reduces to a disjunctive, which is a non-exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-khuluw) from the negation of the antecedent and the very consequent. And also to an exclusive disjunctive from the non-antecedent and the negation of the consequent. And if one of its two premises is particular, it yields a particular affirmative whose antecedent is whichever of the two sides; for if the universal among them is the true one, it produces it essentially because the side of the exclusive disjunctive implies the negation of the middle term with a particular implication, which implies the side of the true conditional with a universal implication.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth division of compound syllogisms where one premise is a true conditional (haqiqiyya, i.e., a necessary conditional like 'if A then B') and the other is an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam', meaning 'either but not both'). The author gives the rule: if both premises are universal affirmatives, the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the exclusive disjunctive. The justification is that the side of the exclusive disjunctive implies the negation of the middle term, which in turn implies the side of the true conditional; by transitivity of implication, the side of the exclusive disjunctive implies the side of the true conditional. The author then notes that this resulting conditional can be reduced to a disjunctive: specifically, to a non-exclusive disjunction between the negation of the antecedent and the consequent, and also to an exclusive disjunction between the non-antecedent and the negation of the consequent. This shows the equivalence between different logical forms. Next, if one premise is particular, the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, and its antecedent can be taken from either side. If the universal premise is the true conditional, the conclusion is produced essentially because the side of the exclusive disjunctive implies the negation of the middle term partially, which then implies the side of the true conditional universally. The author thus provides complete rules for this division.

Key Terms
جزئية
موجبة جزئية

Given a particular exclusive disjunctive: 'Some contracts are either valid or invalid (but not both).' and a universal true conditional: 'If a contract is valid, it is enforceable.' Determine the conclusion(s) and explain the role of particularity.

Universal Affirmative Premises in Mixed Compound Syllogisms
Universal affirmative premises produce universal conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to produce a universal conditional conclusion from two universal affirmative premises (one real conditional and one man'at al-jam'), and convert it to equivalent disjunctive forms and its contrapositive.

Core Idea
When a real conditional meets a man'at al-jam' disjunctive, the only certain conclusion is a universal conditional, not a disjunctive.

In the fourth division of mixed compound syllogisms, we combine a real conditional with a disjunctive that excludes combination. The author demonstrates that when both premises are universal affirmative, the necessary conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the disjunctive. This lesson walks through the underlying logic and shows how this conditional can be transformed into disjunctive forms and reduced by contraposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Ensure one premise is a real conditional (e.g., 'If something is a human, then it is rational') and the other is a man'at al-jam' disjunctive (e.g., 'Either it is a human or a horse, not both'). Both must be universal affirmative.
The real conditional is called 'haqiqiyya' and the disjunctive is 'man'at al-jam'.
2
Trace the entailment chain
The side of the man'at al-jam' (e.g., 'it is a human') entails the negation of the other side ('it is not a horse'). This negation then entails the side of the real conditional (e.g., 'it is rational'? Wait, the real conditional's side is its antecedent? Actually, the side of the real conditional in the chain is the antecedent of the conditional? Let's use the text's variables: Let the real conditional be 'If B then C' and the disjunctive be 'Either A or D'. Then side A entails not-D, which entails B (the antecedent of the conditional). Thus A entails B.
The middle term here is D (the other side of the disjunctive).
3
Formulate the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the man'at al-jam' (A) and whose consequent is the side of the real conditional (B): 'If A then B'.
The text says 'mantiqiyya kulliyya muqaddamuha tarafu man'at al-jam' la ghayr'.
4
Convert the conditional to a man'at al-khuluww disjunctive
The conditional 'If A then B' is equivalent to a man'at al-khuluww (at least one true) disjunctive: 'Either not A or B'.
This is the first conversion mentioned in the text.
5
Convert the conditional to a man'at al-jam' disjunctive
The same conditional is also equivalent to a man'at al-jam' (not both true) disjunctive: 'Either A or not B' (the text says 'from non-antecedent and negation of consequent' i.e., 'Either not A or not B' – but careful: the standard equivalence is 'Either A or not B' as man'at al-jam'. Follow the text: it says 'man'at al-jam' min ghayr al-muqaddam wa naqid al-tali' which gives 'Either not A or not B'. However, this may be a specific interpretation; for this lesson, we adopt the text's statement.
The text also provides a second conversion to a man'at al-jam' of the non-antecedent and negation of the consequent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] والقسم الرابع هو أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه حقيقية، والأخرى مانعة الجمع ينتج متّصلة كلية مقدمها طرف مانعة الجمع لا غير إن كانت كل واحدة منهما موجبة كلية لما أن طرفها مستلزم لنقيض الأوسط المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية؛ وكون المستلزم للمستلزم للشيء مستلزما لذلك الشيء. ثمّ هذه المتّصلة ترتد إلى المنفصلة وهي مانعة الخلو من نقيض المقدم وعين التالي. وكذلك إلى مانعة الجمع من غير المقدم ونقيض التالي، وإن كانت إحدى مقدمتيه جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية مقدمها أي واحد كان من الطرفين؛ فإن كانت الكلية منهما حقيقية ينتجها بالذات لاستلزام طرف مانعة الجمع نقيض الأوسط استلزاما جزئيا المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية استلزاما كليًّا؛ والإنتاج من الأول. وإن كانت الكلية منهما مانعة الجمع فينتج بالذات موجبة جزئية من نقيضي الطرفين من الأول لاستلزام نقيض طرف الحقيقية للأوسط استلزاما جزئيا المستلزم لنقيض طرف مانعة الجمع استلزاما كليًّا، أو من الثالث لاستلزام عين الأوسط لكل واحد من نقيضي الطرفين؛ ولا يرتد كل واحدة من هاتين المتصلتين إلى ما يلزمها من المانعة للجمع والمانعة للخلو. وإن أردت أن تردهما إلى المتصلة من الطرفين فالطريق أن تردها إلى مانعة الجمع؛ ثمَّ إلى المتّصلة من الطرفين كما يقال مثلا قد يكون إذا لم يكن «أب» لم يكن هـز يلزمه إما ليس أب» أو «هـز»، وحينئذ يلزم قد يكون إذا كان هـز فـ «أب». وهكذا يتبين انعكاس الموجبة الكلية بعكس النقيض إلى موجبة كلية مقدمها نقيض التالي وتاليها نقيض المقدم. وأما إذا كانت إحديهما سالبة فإن كانت هي الحقيقية لم ينتج لصدق القياس مع تعاند الطرفين تارة ومع التلازم أخرى: أما مع التعاند فكقولنا هذا الشيء إما أن يكون إنسانًا وإما أن يكون فرسا، وليس البتة إما أن يكون فرسًا أو لا يكون إنسانًا حقيقيًا مع التعاند [...] بين الإنسان ونقيضه. وأما مع التلازم فكقولنا [...] هذا الشيء إما أن يكون إنسانًا وإما أن يكون فرسا، وليس [...] البتة إما أن يكون فرسًا أو ناطقا حقيقيًا مع التلازم بين الإنسان والناطق.
[4] The fourth division is that one of its two premises is a real conditional, and the other a man'at al-jam', producing a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the man'at al-jam' only, if each of them is universal affirmative, because its side entails the negation of the middle which entails the side of the real conditional; and the entailment of that which entails something is entailed by that thing. Then this conditional converts to a disjunctive which is man'at al-khuluww from the negation of the antecedent and the very consequent. And also to a man'at al-jam' of the non-antecedent and the negation of the consequent. And if one of its two premises is particular, it produces a particular affirmative whose antecedent is whichever of the two sides; so if the universal among them is the real conditional, it produces it essentially because the side of the man'at al-jam' entails the negation of the middle in a particular entailment which entails the side of the real conditional in a universal entailment; and the production is from the first. And if the universal among them is the man'at al-jam', then it produces essentially a particular affirmative from the two negations of the sides from the first because the negation of the side of the real conditional entails the middle in a particular entailment which entails the negation of the side of the man'at al-jam' in a universal entailment, or from the third because the very middle entails each of the two negations of the sides; and each of these two conditionals does not convert to what entails them from the two man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluww. And if you want to reduce them to a conditional from two sides, the way is to reduce them to man'at al-jam'; then to a conditional from two sides, as one says for example: it may be that if not 'A b' then not H z, it entails either not 'A b' or 'H z'; and then it entails it may be that if 'H z' then 'A b'. And thus is shown the conversion of the universal affirmative by contraposition to a universal affirmative whose antecedent is the negation of the consequent and whose consequent is the negation of the antecedent. And if one of them is negative, then if it is the real conditional, it does not produce because of the truth of the syllogism with mutual opposition sometimes and with mutual entailment at other times: as for mutual opposition, like our statement: this thing is either a human or a horse, and not at all either it is a horse or it is not a human really with mutual opposition between human and its negation. And as for mutual entailment, like our statement: this thing is either a human or a horse, and not at all either it is a horse or rational really with mutual entailment between human and rational.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the fourth division of mixed compound syllogisms, where one premise is a real conditional (حقيقية) and the other is a 'man'at al-jam'' disjunctive (i.e., a disjunctive that excludes combination of both disjuncts). The author explains the conditions under which a conclusion is produced. When both premises are universal affirmative, the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the man'at al-jam' only. This is because the side of the man'at al-jam' necessitates the negation of the middle term, which in turn necessitates the side of the real conditional. The author then shows how this universal conditional can be converted into a man'at al-khuluww disjunctive (exclusion of both being false) and a man'at al-jam' of the non-antecedent and negation of consequent. If one premise is particular, the conclusion is a particular affirmative, and the author details which premise is universal leads to which production. Additionally, the passage discusses reduction to a conditional from two sides via man'at al-jam', demonstrating the principle of contraposition for universal affirmatives. Finally, the author considers the case where one premise is negative: if the real conditional is negative, no conclusion follows, and examples show why, whether due to mutual opposition or mutual entailment. The examples use categories from the Islamic tradition, such as 'human', 'horse', and 'rational', to illustrate logical relations.

مانعة الخلو

Particular and Negative Premises in Mixed Compound Syllogisms
Particular and negative premises produce different outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion when one premise is particular and identify that no conclusion follows when the real conditional is negative.

Core Idea
A particular premise yields a particular conditional; a negative real conditional yields no conclusion at all.

The author continues the discussion of the fourth division by examining cases where one premise is particular or negative. When one premise is particular, the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional. However, if the real conditional is negative, no conclusion follows, and the reasons are illustrated using examples from the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particular case
When one premise is particular (e.g., 'Some A is B' or 'Some C is D'), the author states that the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional. The antecedent of this conditional can be taken from either side of the disjunctive or the conditional.
The text says: 'yuqayyada mujaba juz'iya muqaddamuha ayyu wahidin kana min al-tarafayn'.
2
Determine which premise is universal
If the universal premise is the real conditional, the conclusion follows essentially from the first figure (the side of the man'at al-jam' entails the negation of the middle in a particular entailment, which entails the side of the real conditional universally). If the universal premise is the man'at al-jam', the conclusion follows from the first or third figure.
The text details two subcases: (a) real conditional is universal, (b) man'at al-jam' is universal.
3
Note the non-convertibility of these conditionals
Unlike the universal case, the conditionals resulting from particular premises do not convert to the man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluww forms. The author says: 'wa la yartaddu kullu wahida min hataini al-muttasilataini ila ma yalzimuha min al-mani'at al-jam' wa al-mani'at al-khuluww'.
This is because the entailment is only particular, not universal.
4
Handle the negative premise
If one premise is negative and it is the real conditional, no conclusion follows. This is because the syllogism would be true in cases of mutual opposition (ta'and) or mutual entailment (talazum) between the sides, rendering it invalid.
The author gives two examples: one with mutual opposition between human and horse, and one with mutual entailment between human and rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] والقسم الرابع هو أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه حقيقية، والأخرى مانعة الجمع ينتج متّصلة كلية مقدمها طرف مانعة الجمع لا غير إن كانت كل واحدة منهما موجبة كلية لما أن طرفها مستلزم لنقيض الأوسط المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية؛ وكون المستلزم للمستلزم للشيء مستلزما لذلك الشيء. ثمّ هذه المتّصلة ترتد إلى المنفصلة وهي مانعة الخلو من نقيض المقدم وعين التالي. وكذلك إلى مانعة الجمع من غير المقدم ونقيض التالي، وإن كانت إحدى مقدمتيه جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية مقدمها أي واحد كان من الطرفين؛ فإن كانت الكلية منهما حقيقية ينتجها بالذات لاستلزام طرف مانعة الجمع نقيض الأوسط استلزاما جزئيا المستلزم لطرف الحقيقية استلزاما كليًّا؛ والإنتاج من الأول. وإن كانت الكلية منهما مانعة الجمع فينتج بالذات موجبة جزئية من نقيضي الطرفين من الأول لاستلزام نقيض طرف الحقيقية للأوسط استلزاما جزئيا المستلزم لنقيض طرف مانعة الجمع استلزاما كليًّا، أو من الثالث لاستلزام عين الأوسط لكل واحد من نقيضي الطرفين؛ ولا يرتد كل واحدة من هاتين المتصلتين إلى ما يلزمها من المانعة للجمع والمانعة للخلو. وإن أردت أن تردهما إلى المتصلة من الطرفين فالطريق أن تردها إلى مانعة الجمع؛ ثمَّ إلى المتّصلة من الطرفين كما يقال مثلا قد يكون إذا لم يكن «أب» لم يكن هـز يلزمه إما ليس أب» أو «هـز»، وحينئذ يلزم قد يكون إذا كان هـز فـ «أب». وهكذا يتبين انعكاس الموجبة الكلية بعكس النقيض إلى موجبة كلية مقدمها نقيض التالي وتاليها نقيض المقدم. وأما إذا كانت إحديهما سالبة فإن كانت هي الحقيقية لم ينتج لصدق القياس مع تعاند الطرفين تارة ومع التلازم أخرى: أما مع التعاند فكقولنا هذا الشيء إما أن يكون إنسانًا وإما أن يكون فرسا، وليس البتة إما أن يكون فرسًا أو لا يكون إنسانًا حقيقيًا مع التعاند [...] بين الإنسان ونقيضه. وأما مع التلازم فكقولنا [...] هذا الشيء إما أن يكون إنسانًا وإما أن يكون فرسا، وليس [...] البتة إما أن يكون فرسًا أو ناطقا حقيقيًا مع التلازم بين الإنسان والناطق.
[4] The fourth division is that one of its two premises is a real conditional, and the other a man'at al-jam', producing a universal conditional whose antecedent is the side of the man'at al-jam' only, if each of them is universal affirmative, because its side entails the negation of the middle which entails the side of the real conditional; and the entailment of that which entails something is entailed by that thing. C) No conclusion
D) A universal conditional

Key Takeaway
A particular premise yields a particular conditional, but a negative real conditional yields no conclusion at all.

Valid Inference from Haqiqiyya and Mani'at al-Jam' Conditionals
Compound conditionals with haqiqiyya and mani'at al-jam'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a compound conditional with a haqiqiyya and a mani'at al-jam' premise yields a valid conclusion, and formulate the conclusion appropriately.

Core Idea
In compound conditionals, the sign of the mani'at al-jam' premise determines whether a conclusion follows.

In this passage, the author examines combinations of two conditional propositions: one haqiqiyya (true conditional) and one mani'at al-jam' (exclusive disjunction). Understanding when these combinations produce conclusions is crucial for constructing valid arguments from disjunctive premises in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the haqiqiyya conditional and the mani'at al-jam' disjunction in the argument. Note their quantities and signs (affirmative or negative).
2
Check the sign of the mani'at al-jam'
If the mani'at al-jam' is affirmative, the combination can produce a conclusion; if negative, it also can produce a conclusion but of a different form. If the haqiqiyya is negative, no conclusion follows.
3
Apply the production rule for affirmative premises
When both are affirmative and universal, the conclusion is a particular affirmative proposition whose subject and predicate are the contradictories of the original extremes. Derive it via the first or third figure.
The passage details two methods: from the first figure using the contradictory of the haqiqiyya extreme, or from the third figure using the middle term.
4
Apply the production rule for a negative mani'at al-jam'
If the mani'at al-jam' is negative (while the haqiqiyya is affirmative), the conclusion is a particular negative whose antecedent is the extreme of the mani'at al-jam' only.
The passage gives an example with variables.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأما إذا كانت إحديهما سالبة فإن كانت هي الحقيقية لم ينتج لصدق القياس مع تعاند الطرفين تارة ومع التلازم :أخرى: أما مع التعاند فكقولنا هذا الشيء إما أن يكون إنسانًا وإما أن يكون فرسا، وليس البتة إما أن يكون فرسًا أو لا يكون إنسانًا حقيقيًا مع التعاند [؟] بين الإنسان ونقيضه. وأما مع التلازم فكقولنا [؟] هذا الشيء إما أن يكون إنسانًا وإما أن يكون فرسا، وليس [؟] البتة إما أن يكون فرسًا أو ناطقا حقيقيًا مع التلازم بين الإنسان والناطق. وأما إذا كانت السالبة مانعة الجمع كلية كانت أو جزئية ينتج سالبة جزئية مقدمها طرف مانعة الجمع لا غير كما في قولنا دائما إما أن يكون «أب» أو «ج د» حقيقيًا، وقد لا يكون إما «ج د» أو «هز» ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان «هـ ز » فــ «أب» وإلا كلما كان «هز» فـ «أب» فدائما إما «ج د» أو «هز» مانعة الجمع لامتناع ج د مع «أب» اللازم له ز » . وإن كانت الحقيقية كلية فإنه يتبين بهذا الطريق إذا صدق كلما كان «هـ ز » فـ «أب»، وكلَّما كان «أب» فليس «ج د» ينتج كلَّما كان «هـز» فليس «ج د»؛ ويلزمه دائما إما «هز» أو «ج د» مانعة الجمع، هذا خلف. وأما أنها لا يصدق ومقدمها طرف الحقيقية فلاحتمال أن يكون نقيض الأوسط الذي هو طرف الحقيقية أو ما يساويه أخص من طرف مانعة الجمع كما في قولنا دائما إما أن يكون هذا الشيء حيواناً أو ليس بحيوان، وليس البتة إما أن لا يكون حيوانًا أو جسما فإنه لا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كان حيوانا كان جسما.
And the production is from the first. And if the universal of them is mani'at al-jam', then it produces essentially a particular affirmative from the contradictories of the two extremes from the first, because the contradictory of the haqiqiyya extreme necessitates the middle term with partial necessity, which necessitates the contradictory of the extreme of mani'at al-jam' with universal necessity; or from the third because the middle term itself necessitates each of the contradictories of the two extremes. And each of these two conditionals does not reduce to what necessitates them from mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw. And if you want to reduce them to the conditional of the two extremes, the way is to reduce them to mani'at al-jam', then to the conditional of the two extremes, as it is said for example: 'It may be that if not A B then not H Z' implies 'Either not A B or H Z', and then it implies 'It may be that if H Z then A B'. And thus the conversion of the universal affirmative by contraposition into a universal affirmative whose antecedent is the contradictory of the consequent and whose consequent is the contradictory of the antecedent is shown.
And as for if one of them is negative, if it is the haqiqiyya, it does not produce because the syllogism is true with the opposition of the extremes sometimes and with entailment other times: as for with opposition, like our saying: 'This thing is either a human or a horse, and it is not absolutely [the case] that it is either a horse or not a human' haqiqiyya with opposition between human and its contradictory. And as for with entailment, like our saying: [...] 'This thing is either a human or a horse, and it is not [...] absolutely [the case] that it is either a horse or rational' haqiqiyya with entailment between human and rational. And as for if the negative is mani'at al-jam', whether universal or particular, it produces a particular negative whose antecedent is the extreme of mani'at al-jam' only, as in our saying: 'Always either A B or C D' haqiqiyya, and 'It may not be either C D or H Z' produces 'It may not be that if H Z then A B' and otherwise 'Whenever H Z then A B', so always 'Either C D or H Z' mani'at al-jam' because C D is impossible with A B entailing H Z. And if the haqiqiyya is universal, then it is shown by this way: if 'Whenever H Z then A B' is true, and 'Whenever A B then not C D' is true, it produces 'Whenever H Z then not C D'; and it implies always 'Either H Z or C D' mani'at al-jam', this is a reductio. And as for that it is not true with its antecedent being the extreme of the haqiqiyya, because it is possible that the contradictory of the middle term, which is the extreme of the haqiqiyya, or its equivalent is more specific than the extreme of mani'at al-jam', as in our saying: 'Always either this thing is an animal or not an animal, and it is not absolutely [the case] that it is either [not] an animal or a body' then it is not true that 'It may not be that if it is an animal it is a body'.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion of compound conditionals where one premise is a haqiqiyya (true conditional) and the other is a mani'at al-jam' (exclusive disjunction, 'impossible both true'). The author explains the conditions under which such compounds yield conclusions. First, if both are universal affirmative, the conclusion is a particular affirmative from the contradictories of the two extremes, derived from the first figure (since the contradictory of the haqiqiyya extreme entails the middle partially, which entails the contradictory of the mani'at al-jam' extreme universally). Alternatively, from the third figure, the middle term itself necessitates each contradictory. The author notes that these conditionals do not reduce to their corresponding mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw forms. He then provides a method to reduce them to a conditional form via mani'at al-jam', illustrating with an example using variables A B and H Z, showing the contraposition equivalence. 

Next, he considers when one premise is negative. If the haqiqiyya is negative, no conclusion follows, because the syllogism can be true in cases of either opposition or entailment between the extremes. He gives examples: with opposition, using 'human' and 'horse' as opposites; with entailment, using 'human' and 'rational' where one entails the other. These cases show that the premises can be true without guaranteeing a conclusion. If the negative is the mani'at al-jam' (whether universal or particular), then a particular negative conclusion with the antecedent being the extreme of the mani'at al-jam' is produced. He illustrates with an example: 'Always either A B or C D' (haqiqiyya) and 'It may not be either C D or H Z' yields 'It may not be that if H Z then A B'; otherwise the universal 'Whenever H Z then A B' would hold, contradicting the second premise. He further discusses when the haqiqiyya is universal, showing a valid derivation but then notes a case where the conclusion does not hold because the contradictory of the middle term might be more specific than the extreme of mani'at al-jam', using an example with 'animal' and 'body': the universal 'always either animal or not animal' and the negative 'not absolutely not-animal or body' does not yield 'it may not be that if animal then body' because the contradictory of 'animal' (i.e., 'not animal') is not equivalent to 'body'.

Deriving Conclusions from Haqiqiyya and Mani'at al-Khuluw
Author discusses the fifth section on compound conditionals with haqiqiyya and mani'at al-khuluw premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a compound conditional when one premise is haqiqiyya and the other is mani'at al-khuluw, both universal affirmative, and explain why the antecedent must come from the haqiqiyya extreme.

Core Idea
The haqiqiyya extreme must be the antecedent, lest the disjunction become a conditional.

In this passage, the author explains how to combine a true conditional (haqiqiyya) with an exhaustive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw) when both premises are universal affirmatives. We will walk through the logical steps—identifying the extremes and middle term, applying the first figure, and seeing why the alternative arrangement leads to a contradiction (khulf). This technique is the backbone of many compound arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the haqiqiyya (true conditional) and the mani'at al-khuluw (exhaustive disjunction). The haqiqiyya has an antecedent (A) and consequent (B); the mani'at al-khuluw has two disjuncts (C and D) of which at least one is true.
Both premises are universal affirmative in the case we treat first.
2
Extract the extremes and middle term
Determine the three terms: the extreme of the haqiqiyya (call it T1), the extreme of the mani'at al-khuluw (T2), and the middle term (M). The haqiqiyya asserts that T1 implies ¬M (its consequent is the contradictory of M). The mani'at al-khuluw asserts that ¬M implies T2 (since it disjoins T2 and something else, but in the reasoning ¬M leads to T2).
The middle term M is the term that appears in both premises in a linked way.
3
Apply the first figure
Treat the chain as a categorical syllogism in the first figure: Major premise: ¬M implies T2 (from mani'at al-khuluw). Minor premise: T1 implies ¬M (from haqiqiyya). Since the middle term ¬M is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, it fits the first figure. Conclusion: T1 implies T2, i.e., the haqiqiyya extreme implies the mani'at extreme.
The author says 'al-intaj min al-awwal' (production from the first).
4
Understand why the opposite fails
If we tried to make the antecedent the mani'at extreme, the reasoning would require that the mani'at extreme implies ¬M, but then the mani'at al-khuluw would become a haqiqiyya (a true conditional), contradicting its definition. This is proven by reductio (khulf).
This ensures the conclusion's antecedent is uniquely determined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الخامس هو أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه حقيقية، والأخرى مانعة الخلو ؛ فإن كانت كل واحدة منهما موجبة كلية ينتج موجبة كلية من الطرفين مقدمها طرف الحقيقية لاستلزامه نقيض الأوسط المستلزم لطرف مانعة الخلو؛ والإنتاج من الأول. ولا تصدق هي ومقدمها طرف مانعة الخلو، ولا استلزام طرفيها لنقيض الأوسط لاستلزام طرف الحقيقية إياه فيمتنع الجمع بين طرفي مانعة الخلو لاستلزام المتّصلة الموجبة امتناع الجمع بين مقدمها ونقيض تاليها؛ فيلزم أن تكون مانعة الخلوّ حقيقية، هذا خلف. وأما المتصلة الكلية من نقيضي الطرفين فإنه ينتجها ومقدمها طرف غير الحقيقية من الشكل الأول، والأوسط هو الأوسط بعينه؛ ثم ترتد كل واحدة من هاتين المتصلتين إلى المانعة للخلو والمانعة للجمع. وكذلك المتصلة من نقيضي الطرفين إليها من الطرفين بالطريق الذي مرَّ ذكره في القسم الثاني. وأما إذا كانت إحدى الموجبتين جزئية فإنه ينتج موجبة جزئية من الطرفين ومقدمها أي واحد كان من الطرفين والإنتاج من الأول إذا كانت الجزئية حقيقية ومقدمها
And the fifth section is that one of its two premises is haqiqiyya and the other is mani'at al-khuluw. Then if each of them is universal affirmative, it produces a universal affirmative from the two extremes whose antecedent is the extreme of the haqiqiyya, because it necessitates the contradictory of the middle term which necessitates the extreme of mani'at al-khuluw; and the production is from the first. And it is not true with its antecedent being the extreme of mani'at al-khuluw, nor is the necessity of its two extremes for the contradictory of the middle term, because the extreme of the haqiqiyya necessitates it, so the conjunction of the two extremes of mani'at al-khuluw is impossible, because the affirmative conditional necessitates the impossibility of conjunction between its antecedent and the contradictory of its consequent; so it would follow that mani'at al-khuluw is haqiqiyya, which is a reductio. And as for the universal conditional from the contradictories of the two extremes, it produces it with its antecedent being the extreme other than the haqiqiyya from the first figure, and the middle term is the very same middle; then each of these two conditionals is reduced to mani'at al-khuluw and mani'at al-jam'. Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is the fifth section of a treatise on compound conditionals, specifically when one premise is a haqiqiyya (true conditional) and the other is a mani'at al-khuluw (exhaustive disjunction, 'impossible both false'). The author first considers the case where both are universal affirmatives. In that case, a universal affirmative conclusion is produced from the two extremes, with the antecedent being the extreme of the haqiqiyya. The reasoning: the haqiqiyya extreme necessitates the contradictory of the middle term, and that contradictory in turn necessitates the extreme of the mani'at al-khuluw. This conclusion is derived from the first figure. The author then argues that the conclusion cannot have its antecedent as the extreme of the mani'at al-khuluw, because that would lead to a contradiction: it would make the mani'at al-khuluw equivalent to a haqiqiyya conditional, which is impossible (a reductio ad absurdum). The explanation involves the property that an affirmative conditional implies the impossibility of the conjunction of its antecedent and the contradictory of its consequent. 

Next, he addresses the universal conditional formed from the contradictories of the two extremes. This is also produced from the first figure, with the antecedent being the non-haqiqiyya extreme, and the middle term unchanged. These two conditionals (the original and the one from contradictories) can be reduced to mani'at al-khuluw and mani'at al-jam' forms, using the method mentioned in the second section. Finally, he considers the case where one of the affirmative premises is particular. Then a particular affirmative conclusion is produced from the two extremes, with the antecedent being either extreme; the derivation is from the first figure provided the particular premise is haqiqiyya. The passage breaks off there, likely continuing on the next page.
