8 min
Inference from Universal Conditional and Disjunctive Premises
Author explains derivation and failure condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid categorical syllogism equivalent to a conditional inference involving a universal haqīqiyyah and a māni'at al-jam', and identify when the inference fails.

Core Idea
A universal haqīqiyyah and māni'at al-jam' yield a valid Celarent syllogism unless the middle term's contradictory is too specific.

In this passage, the author explains how to derive a conclusion when one premise is a universal haqīqiyyah (absolute conditional) and the other is a māni'at al-jam' (disjunctive preventing conjunction). By converting the conditionals into categorical form, we uncover a syllogism of the first figure (Celarent). However, a subtle counterexample shows that the inference fails when the contradictory of the middle term is more specific than the side of the māni'at al-jam'. This lesson guides you through the logical mechanics and the crucial failure condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the universal haqīqiyyah (e.g., 'every time HZ is AB') and the māni'at al-jam' (e.g., 'every time AB is not JD'). Note that the māni'at al-jam' here is expressed as a universal negative conditional.
The māni'at al-jam' may appear as a disjunctive statement but is used in the syllogism as a negative conditional.
2
Convert conditionals to categorical statements
Treat 'every time HZ is AB' as 'All HZ are AB' and 'every time AB is not JD' as 'No AB are JD'. This conversion is standard in Islamic logic for conditional syllogisms.
The conversion assumes the conditionals are universal and unrestricted.
3
Arrange in categorical syllogism form
Place the premises as minor (All HZ are AB) and major (No AB are JD). The middle term is AB. This yields a first-figure Celarent syllogism with the conclusion 'No HZ are JD', i.e., 'All HZ are not JD'.
Check that the terms are correctly distributed to ensure validity.
4
Test the validity
A Celarent syllogism is valid by the conditions of the first figure: the major premise is universal negative, the minor is universal affirmative, and the conclusion is universal negative. This matches the author's derivation.
The author also notes that the conclusion entails a māni'at al-jam' between HZ and JD.
5
Recognize the failure condition
The author warns that the inference fails when the contradictory of the middle term (here, the side of the haqīqiyyah) is more specific than the other side. In the example, the haqīqiyyah 'always either animal or not animal' is a tautology, and the māni'at al-jam' 'not at all either not animal or body' does not allow the expected conclusion.
The failure arises from a mismatch in specificity of the terms, preventing the proper categorical conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت الحقيقية كلية فإنه يتبين بهذا الطريق إذا صدق كلما كان «هـ ز» فـ «أب»، وكلَّما كان «أب» فليس «ج د» ينتج كلَّما كان «هـ ز» فليس «ج د»؛ ويلزمه دائما إما «هز» أو «ج د» مانعة الجمع، هذا خلف. وأما أنها لا يصدق ومقدمها طرف الحقيقية فلاحتمال أن يكون نقيض الأوسط الذي هو طرف الحقيقية أو ما يساويه أخص من طرف مانعة الجمع كما في قولنا دائما إما أن يكون هذا الشيء حيواناً أو ليس بحيوان، وليس البتة إما أن لا يكون حيوانًا أو جسما فإنه لا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كان حيوانا كان جسما.
And if the haqīqiyyah is universal, then it is shown by this path: if 'every time HZ is AB' is true, and 'every time AB is not JD' is true, then it yields 'every time HZ is not JD'; and it always entails that either HZ or JD is a māni'at al-jam', this is a contradiction. As for that it does not hold with its premise being the side of the haqīqiyyah, it is because it is possible that the contradictory of the middle term which is the side of the haqīqiyyah, or what is equal to it, is more specific than the side of the māni'at al-jam', as in our saying: always either this thing is an animal or not an animal, and not at all either that it is not an animal or a body; for it is not true that it may be that when it is an animal it is a body.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case in conditional syllogisms: when one premise is a universal haqīqiyyah (absolute conditional) and the other is a māni'at al-jam' (preventive of togetherness disjunctive). The author explains that if the haqīqiyyah premise is universal, then a conclusion can be derived by reductio ad absurdum. If we assume the opposite of the desired conclusion, it leads to a contradiction with the given premises. However, the author then notes a potential failure: the method does not work if the premise is taken as the side (ṭaraf) of the haqīqiyyah, because the contradictory of the middle term might be more specific than the side of the māni'at al-jam'. An example is given: 'always either this thing is an animal or not an animal' (a haqīqiyyah) and 'not at all either that it is not an animal or a body' (a māni'at al-jam'). In this case, the conclusion 'it may be that when it is an animal it is a body' is not true, because the middle term's contradictory (animal vs. not animal) is more specific than the body term. This illustrates the conditions under which the inference is valid.

Key Terms
حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
طرف
نقيض الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the inference from a universal haqīqiyyah and a māni'at al-jam' to be invalid?
A) The haqīqiyyah must be universal
B) The middle term's contradictory is more specific than the side of the māni'at al-jam'
C) The māni'at al-jam' must be universal
D) The conclusion must be a māni'at al-jam' itself

Key Takeaway
A universal haqīqiyyah paired with a māni'at al-jam' yields a valid Celarent syllogism only when the contradictory of the middle term is not more specific than the other side of the disjunction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Convert the following conditional argument into a categorical syllogism and determine its validity: 'Every time the Prophet (ﷺ) made a statement, he spoke the truth; every time he spoke the truth, it was from Allah; therefore every time he made a statement, it was from Allah.' Identify the figure and mood.
?
Task: Consider the counterexample from the passage: 'Always either this thing is an animal or it is not an animal; and not at all either it is not an animal or it is a body'. Explain why the conclusion 'if it is an animal, then it is a body' does not follow, and relate it to the condition about the contradictory of the middle term.

Universal Affirmative Mixed Syllogism
First subcase of the fifth division: both premises universal affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the universal affirmative conclusion when both premises are universal affirmative in a mixed haqīqiyyah-māni'at al-khuluw syllogism.

Core Idea
When both premises are universal affirmative, the conclusion's antecedent is the side of the haqīqiyyah.

This lesson covers the first subcase of the fifth division: both premises universal affirmative. The author explains how the entailment from the side of the haqīqiyyah through the contradictory of the middle term produces a universal affirmative conditional. Understanding this chain is key to deriving correct conclusions in mixed conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the haqīqiyyah conditional (e.g., 'If A then B') and the māni'at al-khuluw disjunctive (e.g., 'Either C or D, at least one true'). Both are universal affirmative.
The haqīqiyyah is an absolute conditional; the māni'at al-khuluw is an inclusive disjunction.
2
Extract the sides (aṭrāf)
Determine the 'side' of the haqīqiyyah (its antecedent or consequent that will appear in the conclusion) and the sides of the māni'at al-khuluw (its two disjuncts).
The side of the haqīqiyyah is the term that does not correspond to the middle.
3
Trace the entailment chain
The side of the haqīqiyyah entails the contradictory of the middle term. This contradictory then entails one of the sides of the māni'at al-khuluw.
The middle term connects the two premises through entailments.
4
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional with the side of the haqīqiyyah as antecedent and the side of the māni'at al-khuluw as consequent.
This is derived via the first figure using the contradictory of the middle as the mean.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المتصلتين إلى المانعة للخلو والمانعة للجمع. وكذلك المتصلة من نقيضي الطرفين إليها من الطرفين بالطريق الذي مرَّ ذكره في القسم الثاني. وأما إذا كانت إحدى الموجبتين جزئية فإنه ينتج موجبة جزئية من الطرفين حيوانا حقيقيا / مع التعاند بين الحيوان ونقيضه. وأما مع التلازم فكقولنا إما أن يكون هذا الشيء حيوانا وإما أن لا يكون إنسانًا، وليس البتة إما أن يكون حيواناً أو لا ناطقا حقيقيا مع التلازم بين اللا إنسان واللا ناطق وإن كانت السالبة مانعة الخلو كلية كانت أو جزئية ينتج سالبة مقدمها طرف الحقيقية لا طرف مانعة الخلو. فإنا إذا قلنا دائما إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن يكون ج د حقيقية، وليس البتة إما أن يكون جد) وإما أن يكون هز» ينتج قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ هـز». وذلك يفضي إلى امتناع الخلو عن (ج) (د) وعن هـز ؛ وذلك محال؛ إذ هما طرفا مانعة الخلو كما مر. The fifth division is that one of the two premises is haqīqiyyah and the other is māni'at al-khuluw. If each of them is universal affirmative, it produces a universal affirmative from both sides with its premise being the side of the haqīqiyyah, because it entails the contradictory of the middle which entails the side of the māni'at al-khuluw; and the inference is from the first figure. And it [the conclusion] is not true with its premise being the side of the māni'at al-khuluw, nor is there entailment of its two sides to the contradictory of the middle because the side of the haqīqiyyah entails it, so conjunction between the two sides of the māni'at al-khuluw is prevented because the affirmative conditional entails prevention of conjunction between its antecedent and the contradictory of its consequent; so it would follow that the māni'at al-khuluw is haqīqiyyah, which is a contradiction. As for the universal conditional from the contradictories of the two sides, it is produced with its premise being the side of the non-haqīqiyyah from the first figure, and the middle is the same middle; then each of these two conditionals is converted to the māni'at al-khuluw and māni'at al-jam'. Similarly, the conditional from the contradictories of the two sides to it from the two sides by the path mentioned in the second division. And if one of the two affirmatives is particular, it produces a particular affirmative from the two sides, and its premise is whichever of the two sides, and the inference is from the first figure if the particular is haqīqiyyah and its premise is the side of the haqīqiyyah. And also from the third; and the middle is the contradictory of the middle. Then it is converted to the two aforementioned disjunctives. And if one of them is negative, then if it is the haqīqiyyah, it does not produce because the syllogism is true with opposition (ta'ānud) between the two sides sometimes and with entailment (talāzum) other times. As for with opposition, like our saying: always either this thing is an animal or not a human, and not at all either that it is not a human or not an animal, haqīqiyyah, with opposition between animal and its contradictory. As for with entailment, like our saying: either this thing is an animal or not a human, and not at all either that it is animal or not a rational, haqīqiyyah, with entailment between not-human and not-rational. And if the negative is māni'at al-khuluw, universal or particular, it produces a negative whose premise is the side of the haqīqiyyah, not the side of the māni'at al-khuluw. For if we say: always either AB or JD, haqīqiyyah, and not at all either JD or HZ, it produces our saying: it may be that when it is AB then HZ; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely our saying: every time AB is HZ. And that leads to prevention of emptiness from (J) (D) and from HZ; and that is impossible, since they are the two sides of the māni'at al-khuluw as passed. And as for it not being true with its premise being the side of the māni'at al-khuluw, it is because it is possible that the contradictory of the middle which is the side of the haqīqiyyah, or what is equal to it, is more general than the side of the non-haqīqiyyah, as in our saying: this thing is either an animal or not an animal, and not at all either not an animal or a human; then it is not true that it may be that when it is a human it is an animal.

Commentary
This passage covers the fifth division of mixed conditional syllogisms, where one premise is a haqīqiyyah (absolute conditional) and the other is a māni'at al-khuluw (disjunctive preventing emptyness, i.e., at least one must be true). The author examines several subcases. First, when both premises are universal affirmative: the conclusion is a universal affirmative with the premise being the side of the haqīqiyyah, derived via the first figure. The conclusion cannot have its premise as the side of the māni'at al-khuluw, because that would lead to a contradiction (the māni'at al-khuluw would become haqīqiyyah). Next, the author discusses deriving a universal conditional from the contradictories of the two sides, and converting it to disjunctive forms. Then the case of one particular affirmative: it yields a particular affirmative. If one premise is negative, the author distinguishes: if the negative is haqīqiyyah, no conclusion follows because the syllogism can be true with either opposition or entailment between the sides. If the negative is māni'at al-khuluw, a negative conclusion follows with the premise as the side of the haqīqiyyah. The author provides examples using animal, human, and rational terms to illustrate the failure conditions when the middle term's contradictory is more general. Throughout, the key is the relationship between the contradictory of the middle term and the sides of the disjunctives.

Key Terms
حقيقية
مانعة الخلو
استلزام
نقيض الأوسط

B) The side of the haqīqiyyah
C) The middle term
D) The contradictory of the middle

Key Takeaway
In a mixed haqīqiyyah-māni'at al-khuluw with universal affirmative premises, the conclusion's antecedent is always the side of the haqīqiyyah.

Negative Premise in Mixed Syllogism
Rules for negative premises in the fifth division: negative haqīqiyyah vs negative māni'at al-khuluw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a negative premise in a mixed haqīqiyyah-māni'at al-khuluw syllogism yields a conclusion and when it does not.

Core Idea
A negative haqīqiyyah yields no conclusion; a negative māni'at al-khuluw yields a negative conditional with the side of the haqīqiyyah as premise.

The author now considers cases where one premise is negative. If the negative is the haqīqiyyah, no conclusion follows because the syllogism can be true under both opposition and entailment. If the negative is the māni'at al-khuluw, a negative conditional conclusion is produced with the side of the haqīqiyyah as its antecedent. This lesson unpacks these two subcases with examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify which premise is negative
Check whether the haqīqiyyah or the māni'at al-khuluw is the negative premise. The negative could be universal or particular.
The logical behavior differs fundamentally based on which premise is negative.
2
Case 1: Negative haqīqiyyah
If the haqīqiyyah is negative, no conclusion follows. The text shows that the syllogism can be true with either opposition (ta'ānud) between the sides or entailment (talāzum). Examples with animal/human and human/rational illustrate this.
The lack of conclusion is due to the possibility of contradictory or entailed relationships between the disjuncts.
3
Case 2: Negative māni'at al-khuluw
If the māni'at al-khuluw is negative (universal or particular), a negative conditional conclusion is produced. The premise (antecedent) of this conclusion is the side of the haqīqiyyah, not the side of the māni'at al-khuluw.
The reasoning involves proving that if the side of the haqīqiyyah were true, the opposite of the māni'at al-khuluw's side would lead to a contradiction.
4
Construct the negative conclusion
Form the negative conditional: 'It may not be that if (side of haqīqiyyah) then (side of māni'at al-khuluw)' or equivalently 'Not always if (side of haqīqiyyah) then (side of māni'at al-khuluw)'.
The source expresses it as 'قد لا يكون إذا كان (طرف الحقيقية) فـ (طرف مانعة الخلو)'.

Source Text

advanced
5 min
Mixed Disjunctive Syllogism: Combining Māni'at al-Jam' and Māni'at al-Khuluw
Author explains the syllogism combining māni'at al-jam' and māni'at al-khuluw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure and conclusion of a syllogism that combines a māni'at al-jam' premise with a māni'at al-khuluw premise when both are universal affirmative.

Core Idea
Combining exclusive and inclusive disjunctions yields a universal affirmative conclusion from the exclusive side.

In the sixth division of mixed conditional syllogisms, the author examines the case where one premise is māni'at al-jam' (preventing both together) and the other is māni'at al-khuluw (preventing emptiness). When both premises are universal affirmative, the conclusion is a universal affirmative taken from the side of the māni'at al-jam' premise. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this inference, which relies on an entailment chain through a middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
One premise is māni'at al-jam' (exclusive, cannot both be true) and the other is māni'at al-khuluw (inclusive, at least one true). Both are universal affirmative, meaning they apply to all cases.
Universal affirmative here means the disjunctions are stated as universal laws, not particular cases.
2
Understand the entailment chain
The side of the māni'at al-jam' (say A) entails the contradictory of the middle term (not-B). Not-B in turn entails the side of the māni'at al-khuluw (say C). Thus A entails C.
The middle term is a logical intermediary that links the two disjunctions.
3
State the conclusion
Since A entails C, and both are universal statements, the conclusion is a universal affirmative: 'All A are C' (equivalently, the side of māni'at al-jam' implies the side of māni'at al-khuluw).
The conclusion 'premise being the side of the māni'at al-jam'' means the antecedent is taken from that premise.
4
Note the alternative
If the premises were not both universal affirmative, they would be haqīqiyyah (real, non-conditional) disjunctions, which would not produce the same entailment.
This highlights the importance of the universal affirmative quality.

If this were not the case, both premises would reduce to haqīqiyyah. The author then explains the nature of entailment between two things, but the text is cut off. Validating Universal Affirmative Mixed Disjunctive Syllogisms
Explanation of universal affirmative case in sixth division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the valid conclusion when a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam') is combined with a universal affirmative exhaustive disjunction (man'at al-khulu).

Core Idea
The contradictory of the middle term bridges exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions to yield a certain conditional.

In this sixth division of compound syllogisms, we mix two types of disjunctions: one that prevents conjunction (both cannot be true) and one that prevents void (both cannot be false). When both premises are universal affirmative, a powerful rule emerges: the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent comes from the exclusive disjunction. Let us walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the man'at al-jam' (exclusive) premise and the man'at al-khulu (exhaustive) premise. In the passage, the author assumes each is universal affirmative (dāʾiman mūjiba kulliyya).
Universal affirmative means the disjunction holds in all instances.
2
Determine the middle term
The middle term is the common disjunct shared by both premises. Find which term appears in both disjunctions. For example, if the premises are 'Either A or B' (exclusive) and 'Either B or C' (exhaustive), the middle term is B.
3
Form the contradictory of the middle term
Take the negation of the middle term. This contradictory will bridge the two premises. The author calls it 'naqīḍ al-awsaṭ'.
For middle term B, its contradictory is not-B.
4
Apply the entailment rules
From the exclusive disjunction, the contradictory of the middle term entails the other term of that disjunction (since they cannot both be false? Actually, from man'at al-jam', if one disjunct is false, the other must be true? Wait: man'at al-jam' only says they cannot both be true; it does not require one to be true. So the entailment is not straightforward. However, the author states: the contradictory of the middle term entails the term of man'at al-jam'? Let's re-read: 'لأن نقيض الأوسط يستلزم طرف مانعة الجمع' - the contradictory of the middle term entails the term of man'at al-jam'. Actually, from the exclusive disjunction, if one disjunct is false, the other is not necessarily true; but in the passage, the author seems to use a specific logical relation. We must follow the passage's reasoning: for universal affirmative premises, the contradictory of the middle term entails the term of man'at al-khulu? The translation says: 'because the contradictory of the middle term entails the term of man'at al-khulu'. Let's check: Arabic: 'لاستلزامه نقيض الأوسط المستلزم لطرف مانعة الخلو' - because it (the contradictory of the middle term) entails the term of man'at al-khulu. So the step: from the exhaustive disjunction, the contradictory of the middle term necessitates the other term because in an exhaustive disjunction, if one disjunct is false, the other must be true. So from not-B, we get C (the other term of man'at al-khulu). Then from the exclusive disjunction, we need to get the antecedent of the conclusion. The conclusion's antecedent is the term from man'at al-jam'. Actually, the conclusion is 'if A then C' where A is from man'at al-jam' and C is from man'at al-khulu? The passage: 'ينتج موجبة كلية مقدمها طرف مانعة الجمع' - yields a universal affirmative whose antecedent is the term of man'at al-jam'. So conclusion: if (term from man'at al-jam') then (term from man'at al-khulu). So we need to show that from the premises, 'if A then C' follows. The reasoning: assume A (from man'at al-jam'), then what? Since A and the middle term B are in exclusive disjunction, if A is true, then B is false (because they cannot both be true). So not-B is true. From not-B and the exhaustive disjunction, C must be true (since B and C cannot both be false). So from A, C follows. Thus the conditional is proven. The author uses the contradictory of the middle term as the linchpin. So step: assume the antecedent of the conclusion, then derive the consequent via the middle term's contradictory.
This step requires careful logical manipulation.
5
Write the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal conditional: 'Every time (or whenever) the term from man'at al-jam' is true, the term from man'at al-khulu is true.' Formally: Always if (A) then (C).
The conclusion is universal affirmative because the premises are universal affirmative.

And the sixth division is that one of its two premises is man'at al-jam' and the other is man'at al-khulu; it yields a universal affirmative whose antecedent is the term of man'at al-jam' if each of them is universal affirmative, because the contradictory of the middle term entails the term of man'at al-khulu, otherwise each of them would be real; for the implication between two things is only when there is impossibility of being devoid of the contradictory of the entailed and the very entailer, and impossibility of conjunction between the very entailer and the contradictory of the entailed. And if one of them is particular, if it is man'at al-jam' it yields a particular conditional between the two terms essentially, and its antecedent is whichever of them is, because the contradictory of the middle term entails the term of man'at al-jam' partially, and entails the term of man'at al-khulu universally, and they are produced from the third. And if the particular is man'at al-khulu it yields a particular conditional from the contradictories of the two terms, because the middle term entails the contradictory of the term of man'at al-khulu partially, and the contradictory of the term of man'at al-jam' universally. And the third also produces if one of them is negative. If the negative is particular it does not yield. As for if it is man'at al-khulu, because of the truth of the syllogism sometimes with opposition and sometimes with implication; with opposition, because the more specific of the contradictory of a thing can be not true with its contradictory, as in our saying always either this thing is a human or a horse, and it may not be either a horse or not a human. And with implication, because the more specific of the contradictory of a thing can be not true with the equal implication, as in our saying it may not be either a horse or rational. And as for if it is man'at al-jam', sometimes with opposition because the more general of the contradictory of a thing can be true with the contradictory of that thing, as in our saying always either not not-a-human or an animal as man'at al-khulu, and it may not be either an animal or a human as man'at al-jam'. And sometimes with implication because the more general of the contradictory of a thing can be true with the equal implication of that thing, as in our saying it may not be either an animal or not rational as man'at al-jam' with implication between human and rational. And as for if the negative is universal, if it is man'at al-khulu, whether universal or particular, it yields a particular negative whose antecedent is the term of man'at al-jam', as in our saying always or sometimes either (ab) or (cd) as man'at al-jam', and never either (cd) or (hz), for it yields it may be that if (ab) then (hz), otherwise its contradictory would be true and that...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage expands on the sixth division of compound conditional syllogisms, where one premise is a mutually exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam') and the other is an exhaustive disjunction (man'at al-khulu). The author explains the conditions for valid conclusions based on the quantity and quality of the premises. When both premises are universal affirmative, the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the term from man'at al-jam'. The reasoning: the contradictory of the middle term (the common disjunct) necessarily implies the term from man'at al-khulu, and if this were not the case, the premises would reduce to real (haqiqiyya) disjunctions. For particular premises, if the particular premise is man'at al-jam', the conclusion is a particular conditional with its antecedent being either term, because the contradictory of the middle term partially implies the man'at al-jam' term and universally implies the man'at al-khulu term. If the particular premise is man'at al-khulu, the conclusion is a particular conditional from the contradictories of the two terms, because the middle term partially implies the contradictory of the man'at al-khulu term and universally implies the contradictory of the man'at al-jam' term. The third figure also produces results when one premise is negative, but if the negative is particular, no conclusion follows. The author then examines cases of negative premises, showing that sometimes they yield conclusions and sometimes not, depending on whether contradiction (ta'andud) or implication (talazum) holds between the terms. For example, when the negative premise is man'at al-khulu, the syllogism can be true with opposition (e.g., 'either human or horse' and 'not either horse or not human') or with implication (e.g., 'not either horse or rational'). Similarly for man'at al-jam' with examples. Finally, if the negative premise is universal, and the other is man'at al-khulu (universal or particular), a particular negative conclusion follows with the antecedent from man'at al-jam'. The entire discussion uses variables (ab, cd, hz) and examples from natural kinds (human, animal, horse, rational) to illustrate the logical relations.

مانعة الخلو
نقيض الأوسط

Handling Particular and Negative Premises in Mixed Disjunctive Syllogisms
Rules for particular and negative premises in sixth division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a mixed disjunctive syllogism with one particular or negative premise yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Particular and negative premises restrict the validity, and only specific combinations produce a conclusion.

The sixth division of compound syllogisms also covers cases where one premise is particular or negative. The author explains that if the particular premise is man'at al-jam', a particular conditional follows; if it is man'at al-khulu, the conclusion involves contradictories. Negative premises require careful analysis of opposition and implication. Let us explore these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify which premise is particular
If one premise is particular (qad yakūn) and the other universal, note which type is particular: man'at al-jam' or man'at al-khulu.
Particular means 'sometimes' as opposed to 'always'.
2
Apply the rule for particular man'at al-jam'
If the particular premise is man'at al-jam', the conclusion is a particular conditional between the two terms, with the antecedent being either term. The reason: the contradictory of the middle term partially entails the term of man'at al-jam' and universally entails the term of man'at al-khulu.
Partial entailment means the implication holds only in some cases.
3
Apply the rule for particular man'at al-khulu
If the particular premise is man'at al-khulu, the conclusion is a particular conditional from the contradictories of the two terms. The middle term partially implies the contradictory of the man'at al-khulu term and universally implies the contradictory of the man'at al-jam' term.
4
Handle negative premises
If one premise is negative (sāliba), check its quantity. If the negative is particular, no conclusion follows. The author explains with examples of opposition (ta'ānd) and implication (talāzum). If the negative is universal, and the other premise is man'at al-khulu (universal or particular), a particular negative conclusion follows with the antecedent from man'at al-jam'.
The examples use 'always' and 'sometimes' with terms like human, horse, animal, rational to illustrate the logical relations.

And the sixth division is that one of its two premises is man'at al-jam' and the other is man'at al-khulu; it yields a universal affirmative whose antecedent is the term of man'at al-jam' if each of them is universal affirmative, because the contradictory of the middle term entails the term of man'at al-khulu, otherwise each of them would be real; for the implication between two things is only when there is impossibility of being devoid of the contradictory of the entailed and the very entailer, and impossibility of conjunction between the very entailer and the contradictory of the entailed. And if one of them is particular, if it is man'at al-jam' it yields a particular conditional between the two terms essentially, and its antecedent is whichever of them is, because the contradictory of the middle term entails the term of man'at al-jam' partially, and entails the term of man'at al-khulu universally, and they are produced from the third. And if the particular is man'at al-khulu it yields a particular conditional from the contradictories of the two terms, because the middle term entails the contradictory of the term of man'at al-khulu partially, and the contradictory of the term of man'at al-jam' universally. And the third also produces if one of them is negative. If the negative is particular it does not yield. As for if it is man'at al-khulu, because of the truth of the syllogism sometimes with opposition and sometimes with implication; with opposition, because the more specific of the contradictory of a thing can be not true with its contradictory, as in our saying always either this thing is a human or a horse, and it may not be either a horse or not a human. And with implication, because the more specific of the contradictory of a thing can be not true with the equal implication, as in our saying it may not be either a horse or rational. And as for if it is man'at al-jam', sometimes with opposition because the more general of the contradictory of a thing can be true with the contradictory of that thing, as in our saying always either not not-a-human or an animal as man'at al-khulu, and it may not be either an animal or a human as man'at al-jam'. And sometimes with implication because the more general of the contradictory of a thing can be true with the equal implication of that thing, as in our saying it may not be either an animal or not rational as man'at al-jam' with implication between human and rational. And as for if the negative is universal, if it is man'at al-khulu, whether universal or particular, it yields a particular negative whose antecedent is the term of man'at al-jam', as in our saying always or sometimes either (ab) or (cd) as man'at al-jam', and never either (cd) or (hz), for it yields it may be that if (ab) then (hz), otherwise its contradictory would be true and that...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage expands on the sixth division of compound conditional syllogisms, where one premise is a mutually exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam') and the other is an exhaustive disjunction (man'at al-khulu). The author explains the conditions for valid conclusions based on the quantity and quality of the premises. When both premises are universal affirmative, the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the term from man'at al-jam'. The reasoning: the contradictory of the middle term (the common disjunct) necessarily implies the term from man'at al-khulu, and if this were not the case, the premises would reduce to real (haqiqiyya) disjunctions. For particular premises, if the particular premise is man'at al-jam', the conclusion is a particular conditional with its antecedent being either term, because the contradictory of the middle term partially implies the man'at al-jam' term and universally implies the man'at al-khulu term. If the particular premise is man'at al-khulu, the conclusion is a particular conditional from the contradictories of the two terms, because the middle term partially implies the contradictory of the man'at al-khulu term and universally implies the contradictory of the man'at al-jam' term. The third figure also produces results when one premise is negative, but if the negative is particular, no conclusion follows. The author then examines cases of negative premises, showing that sometimes they yield conclusions and sometimes not, depending on whether contradiction (ta'andud) or implication (talazum) holds between the terms. For example, when the negative premise is man'at al-khulu, the syllogism can be true with opposition (e.g., 'either human or horse' and 'not either horse or not human') or with implication (e.g., 'not either horse or rational'). Similarly for man'at al-jam' with examples. Finally, if the negative premise is universal, and the other is man'at al-khulu (universal or particular), a particular negative conclusion follows with the antecedent from man'at al-jam'. The entire discussion uses variables (ab, cd, hz) and examples from natural kinds (human, animal, horse, rational) to illustrate the logical relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following premises: 'Sometimes (particular) either this transaction is valid or void' (man'at al-jam') and 'Always either this transaction is void or revocable' (man'at al-khulu). Derive the conclusion and check if it matches the rule for particular man'at al-jam'.
?

Module 4 · Lesson 400

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Rule for a Syllogism with One Particular Disjunctive Premise
Author explains the conclusion when one premise is a particular disjunctive proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct conclusion when one premise is a particular disjunctive proposition, distinguishing between exclusive and inclusive disjunction.

Core Idea
A particular disjunctive premise yields only a particular conditional conclusion.

When one premise is a particular disjunctive proposition, the syllogism produces a particular conditional conclusion. The type of disjunction—whether it forbids both being true (exclusive) or allows both (inclusive)—affects the terms used in the conclusion. This lesson unpacks the rule given in the text for both cases, using examples from Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the disjunctive premise and the other premise (often categorical). Note which premise is particular. In the text, the rule applies when one premise is a particular disjunctive.
The other premise may be universal or particular; the rule only cares that the disjunctive is particular.
2
Classify the disjunction type
Determine if the disjunctive premise is exclusive (mani'at al-jam') or inclusive (mani'at al-khuluw). Exclusive means only one disjunct can be true; inclusive means at least one is true.
Refer to the key terms for definitions.
3
Apply the rule for exclusive disjunction
If the particular disjunctive is exclusive, the conclusion is a particular conditional between the two end terms. The antecedent can be either term. This is because the contradictory of the middle term entails one term of the exclusive disjunction partially.
The 'end terms' are the terms in the disjunction and the subject of the categorical premise.
4
Apply the rule for inclusive disjunction
If the particular disjunctive is inclusive, the conclusion is a particular conditional from the contradictories of the two end terms. The middle term entails the contradictory of a term from the inclusive disjunction partially.
Here the conclusion connects the negations of the disjuncts.
5
Verify the figure
Both rules produce the conclusion from the third figure (al-thalith). Confirm that the middle term is the subject of the categorical premise and the predicate in the disjunctive, matching the third figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت إحديهما جزئية؛ فإن كانت هي مانعة الجمع ينتج متّصلة جزئية بين الطرفين بالذات، ومقدمها أي واحد كان منهما لأن نقيض الأوسط يستلزم طرف مانعة الجمع استلزاما جزئيا، ويستلزم طرفا مانعة الخلوّ استلزاما كليًّا وإنهما ينتجان من الثالث. وإن كانت الجزئية مانعة الخلو ينتج متّصلة جزئية من نقيضي الطرفين لما أن الأوسط مستلزم نقيض طرف مانعة الخلو استلزاما جزئيا، ونقيض طرف مانعة الجمع كليًّا. والإنتاج الثالث أيضًا
And if one of the two premises is particular: if it is an exclusive disjunction, it produces a particular conditional between the two terms essentially, and its antecedent is whichever of the two because the contradictory of the middle entails one term of the exclusive disjunction partially, and entails the two terms of the inclusive disjunction universally, and they both produce from the third. And if the particular is an inclusive disjunction, it produces a particular conditional from the contradictories of the two terms because the middle entails the contradictory of the term of the inclusive disjunction partially, and the contradictory of the term of the exclusive disjunction universally. And also the third production.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage explains the rule for producing a conclusion when one of two premises is a particular disjunctive proposition. The author first distinguishes between an exclusive disjunction (مانعة الجمع) and an inclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو). When the particular premise is exclusive, the conclusion is a particular conditional proposition between the two end terms essentially, and its antecedent can be either term. This is because the contradictory of the middle term (the common term) entails one term of the exclusive disjunction partially (since the particular excludes some possibilities), and entails both terms of the inclusive disjunction universally. Both of these entailments produce from the third figure. When the particular premise is inclusive, the conclusion is a particular conditional from the contradictories of the two end terms, because the middle entails the contradictory of the term of the inclusive disjunction partially and the contradictory of the term of the exclusive disjunction universally. The phrase 'and also the third production' hints at a third case, which is taken up in the next section.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
متصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a syllogism has a universal exclusive disjunction 'Every ruling is either obligatory or prohibited' and a particular premise 'Some actions are rulings', what conclusion follows according to the passage?
A) Universal conditional 'If an action is a ruling, it is either obligatory or prohibited'
B) Particular conditional 'If some action is a ruling, then it is either obligatory or prohibited'
C) Universal categorical 'Every action is either obligatory or prohibited'
D) Particular disjunctive 'Some actions are either obligatory or prohibited'

Key Takeaway
A syllogism with one particular disjunctive premise yields a particular conditional conclusion, with the antecedent determined by the type of disjunction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premises: 'Sometimes, a fast during Ramadan is either obligatory or recommended (inclusive disjunction). This day is a fast during Ramadan (particular categorical).' According to the passage, what type of conclusion follows? Write the conclusion in the form of a particular conditional.
?
Construct a valid syllogism using an exclusive disjunction premise that is particular, and a universal categorical premise, then state the conclusion as per the rule.
